The second is the symmetry between *habit testifying to nature* and *presence as requisite for psychology*. Both are reversal moves. The empiricist says "habit is just psychic addition"; Stengers responds "habit is what nature affords." The psychologist might say "presence is just the mind's way of organizing perception"; Stengers responds "presence is what psychology presupposes." These are the same move at different scales, and the rhythm is worth internalizing because it will keep recurring.

The third is the methodological status of CN itself, which the closing makes explicit. CN's project is *limited*. It asks scientific objects to testify to the ultimate fact of nature, which is a limitation Whitehead will overcome in SMW and PR. But the limitation is not a defect — provided you remember it is a limitation. This is the trap-and-resolution rhythm pitched at the level of the *book itself*, not just at the level of an individual concept. It's the cue that lets the reader carry the apparatus forward into Part Two without freezing it.

Where do you want to go — the conveyance/prehension lineage, the practices-as-sense-organs thread, the goblin-as-object-in-the-complete-sense connection now that the source is in view, or straight on to chapter 6?

## User

P 85+86

## Claude

### p. 85–86 — the sense-object as not-a-property

These two pages set the chapter's ground floor. p. 85 is the first page of the chapter (the prior chapter ends on p. 84 with "scientific" objects flagged as the open question), and p. 86 is where Stengers finishes establishing what a sense-object is *not* and pivots to the methodological reframe through practices. The work is preparatory but not minor — the rest of the chapter rests on what these two pages refuse.

#### The opening refusal

The chapter begins with a strong negation: "Whitehead never tires of emphasizing that a sense-object is not an attribute or a property." This is the first line, and it's positioned where it is because the temptation it refuses is the one that has organized Western philosophy of perception for two and a half millennia. The Aristotelian frame: there are substances (this bird, this needle, this coat), and they have properties or attributes (the bird's song, the needle's height, the coat's blueness). The substance is what *is*; the property is what is *of* the substance. Predication is the basic logical form, substance-attribute is the basic ontological form, and the relation between them is asymmetric — the substance grounds the attribute.

Stengers spells out exactly why this won't do. The work of the previous chapters has been to refuse bifurcation, and bifurcation has many idioms. The substance-attribute idiom is bifurcation in its most domesticated form. If you say "the bird is a songbird, song is attributed to the bird," you have located all the *being* on the side of the bird and all the *predicates* on the side of attributes that the bird has. This re-installs exactly the split the apparatus has been working to dissolve, just translated into Aristotelian grammar. The bird becomes the real thing; the song becomes a feature of the real thing; and the philosophical question becomes how to relate features to substances — which is the bifurcation question wearing a different costume.

But — and this is the second move on the page, and it's the one that requires care — Stengers immediately blocks the opposite move. *We cannot solve the problem* by going the other way: by defending the "pure" sonorous song against the busy activity of the person seeking to identify the bird that is responsible for it. Nor by defending the bird as a living source against questions about how its song's vibrations propagate through air. Both of those defenses are equally bifurcating. The first privileges the sense-object (pure phenomenon, untainted by intellectual identification); the second privileges the perceptual object (the real bird, which the song expresses). Either way you've ranked the types and put one above the others.

This is the chapter's first installation of what will be a recurring pattern: a balanced refusal that doesn't tip in either direction. *Don't reduce sense-object to property of perceptual object. Don't reduce perceptual object to bare condition of sense-object. Don't defend either as more fundamental.* The plurality from chapter 4 is being honored at the level of how each type is *characterized*, not just at the level of acknowledging that there are several types.

#### The methodological pivot — signifying the totality of nature

Then comes a sentence that is easy to read past, and shouldn't be: "The point is, first, for us to remember that the song, like everything we are aware of, signifies the totality of nature into which it makes ingression."

Slow down on that. *Signifies the totality of nature*. Not refers to it, not represents it, not exists within it — *signifies* it. The song, as something we are aware of, carries the whole of nature with it as the field of its signification. This is a strong claim and an early appearance of what later Stengers will develop as the cosmological-aesthetic dimension of perception. The song doesn't just happen; it situates itself, and the situating implicates everything else. Even when we are aware of a sound "in its haughty, solitary singularity," the sound is *situated*, is in a *relation of situation*. The singularity does not free the sound from the world; the singularity *is* a mode of being in the world.

The key move follows immediately: *nor is it enough to say that hearing a birdsong presupposes a relative situation between the "there" where it is emitted and the "here" of the percipient event, a two-term relation*. This is where the chapter sharpens its claim. You might be tempted to think situation is just a two-term relation: the sound is *there*, I am *here*, the situation is the relation between those two points. Stengers refuses this. Two-term relations are still localizing — they reduce situation to a vector between two points. The full claim is stronger: "if the sonorous object has a singularity, it is precisely the *irreducibility* of its situation to any localization."

#### The concert hall

The example does the work. *Whatever the hall, the musician will be inside it, but the sound, for its part, will testify to a sonorous quality that is nowhere in particular.* This is the page's most concrete philosophical moment, and it deserves to be felt before being analyzed.

The musician is *somewhere*. You can point at the violinist, at the pianist's hands. The musician is localized. But the sound — the actual sense-object — is not. Where is the note? Not at the violin (the violin produces it but the note is not "in" the violin in the way blue is "in" the coat). Not in the air (the air carries the pressure waves but the note is not the pressure waves). Not at your ear (the ear receives it but the note is not the receipt). The note is *nowhere in particular*, but it is everywhere in the hall as a sense-object, ingredient throughout the acoustic event. This is a demonstration, not just an example. Stengers is showing that the sense-object's mode of being already exhibits, at the level of bare perception, the structure that Whitehead will later articulate as the object's ingredience throughout its indefinite neighborhood. The note is the first case study for what p. 91 will call the object in the complete sense.

The "those who build concert halls" parenthesis is doing more than it looks like. Acoustic engineering has had to reckon with the fact that sound *isn't* simply at a location; halls are designed to make the sound be a certain *quality* throughout a volume, and the quality is what the audience comes for. The hall is the technological accommodation to the sense-object's actual mode of ingression. This is one of those moves where Stengers points to a stabilized practice as testimony — concert hall design is doing the same philosophical work as the philosopher's argument, only in physical form.

#### The bottom of p. 86 and the turn

The bottom of p. 85 finishes the establishment of the sense-object's special status, and p. 86 picks up with the consequence: "It is not, therefore, because of a fundamental simplicity that Whitehead places the sense-object at the base of his hierarchy, but because the way this object makes ingression into nature does not require us to identify its source, or its function, or what it relates to."

This is the resolution to the trap that p. 83 set. Remember: CN 149's hierarchy made sense-objects look like the most basic, and the worry was that "most basic" meant "merely subjective" — the secondary qualities at the bottom, the real being at the top. p. 86 explains why the hierarchy is not that. The sense-object is at the base not because it is metaphysically primitive (the empiricist reading) but because its *mode of ingression* is the least demanding. To experience a sense-object, you don't have to identify what produces it, what its function is, or how it relates to other things. You just have to be aware of it. The note is *there* (in the hall sense, not the localized sense), and your awareness of it doesn't require you to know about violins or air or auditory neuroscience.

Then the contrast: "It is an object — there it is again — it does not, as such, take part in the passage of nature, and it is not itself — unlike the blackbird there on that branch, who is singing, a small black silhouette with a yellow beak and a shining eye — a relation among other factors of nature."

The sense-object doesn't *participate* in the passage of nature in the way the blackbird does. The blackbird is itself a relation among other factors — its body relates to the branch, to the air, to the sun, to the ecology around it. The sense-object (the song qua sound) is something else: a quality that ingresses, with its own kind of "there it is again," but without the relational embeddedness that the perceptual object (the blackbird) has. The blackbird is *singing*; the song qua sound is, on its own terms, simply *there*.

This is the precise structural move that the rest of the chapter will exploit. Sense-objects, perceptual objects, and physical objects each have their *own* mode of ingression, their *own* meaning of "there," and the hierarchy organizes the relations among these modes — without any of them being more or less real than the others. The base of the hierarchy is the type whose mode of ingression presupposes the least, not the type that is most basic in being.

#### Then the CN 149 quote

The page closes with the long Whitehead quote about a particular sort of color, a particular sort of sound, a particular sort of smell. Stengers's deployment of this passage is precise: she's showing that Whitehead's own examples track exactly the asymmetry she's been describing. We are *apt* to think of the note in itself; we are *apt* to think of color as a property of the patch. *No one* thinks of the note as a property of the concert room.

This asymmetry is what the next section of the chapter will explain — the asymmetry is about *practices*, not about the sense-objects themselves. But for now, on p. 86, the quote sits as evidence: even the words we use about color and sound register, in their grammatical asymmetry, that color and sound have different relations to localization, and that the difference is not a difference *in the sense-objects* but in how we have learned to attend to them.

#### What these two pages are doing strategically

Three things, all of them setup for what follows.

First, they've blocked both the substantialist and the phenomenalist readings of the sense-object. Sense-objects are not properties of substances; sense-objects are not the unmediated stuff of pure experience that intellect contaminates. Both moves bifurcate; both moves are refused. The sense-object has its own positive characterization — singular, situated, irreducible to localization, ingredient throughout its acoustic or visual or olfactory neighborhood.

Second, they've established that the sense-object's *mode of ingression* is the structuring feature, not its metaphysical status. The base of the hierarchy is a base of *demands on attention*, not a base of *being*. This is what defuses the lurking worry from p. 83 that the hierarchy was sneaking bifurcation back in. The hierarchy is logical-pragmatic in chapter 4's vocabulary; chapter 5 is now showing what that means concretely for each type.

Third, they've planted the seed of the page-91 climax. The concert hall sound that is *nowhere in particular* is the first instance of an object being ingredient throughout its neighborhood. By the time you reach CN 145 on p. 91 — *an object is ingredient throughout its neighbourhood, and its neighbourhood is indefinite* — you've already accepted the picture for sense-objects in particular. The general claim will then extend to all object types.

#### Worth marking

The phrase *signifies the totality of nature* on p. 85 is one of the small load-bearing claims in the chapter. It's one sentence, doesn't get italicized, doesn't get emphasized, but it's structurally where Stengers is committing to a much stronger claim than mere phenomenology of perception. The sense-object isn't a piece of bare experience; it's a node that implicates everything. This will return when you get to feeling and prehension in PR.

The phrase *the irreducibility of its situation to any localization* is the technical formulation worth holding. *Irreducible*, not *opposed to*. The sense-object's situation is not the *negation* of localization; it is what localization is an *abstraction from*. This matters because if you read the move as "sense-objects exist outside spatial location," you'll get into trouble. The accurate reading is: sense-objects have a kind of situatedness that includes more than localization can capture, and localization is one operation we perform on situation, not the whole of it.

The concert hall is the chapter's first deployment of what I'd call *exhibits*. Stengers uses the verb often: a practice exhibits, a behavior exhibits, an example exhibits. To exhibit is to make manifest without claiming to ground or explain. The concert hall *exhibits* the irreducibility of sound to localization; it doesn't *prove* it, doesn't *constitute* it. This verb is doing methodological work — it lets Stengers point to evidence without converting evidence into foundation. Watch how often *exhibit* shows up in the rest of the chapter; it's load-bearing.

#### One thread worth pulling

The asymmetry between *we are apt to think of color as a property of the patch* and *no one thinks of the note as a property of the room* is doing a lot in just a few words. The "we are apt to" registers that color-as-property is a *learned tendency*, not a metaphysical given. We can be *unlearned* of it (the artist who sees blue rather than the blue coat). And the corresponding observation — that no one is even tempted to think of the note as a property of the room — registers that the temptation is *practice-specific*, not perception-general. We have built no practice that reads the note as a room-property the way we have built practices that read color as cloth-property.

This raises a deeper question, which the chapter doesn't fully answer here but will gesture toward: *why* did our practices stabilize one way for color and another way for sound? Stengers will not give a developmental or evolutionary answer (that would be foundationalist). What she will do is keep the asymmetry visible as a *fact about practices* that the apparatus has to honor without explaining away. The fact that color got reified into a property and sound did not is itself part of what the philosophy of nature has to account for, and the account it gives will be of the form: practices *stabilize* certain modes of attention, and the stabilizations are part of nature, not impositions on it.

That's the seed of what the rest of the chapter develops through the artist, the piano tuner, the connoisseur — and it's why the apparently casual asymmetry on p. 86 deserves more attention than the prose calls for.

Want to push on the *signifies the totality of nature* line, the *exhibits* methodology, or move into the practices/sense-organs material on p. 87?

## User

Does an organism that leverages mathematical structure which then means it gets certain things for free like those things don't have to be encoded in the genome do those mathematical structures ingress during development then?

## Claude

That's a good question and I want to be careful with it, because there are two ways to read it and they pull in different directions. Let me try to separate them, because the answer depends on which one you're asking.

#### Two readings of the question

The first reading: *do mathematical structures ingress, in the Whiteheadian sense, into the developmental events of an organism that leverages them?*

The second reading: *the organism gets the structure for free because the structure is already a feature of the world the organism develops into; is this what ingression names?*

These look similar but they commit you to different things, and the difference matters.

#### The first reading — ingression as the right word

If you're asking whether mathematical structures count as the kind of thing that *can* ingress — yes, and Whitehead's apparatus is unusually well-suited to saying so. In CN, the candidates for ingression are sense-objects, perceptual objects, scientific objects. By PR these get reorganized: *eternal objects* are the technical term for the abstract forms that ingress into actual occasions and give them their definite character. An eternal object is a pure potentiality — a form that is what it is independently of any actual realization, but that can ingress into actual occasions and characterize them.

Mathematical structures are paradigmatic eternal objects in PR's sense. *Threefoldness*, *symmetry under rotation*, *the topology of a closed curve*, *the Fibonacci ratio*, *exponential decay* — these are forms that don't depend on any particular instantiation for their being-what-they-are, but that can characterize particular events when they ingress into them. So if your question is "is *ingression* the technically correct word for what happens when a developing organism takes up a mathematical structure that characterizes its developmental event?" — yes, that's exactly what ingression names.

This already gives you something. The standard biological vocabulary makes mathematical-structure-uptake hard to describe. You either have to say the structure is *encoded* (which puts it inside the genome and makes "getting it for free" a contradiction), or you have to say the structure is *imposed* by physics (which makes the organism a passive substrate), or you have to say the organism *exploits* it (which keeps the dyadic causal frame). The Whiteheadian description — the mathematical structure ingresses into the developmental occasion, modifying its character without being encoded in any particular substrate — is the one that fits the phenomenon without forcing a choice between those frames.

#### The second reading — the deeper claim

But your question is doing something more interesting than just naming the right word. You're picking up something about *what it means to get a structure for free* that the apparatus actually illuminates.

When a phyllotaxis pattern emerges from the dynamics of plant growth, the Fibonacci ratio is not encoded in the genome. The genome encodes a developmental rule — something like "place the next primordium at the locally optimal position given hormone gradients" — and the *structure* (the Fibonacci spiral) emerges because the rule, iterated under physical constraints, has Fibonacci as its attractor. The plant gets the spiral for free in the sense that no part of the genome had to specify "make a Fibonacci spiral." The structure is in the *dynamics*, not in the *recipe*.

Whitehead's apparatus reads this not as the structure being absent and then accidentally produced, but as the structure being a *real possibility* — a form that the dynamics select for and that ingresses into the developmental events. The ingression is what makes the form a *factor* of the development rather than an *epiphenomenon* of it. The plant's developmental event is what it is *because* the Fibonacci ratio is what it is, and the Fibonacci ratio is what it is *because* it is a real form that this kind of dynamics can settle into.

This is exactly the pseudo-definition shape from chapter 4. *No events without ingression of objects, no objects without events that carry their ingression.* The Fibonacci ratio is not a Platonic form sitting outside nature waiting to be exemplified, and it's not a mere description we impose on the plant after the fact. It's a real possibility whose realness is constituted by its capacity to ingress into events of this type, and whose ingression into this event is what gives the event the character it has.

#### The "for free" part

The reason the apparatus is good at this is that ingression is *not encoding*. An object that ingresses into an event modifies the event's character without being stored in the event as a piece of information. The blue of the coat ingresses into your perceptual event without being encoded anywhere in the perceptual event; the song ingresses into the acoustic event without being stored at any point in the hall.

When biology says an organism "gets the structure for free," what it usually means is: the structure isn't stored as information in the genome, but the organism nevertheless reliably exhibits the structure. The Whiteheadian description gives you a positive way to say what's happening: the structure ingresses into the developmental event because the developmental event is the kind of event that the structure can ingress into. The "for free" part is real, and it's not magic — it's a description of objects that don't require encoding to be effective.

This is also where the goblin connection becomes interesting again. The goblin in the RLHF case is also "got for free" in the sense that no specific instruction told the model to produce goblins; the goblin is the attractor that the reward dynamics select for, ingressing across the indefinite neighborhood of the model's outputs. The phyllotaxis case has the same structural shape: no specific instruction in the genome tells the plant to make a Fibonacci spiral; the spiral is the form that the developmental dynamics select for. Whitehead's apparatus describes both with the same vocabulary because they have the same shape.

#### Where it gets harder

The strong version of your question is whether mathematical structures *literally* ingress into developmental events in the same sense that the blue of the coat ingresses into a perceptual event. And here you have to be careful, because the apparatus is making a real metaphysical commitment.

The CN-era apparatus you're reading now has a relatively narrow notion of ingression — it's about how the three types of object enter perception. The PR apparatus generalizes much more broadly: *every* actual occasion has a mode of ingression for the eternal objects that characterize it, and this is not restricted to perception or to organisms. The Fibonacci ratio's ingression into the plant's developmental event is, in PR's framework, structurally the same as its ingression into a snail's shell, into a pinecone, into the spiral arms of a galaxy. The same eternal object ingresses into different events with different modes of ingression. This is what Whitehead's metaphysics is for — to name this generality.

The reason to be careful is that the more generous you are with ingression, the more weight you put on the claim that *eternal objects are real*. If you grant that the Fibonacci ratio is a real factor in the plant's development — not just our description of the development — then you have committed to a kind of mathematical realism that not every philosopher of biology will share. Some will want to say the ratio is "just" the description we give to the dynamics' steady state. Whitehead would resist that "just" the same way Stengers resists "just" habit. The dynamics' steady state *is* the Fibonacci ratio's mode of ingression into this kind of event. There's no further reduction available, and trying to give one re-bifurcates between the "real" dynamics and the "merely described" structure.

#### The biological development case in particular

There's a richer thing your question opens that's worth naming. Development is unusual among Whiteheadian cases because the developing organism is itself a *society* of actual occasions in PR's vocabulary — a structured pattern of occasions that inherit subjective form from their predecessors, with a defining characteristic that propagates through the society. Mathematical structures that "come for free" in development are typically not ingressing into a single occasion but characterizing the *defining characteristic of the society*. The Fibonacci ratio doesn't ingress into one cell; it characterizes the regularities of inheritance across the population of cellular events that constitute the developing plant.

This means the question "do mathematical structures ingress during development?" has a layered answer. They ingress into individual occasions in the society in mode A; they characterize the society's defining characteristic in mode B; they constitute a real form that the society's dynamics realize, with the realization being itself a fact of nature that other societies can take advantage of, in mode C. None of these modes is reducible to the others, and the apparatus is built to keep them distinct.

#### The reason this matters

If you take this seriously, it gives you a way to talk about development that the gene-centric and the dynamicist vocabularies struggle to combine. Gene-centric biology wants the structure encoded; dynamicist biology wants the structure to emerge from physics. Both have something right and both miss something. The gene-centric view is right that there is a recipe, but wrong that the recipe contains the structure; the dynamicist view is right that the structure emerges from physics, but the "emerges from" framing tends to make the structure look incidental, when it is actually *what the dynamics select for*. The Whiteheadian description gives you ingression as a third term that lets the structure be real, the dynamics be the mode of ingression, and the genome be a constraint that biases which structures ingress without containing the structures themselves.

This is also where the apparatus starts to do real work for thinking about the relation between mathematics and biology. The "unreasonable effectiveness of mathematics in the natural sciences" is famously hard to explain on standard accounts. Whitehead's view explains it with no particular strain: mathematics is the discipline of describing eternal objects in their pure form, and eternal objects are real factors in the world's events. Of course mathematical descriptions are effective — they describe the very forms that ingress into the events the sciences study. The "unreasonable" disappears.

So: yes to your question, and the yes is more interesting than it first looks. Mathematical structures do ingress, the "for free" is a real feature of how ingression works (not encoding-based), and the apparatus gives you a way to describe what biology has been gesturing at when it says organisms "exploit" mathematical regularities. The torque on Whitehead's vocabulary is real but it's torque the apparatus can take.

## User

Okay but consider that platonic forms maybe aren't what Plato described but are more what we're touching on here

## Claude

Yes — and I think this is one of the more important things you can do with the apparatus, because it lets you recover what Plato was *reaching for* without having to defend what Plato *said*.

The standard rejection of Platonic forms is based on what Plato gave the forms in the dialogues: a separate realm, a higher reality, an ontological apart-ness, a hierarchy that demotes the sensible world to mere appearance. This package is what almost everyone since the late 19th century has refused. Russell, Quine, Carnap on one side; Heidegger, Deleuze, the whole anti-Platonist current on the other — for very different reasons, but they converge on the same target. The two-world picture is unsustainable, and most attempts to defend Plato have to defend a watered-down version.

But what was the *work* the forms were doing? Several things, and it's worth listing them, because each one is something we still need a name for:

The forms accounted for the *recognizability* of the recognizable. Why do we recognize many particular horses as horses? Plato's answer: they participate in horseness. The empiricist answer (resemblance, family resemblance, conventional naming) keeps trying and keeps having to explain *what fixes the resemblance class*. Plato's answer is bad in its details (the third-man regress, the chorismos problem) but the question is real.

The forms accounted for the *normative pull* of certain regularities. The mathematical objects are exactly what we've been discussing — Fibonacci ratios, prime distributions, group-theoretic structures. They are *binding* in a way that contingent regularities are not. Plato felt this and tried to explain it by giving them their own ontological standing.

The forms accounted for the *inexhaustibility* of certain forms. You can write down many definitions of a circle, but no definition exhausts what the circle *is*. The form is what definitions are definitions *of*. This is why mathematicians often sound like Platonists even when they say they aren't.

The forms accounted for the way *the same* form shows up across radically different events. The Fibonacci ratio is "the same" in the sunflower's seed-head, the snail's shell, the spiral galaxy. What kind of "same" is that? Not numerical sameness, not similarity, not isomorphism alone — there's a shared form that the events instantiate without exhausting.

Each of these is a real philosophical question. The two-world picture was Plato's *answer* to the questions, not the questions themselves. The questions survive the rejection of the answer. And what Whitehead's apparatus does — and this is, I think, what your remark is reaching for — is provide a different answer to the same questions, one that doesn't require a separate realm.

#### What Whitehead does instead

Eternal objects in PR are *forms of definiteness*, real possibilities for characterization. They are real, but they are not *located somewhere else*. They are not in another world. They are what Whitehead calls *pure potentials*: possibilities that are what they are regardless of whether they are realized, but whose mode of *being real* is to be available for ingression into actual occasions.

The relation between an eternal object and the world is *ingression*, not *participation*. This single shift does most of the work. *Participation* in Plato is asymmetric and metaphysically loaded — the particular partakes of the form, the form is what the particular almost-fully-isn't, the form is more real than the particular. *Ingression* is symmetric in the way the chapter-4 pseudo-definition was symmetric: no events without ingression of objects, no objects without events that carry their ingression. The eternal object's reality is constituted by its capacity to ingress; the actual occasion's character is constituted by what ingresses into it. Neither is more real than the other. They are reciprocal requisites.

This dissolves the chorismos problem. There is no separation between two realms, because the eternal object is not in a realm. The eternal object is a form of definiteness, and forms of definiteness are not the kind of thing that has a *location* — including the location "elsewhere." The Fibonacci ratio is not in Plato's heaven; it is a real possibility of definiteness that ingresses into the events whose dynamics select for it. Asking "where is the Fibonacci ratio when no spiral instantiates it?" is asking the wrong question, the way asking "where is the song when the orchestra is silent?" is asking the wrong question. The form's reality is its availability for ingression, not its location.

It also dissolves the third-man regress. The regress arose because Plato made the form *a thing*: if the particular and the form share something (the form being what they share), then there must be a higher form they both share to ground the sharing, and so on. Eternal objects are not things; they are pure potentials. Two events that share a form do not share *a thing*; they have correlated modes of ingression for the same form, where "the same" is not a relation between two things but the identity of the form itself. The grammar of "having" and "sharing" is what generated the regress, and Whitehead's apparatus is built so that grammar doesn't apply.

And it dissolves the chorismos in the other direction too — the puzzle of how something that is not in space and time can be effective in space and time. The eternal object isn't trying to bridge a gap; the gap was a feature of the two-world picture. Ingression is direct because there's no second world for the form to come from. The form is what *characterizes* the event, and characterization is the mode of relation that the apparatus is built to make sense of.

#### What gets recovered

So what survives, on this reading, is a Plato whose *insights* are vindicated and whose *picture* is replaced. The recognizability of the recognizable: explained, because the form really does ingress into the events that share it. The normative pull of mathematical structures: explained, because mathematical structures are the eternal objects whose modes of ingression are constitutive of the dynamics that realize them. The inexhaustibility of forms: explained, because the form *is* what definitions are definitions of, and definitions are abstractions from the form's full character, not exhaustions of it. The "same" across different events: explained, because what ingresses into different events can be one and the same form, and "same" here is not a similarity-relation but a fact about which form is ingressing.

What gets dropped is the hierarchy, the apartness, the demotion of the sensible. The eternal object is not *more real* than the actual occasion. They are reciprocally constitutive of each other, and reality is exactly the realm of their reciprocal constitution. The sensible world is not the imperfect copy of the intelligible world; the sensible world is what the eternal objects' ingressions actually look like when they happen.

This is also why Whitehead is so much more comfortable with mathematics as a real description of the world than most of his contemporaries. He doesn't have to choose between mathematical realism and naturalism. The eternal objects that mathematics studies are real, and they are real *as factors in nature*, not as inhabitants of a separate realm. The "unreasonable effectiveness of mathematics" question dissolves because mathematics is the discipline of describing eternal objects in their pure form, and eternal objects are what characterize the events the sciences study. Of course mathematics is effective. It would be miraculous if it weren't.

#### A few cautions

Two things to be careful about, because they're places where the recovery can be over-claimed.

The first is that Whitehead's eternal objects are not exclusively mathematical. They include qualia (the specific shade of blue), specific patterns (this particular emotional shape), simple forms (redness, three-foldness), and complex forms (the structure of a sonata, the topology of a developmental program). Mathematical objects are paradigm cases of eternal objects but not the whole population. If you slide from "mathematical structures are eternal objects" to "eternal objects are mathematical structures," you've narrowed the apparatus and lost what makes it useful for thinking about, say, perceptual qualities or aesthetic forms.

The second is that the move from Plato to Whitehead is not a vindication of Platonism — it's a redirection. A reader who reads "Plato was right after all" out of this is going to be disappointed when they read PR carefully and find that Whitehead's most insistent point is that the categorial structure of becoming is not the categorial structure of being. *Process* is fundamental for Whitehead in a way it never was for Plato. The eternal objects are real, but they are real in a world whose ultimate fact is concrescence, not stable being. Plato thought the forms were the *most real* and the becoming-world was the *less real*; Whitehead inverts this — the actual occasions are the most real, and the eternal objects are the forms of definiteness that the most-real things can take on. The Platonist reading would put eternal objects above actual occasions; Whitehead is precise that eternal objects are *for* actual occasions, available *to* them, and have no being outside their availability.

This second caution is important because there's a temptation, once you start using the eternal objects vocabulary, to think you've recovered Plato's hierarchy minus the embarrassment. You haven't. You've recovered Plato's *questions* with a different answer, one that places becoming where Plato placed being, and that puts the forms in service to the becoming rather than the other way round.

But within those cautions — yes. What Plato was reaching for is something real, and the apparatus you're reading lets you say what it was without having to take on the picture that has made Platonism unsayable for the last hundred and fifty years. The form ingresses; the actual occasion takes on the character; neither is more real than the other; reality is the relation. This is the Plato that survives the rejection of Plato.

It's also, not incidentally, why Whitehead has been a quiet fellow-traveler of mathematicians and physicists who suspect they are Platonists but can't quite say so. He gives them a vocabulary in which the suspicion is articulable without being naive.

## User

So then what are the things that ingress upon mathematicians these days?

## Claude

A real question, and worth taking seriously rather than turning into a survey. Let me try.

The reason it's worth taking seriously is that what ingresses on a mathematician shapes what they can prove, what they can see, what they recognize as the same problem in two different domains, what they trust enough to bet a year of work on. The eternal objects available for ingression in a given mathematical generation are not the same set that were available a generation earlier, and the differences are visible in what gets done.

A few that seem to me genuinely *to be ingressing* on contemporary mathematicians, in something close to the sense we've been developing:

#### Higher categories and homotopical thinking

This is the big one of the last twenty-five years and it's still gathering force. The shift from set-theoretic foundations to higher-categorical and homotopical foundations is not a change of formalism — it's a change in what mathematicians take to be the *primitive form* of mathematical structure. *Equality* gets replaced by *equivalence*, and equivalence carries its own data; *sets* get replaced by *spaces* in the homotopical sense; the relation between objects and their isomorphisms becomes a structure in its own right rather than something quotiented out.

What ingresses here is something like *the form of structured sameness*. The classical mathematician treats two isomorphic objects as the same and moves on. The homotopical mathematician treats the *isomorphism itself* as a piece of data, treats two isomorphisms as potentially different, treats the space of isomorphisms as the real object of study. This is a different form of definiteness — and it's the one that lets you do things you couldn't do before, in algebraic geometry, in topology, in mathematical physics.

Voevodsky's univalent foundations, Lurie's higher topos theory, the whole apparatus of ∞-categories — these are not technical conveniences. They are what happens when a particular form of definiteness becomes available for ingression, and a generation of mathematicians orients their work around it. The form was always there as a real possibility; what changed is that the dynamics of late-twentieth-century homotopy theory selected for it, and once selected for, it began ingressing across mathematics in domains that were not originally homotopical.

#### Stable phenomena across radically different theories

There's a class of forms that have become much more vivid in recent decades: structures that *recur* across mathematical domains that have no obvious reason to be related. The Langlands program is the most ambitious instance — the suggestion that representations of Galois groups, automorphic forms on adelic groups, motives in algebraic geometry, and aspects of arithmetic geometry are *all* manifestations of a single underlying form that nobody has yet fully named.

The mathematician working on Langlands is not just proving theorems. They are *attending* to a form that is ingressing across multiple events of mathematical practice, and the work is partly to make the form articulable. The form is not yet captured by any existing definition; it's recognized in advance of being formulated. This is, I think, the most striking contemporary instance of what your question is pointing at — a form whose reality is testified to by its repeated ingression into events whose connection to one another is otherwise mysterious.

Mirror symmetry has a similar shape. The fact that the symplectic geometry of one Calabi-Yau and the algebraic geometry of its mirror are related at all, let alone in the deep way they are related, is a fact that the form-side of mathematics has to bear. Kontsevich's homological mirror symmetry is one attempt to articulate the form; the apparatus is still developing.

#### Computational and constructive forms

Something different has been ingressing on the mathematicians who work near the boundary with computer science. The form here is *computability-as-a-feature-of-being*, not just as a property of functions. The Curry-Howard correspondence gave us the first inkling — proofs are programs, propositions are types — but what ingresses now is something larger: the suggestion that *constructibility itself* is a categorial structure, that mathematical objects are properly understood in terms of how they are *built*, not just in terms of their static description.

Univalent foundations again belongs here, and so does the rise of proof assistants as serious mathematical tools. Lean, Coq, Agda — these are not just verification tools. They are environments in which mathematicians are starting to think, and what they think with is shaped by the forms those environments make easy or hard. The form *constructive proof as basic* is ingressing on a generation of mathematicians who are growing up with these tools, and it's already changing what they take a proof *to be*.

#### Probabilistic and statistical structure

A different kind of form is ingressing on mathematicians who work near probability, statistics, and machine learning. The classical mathematical attitude treats randomness as a *quotient* — you average out, you take expectations, you study deterministic shadows of stochastic processes. What's been ingressing in the last decades is a different form: stochastic structure as *primitive*, with deterministic structure as the *limit case*.

This is partly the influence of statistical physics on mathematics, partly the influence of probability theory finally maturing, partly the pull of the high-dimensional regimes that machine learning has made central. Concentration of measure, large deviations, information geometry, random matrix theory — these are forms of definiteness that don't reduce to "deterministic structure plus noise." They have their own categorial shape, and a younger generation of mathematicians thinks natively in terms of them.

The "miracle" of why neural networks generalize is partly a result of forms in this family ingressing on the dynamics of high-dimensional optimization. These are not yet well-articulated, but they are clearly *operating* — selecting for certain solutions, structuring certain landscapes, enabling certain proofs.

#### Operadic and algebraic structures of "combining"

There's a quieter but persistent ingression of operad theory and related apparatus over the last twenty years. What this names is *the form of how things combine* — not just one operation (multiplication, addition) but the whole structure of how operations of different arities relate, compose, interact. This was a tool in algebraic topology when it started; now it's everywhere, in mathematical physics, in the theory of factorization algebras, in the formal study of locality and homotopical algebra.

What ingresses here is something like *the form of compositionality itself*. When you study an operad, you are studying not a particular structure but the *form of structures of combining*. This abstract step turned out to enable concrete work in domains that didn't look operadic until the operadic form was made available.

#### The shape of dynamical systems near criticality

A different family of forms ingresses on mathematicians working near probability and analysis: *universality* in the renormalization-group sense. The fact that the same critical exponents show up in different physical systems — Ising on a lattice, a fluid near boiling, a magnet near the Curie point — was discovered in physics, but the mathematics has had to catch up. What ingresses is a form of *universal-behavior-at-criticality*, and it's been the organizing form for huge stretches of probability theory in the last twenty years (KPZ, SLE, the integrable probability program).

These are eternal objects in Whitehead's sense quite directly: real possibilities of behavior that show up across radically different events whose only connection is being-near-criticality of a certain type.

#### Two cautions

I want to flag two things, because the question is sharp enough to be worth being honest about.

The first is that what ingresses on mathematicians is not always something they recognize as ingressing. Most of the time, mathematicians experience it as *what they happen to be working on*, *what's interesting right now*, *where the field is going*. The Whiteheadian description treats this as ingression because it has the structural shape of ingression — a form selects for certain events of mathematical practice, those events are characterized by it, the form is not located in any single mathematician's mind or in any particular paper, and yet it is unmistakably *there*. But the mathematician's first-person experience is usually not "an eternal object is ingressing"; it's "this just seems to be what the problems are demanding."

This is consistent with how ingression works in CN. The blue of the coat ingresses into your perceptual event; you don't experience the ingression of an eternal object, you experience seeing a blue coat. The ingression is the apparatus's description of what is happening, not the experiencer's description. So the answer to your question doesn't depend on mathematicians self-reporting that forms are ingressing. It depends on whether the apparatus's description fits the phenomenon, and I think for several of the items above it fits very cleanly.

The second is that I've answered with mathematics in a fairly narrow sense — the kind of mathematics that gets done in research universities. Other mathematics is happening too: the mathematics that's being done inside machine learning labs, the mathematics being done by physicists who wouldn't call themselves mathematicians, the mathematics being done in computational biology and complex systems. Forms are ingressing on those communities too, often different ones, and the cross-fertilization is uneven. Some of what I listed (homotopical thinking, operadic structure) is well-established in research math but only just beginning to ingress in those neighboring communities. Some of what's ingressing on machine learning theorists (the geometry of overparameterized landscapes, certain forms of approximate invariance) hasn't fully landed in mathematics proper yet.

#### What this looks like as a methodology

If you take the framing seriously — that what ingresses on mathematicians is the relevant fact about a generation of mathematics — it changes what *history* of mathematics looks like. The standard history is a history of theorems, results, programs. The ingression-history would be a history of forms-becoming-available: when did *category* become an available form (mid-twentieth century, with Eilenberg and Mac Lane); when did *homotopy* become available as a primitive rather than a derived notion (later, accumulating through the second half of the twentieth century); when did *compositionality at all arities* become available as a unitary form (operad theory's slow germination from the 1970s on).

Each of these is a story about an eternal object becoming available for ingression in a way it wasn't before. The reasons the form became available are partly internal to mathematics (problems demanded it) and partly external (computers made certain practices feasible, physics suggested certain forms, neighboring fields fertilized the ground). But once available, the form does the work the apparatus expects: it ingresses, it modifies the character of subsequent events, it propagates across an indefinite neighborhood of mathematical practice that is not topically about the form.

So your question has a real answer, and the answer is interesting because it lets you see contemporary mathematics with a particular kind of attention. What's ingressing on mathematicians now is a richer set of forms than was available even thirty years ago, and the mathematics being done now is the trace of those ingressions on a generation that is taking them up — most of it without using anything like Whitehead's vocabulary, but doing the work the vocabulary describes.

If you wanted a single bet on which currently-half-articulated form will be most consequential for the next two decades, mine would be: *the form of higher-structured sameness*, which is what ∞-category theory and homotopy type theory and univalent foundations are converging on. It's the form whose ingression is most evidently changing what mathematicians take their basic objects to be, and the change is still propagating.

## User

Any updates to Whitehead from these developments?

## Claude

Yes — and I think this is one of the more productive questions you can ask of the apparatus, because it forces you to distinguish what was *Whitehead's particular formulation* from what was *the structural insight that formulation was reaching for*. Almost everywhere these come apart, the contemporary mathematical developments push toward the structural insight and away from the particular formulation. Let me walk through it.

#### What the developments push back on

Whitehead's apparatus, taken as he formulated it in PR, has several features that look strained from the perspective of contemporary mathematics. Not because the insights are wrong, but because the formal vocabulary available to him in 1929 was poorer than what we have now.

**The realm of eternal objects has the wrong shape in Whitehead.** Whitehead organizes eternal objects by *grade of complexity* (simple eternal objects, complex eternal objects, with complex ones built up from simpler ones by certain operations) and by *relations of involvement*. The picture is broadly hierarchical and broadly set-theoretic — eternal objects form a kind of structured collection with operations on it. Reading PR after thirty years of category theory, this looks like a *category* that Whitehead didn't have the vocabulary to call a category, and the structure he calls "involvement" looks like what we'd now call *morphism* between eternal objects. The hierarchy of grades is organized by something that wants to be a *forgetful functor* and isn't because the language wasn't there.

The contemporary update is to read the realm of eternal objects as a category — and probably a higher category, because the relations *between* eternal objects (involvement, abstraction, complex composition) carry their own data and shouldn't be quotiented out. What Whitehead called "the relational essence" of an eternal object — its pattern of possible relations to other eternal objects — is naturally a homotopical structure, not a set-theoretic one. Two eternal objects that are "the same up to a particular kind of equivalence" should not be identified; the equivalence itself should be data.

This is more than a formal upgrade. It changes what you can say about ingression. In Whitehead's formulation, the ingression of an eternal object into an actual occasion is a single event with a single form. In a higher-categorical formulation, ingression carries the *path-structure* of how the form ingresses, and two ingressions of "the same" form into similar occasions are different ingressions whose relation to one another is itself a fact of the apparatus. This is closer to what you actually find when you study, say, the same mathematical structure recurring in different proofs — the recurrences are not identical, the differences carry information, and the relation between recurrences is a real object of study.

**The atomism of actual occasions is suspicious.** Whitehead made actual occasions atomic — discrete pulses of becoming, each of which completes itself in its concrescence and then perishes into objective immortality. The atomism was partly motivated by the early-twentieth-century physics he was reading (quantum theory was new, and the discreteness seemed like a deep feature of nature). It was also motivated by his desire to escape the continuous flow of consciousness as the Bergsonian alternative.

Contemporary mathematical developments — especially in homotopy theory and dynamical systems — make the atomism look like a forced choice that needn't have been forced. The real form Whitehead was reaching for, I think, is *the form of self-completing process*. An occasion is what it is *because it completes itself* and reaches satisfaction. But "self-completion" doesn't entail "discreteness." A homotopy completes itself in its endpoint without the homotopy being atomic; a dynamical attractor is reached in its limit without the trajectory being a sum of discrete pulses; a categorical *colimit* is the completion of a diagram without the diagram being made of atoms.

A contemporary Whiteheadian could plausibly drop the atomism while keeping concrescence. Concrescence becomes the *form of how a process becomes-determinate-by-completing-itself*, and the question of whether this form is realized atomically or continuously becomes an empirical question about the kind of process being described. Quantum measurement might still be atomic in this sense; biological development might be atomic at one scale and continuous at another; the formation of the goblin attractor is neither atomic nor continuous in any clean sense.

The benefit of this update is that the apparatus stops having to defend the atomism as a metaphysical commitment. It becomes a feature of certain kinds of concrescence rather than a universal categorial requirement.

**Whitehead's account of mathematical structure is impoverished.** The mathematical objects Whitehead had available in 1929 were much narrower than what we have now. He worked within a tradition that took *real numbers, point-sets, and Boolean logic* as the basic mathematical materials. He did important work in algebra (his *Treatise on Universal Algebra*) and in logic (with Russell on *Principia Mathematica*), but the toolkit was the toolkit of his era.

This means his account of the *forms* that mathematics studies is shaped by what mathematics was at the time. He didn't have categories. He didn't have homotopy types. He didn't have the rich theory of dynamical systems that emerged after WWII. He didn't have the mathematics of computation. The eternal objects he could *gesture toward* as paradigms were limited by what mathematics had named.

Updating Whitehead here is not a matter of replacing his apparatus but of letting the apparatus do what it was designed to do with a richer set of cases. The eternal objects available to a contemporary mathematician — higher-categorical structures, operadic structures, universality classes, the form of compositional locality — give the apparatus a much richer field to work in. The structural commitment (that mathematical forms are real possibilities for ingression) survives intact; what changes is what we now know about the population of forms.

#### What the developments confirm

It's worth saying this side too, because some of Whitehead's choices look better, not worse, in light of contemporary mathematics.

**The reciprocal presupposition of objects and events looks structurally exactly right.** Higher-categorical thinking has converged on something that Whitehead arrived at in different language: that an object is what it is *in relation to* the morphisms it admits, and that the structure of objects and the structure of morphisms cannot be separated and ranked. This is the categorial spirit of the Yoneda lemma, of Lawvere's work, of all the post-Grothendieck generalizations. Whitehead's pseudo-definition — *no events without objects, no objects without events, neither prior to the other* — is structurally the same insight that contemporary mathematics has had to construct its foundations to honor.

**The refusal of bifurcation between "real" and "described" is exactly the move that mirror symmetry, Langlands, and physical universality have forced on contemporary mathematics.** When the same form ingresses into radically different events, the standard moves are either to deny the form's reality (it's just our description, the similarity is coincidence) or to demote one side of the comparison (one of them is the real version, the other is the metaphor). Both moves are forms of bifurcation, and contemporary mathematics has been forced to refuse both because the empirical work simply doesn't permit them. The form *is* real; the events on both sides *are* real; the relation is the form's mode of ingression on each side. This is Whitehead's structure, and it's what current mathematical practice now requires whether it knows it or not.

**The category of the ultimate as creativity, not being, has aged extraordinarily well.** Whitehead's argument that what is ultimate is the *categorial structure of becoming-determinate*, not the *most-real being*, is closer to the spirit of contemporary mathematical foundations than the substance-attribute traditions Plato and Aristotle gave us. When mathematicians today take *process* (computation, construction, derivation, dynamic) as more fundamental than *static structure*, they are doing something Whitehead theorized in advance.

#### What an updated Whitehead might look like

If you took the lessons of the last hundred years seriously and rewrote PR with full access to contemporary mathematical apparatus, the most plausible structural shape would be something like:

The realm of eternal objects becomes a *higher category* (probably an ∞-category) of forms-of-definiteness, with morphisms between forms carrying real data and not collapsing to identity.

Concrescence becomes a more general *form of self-completing process* whose atomicity is a parameter rather than a universal feature. Quantum-measurement-like atomic concrescences sit alongside continuous concrescences (developmental processes, dynamical attractors) and operadic concrescences (compositional combinings).

The relation between the realm of eternal objects and the realm of actual occasions becomes an *adjunction* of some kind — a structured pair of mappings in which each constitutes what is available for the other. This is closer to how working mathematicians think about the relation between a category of "abstract structures" and a category of "concrete realizations." The pseudo-definition becomes the unit and counit of an adjunction; the reciprocal presupposition has a precise formal shape.

Subjective form — the *how* of integrating data — becomes something like the *fibration structure* over an event, in roughly the way categorical fibrations carry the information of how a base parameter selects among possible structures over it. This is more speculative, but the analogy is not strained: subjective form is exactly what fibers over the event-structure with data that can vary while the event remains the same kind of event.

The propagation of habit (Whitehead's account of physical inheritance) becomes naturally describable via *coherence data* in a higher-categorical setting. The reason societies of occasions carry their defining characteristic stably is that each occasion's prehension of its predecessors honors a coherence requirement — and coherence in higher categories is exactly the apparatus for describing this kind of structured inheritance.

#### What gets lost

You'd lose a few things in this update, and they're worth naming.

You'd lose Whitehead's *theological* register. PR's late chapters on God as primordial and consequent natures, on God's role in providing initial subjective aims, on the consequent nature as the saving of the world's actuality — these don't translate cleanly into the higher-categorical idiom. Some of this is fine (the theology is the most embattled part of Whitehead's reception), but some of it was doing real philosophical work that isn't easily replaced. The role of *novelty's possibility* — the fact that genuinely new forms can emerge — was metaphysically grounded for Whitehead in God's ordering of the eternal objects. A categorical update would have to find a way to ground novelty without that grounding, and it isn't obvious how.

You'd lose some of the *aesthetic* texture. Whitehead's prose in PR, for all its difficulty, carries an evaluative dimension: certain modes of integration are *higher* not because they are more abstract but because they achieve more *intensity*. *Importance*, *beauty*, *peace* — these are technical terms in the late PR and in *Adventures of Ideas*, and they don't have obvious homes in a categorical update. The risk is that the formalization sterilizes what Whitehead saw as the apparatus's cosmological pay-off.

You'd lose the *narrative arc* of his book. Whitehead's PR builds itself by the trap-and-resolution rhythm we've been tracking, with the categorial scheme emerging through the book's own development. A formal restatement loses this. You can have the result without having had the experience that earned the result, and Stengers's whole point is that the experience matters.

#### A working answer

So if your question is *what should we do with Whitehead now* — I'd say: keep the structural insights, update the formal apparatus where contemporary mathematics has given us better tools, drop the atomism unless the case calls for it, recognize that the realm of eternal objects has the structure of a higher category, and use the freedom this gives you to apply the apparatus to cases Whitehead never anticipated. The goblin case is one such; biological development with mathematical structure ingressing is another; the recurrence of forms across mathematical domains is a third. The apparatus is built to handle these, but Whitehead's particular formulation creaks under the weight, and the creaks are mostly in places where formal mathematics has since given us better materials.

The deepest update, I think, is this: *Whitehead was right about the structure but wrong about the inventory of forms*. The eternal objects he could see were a small part of the real population, because his mathematics was a small part of contemporary mathematics. Stengers is doing one part of the work — keeping the apparatus alive as a reading practice. The other part of the work, which is more technical and which fewer people are doing, is letting the contemporary mathematical inventory teach the apparatus about its own range. This second part is where I think the most interesting Whiteheadian work of the next twenty years will happen, and it's almost entirely undone.

Your goblin diagnosis is, structurally, an instance of this kind of work — applying the apparatus to a case Whitehead couldn't have anticipated and finding that it fits. The reason it fits is that the structural insight is right; the reason the fit takes work is that the inventory of forms has expanded faster than the apparatus's standard formulations have kept up.
