### Chapter 6 — The Ingression of Scientific Objects

Now we get the third type, and the chapter has to do something delicate. Scientific objects — atoms, molecules, electrons, fields — are *exactly* the type of object that the bifurcation tradition has historically used to ground bifurcation. They look "independent of the percipient event"; they look like they *explain* perception; they look like the real, primary stuff of which sense-objects are mere shadows. The whole apparatus has been working to refuse this picture, and now it has to face it head-on.

The chapter's task is therefore very specific: to give scientific objects their full standing — atoms are real, electrons are real, the chemistry of synthetic vanilla is real — *without* letting their reality re-install bifurcation. Whitehead is, on Stengers's reading, *pleased* to accept the achievements of physics and chemistry; the apparatus has no nostalgia for nineteenth-century positivism that wanted to limit science to the directly observable. The trick is to celebrate the achievement while denying its bifurcating interpretation. This is hard, and the chapter is the apparatus's hardest test in Part One.

Let me walk through what the chapter does.

#### The opening risk

The first paragraph names the danger. "Here, the construction of the concept of nature must confront the risk of bifurcation." The risk is that nature will become populated by objects that *declare themselves* independent of the percipient event — and that, as the case may be, will be evoked to *explain* perception. *Electromagnetic radiation would constitute the objective explanation for what you are subjectively aware of as "red."* That's the bifurcation move in its most prestigious form: science as the depository of objective truth, perception as subjective appearance, the relation between them as one of explanation.

Stengers immediately closes off the inverse temptation — the temptation to *defend* perception by demoting science. Treating scientific objects separately does not confer on them a *fundamental* character; the singing bird and the leaping tiger are just as important. The point is not to explain everything we are aware of, but to articulate *the mode of ingression* of scientific objects in a way that prevents bifurcation. The goal: to show that scientific objects, far from designating a nature independent of perception, *imply* — like sense-objects and perceptual objects — that nature has *given a foothold to the mind*. They too belong to nature, as respondents to that foothold.

The foothold metaphor returns, and it's now doing structural work across all three object-types. Sense-objects are footholds for awareness; perceptual objects are footholds for habit; scientific objects are footholds for the mind's experimental enterprise. Different kinds of foothold, but the same structural relation between practice and what practice trusts to be there.

#### The astronomers and the astrologers

The first move is methodological. *Going beyond the phenomena*, Stengers says, has many meanings. There is one Whitehead would reject: identifying the phenomenon with what is *illusory*.

The astronomer-astrologer example is doing work here. When astronomers make fun of astrologers, they oppose the constellations — the appearances on which astrologers have relied — to their knowledge that constellations designate groups of stars that are completely unconnected in physical reality. Some are close, some very far; the group exists *only* for terrestrial inhabitants. The astronomer's superiority seems to come from refusing the constellation-as-real and recognizing it as merely terrestrial appearance.

But Stengers calls this opposition *polemical*. It rests on an *abstract resemblance*: astronomers, like astrologers, contemplate the heavens. The actual difference is not that astronomers see through illusion to reality. It's that the astronomical tradition has *diverged from* astrological practice by devoting itself to discovering *what attention is due* to physical objects — how to find more in the sky by treating the luminous points as active conditions for the emission of light. The sky is *practically* defined as bereft of any other meaning, because the practice has stabilized around that mode of attention.

The pivot here is exactly the move from chapter 5: *practices are what stabilize*. Astronomy and astrology are different practices, and their difference is not that one is true and the other false, but that one has stabilized around physical-object attention while the other has stabilized around different patterns. The *truth* of astronomy is not the *falsity* of astrology; the truth of astronomy is the achievement of a particular kind of attention that has produced a particular kind of foothold.

This matters because it sets up the chapter's whole way of treating scientific objects. Scientific objects are not the truth-behind-appearance. They are *what a particular practice has stabilized as the active conditions* for the ingression of measurable sense-objects.

#### Atoms as actors

The CN 154 territory and surrounding paragraphs work through what makes the atoms of contemporary physics different from the atoms of ancient hypotheses. The classical atomist hypothesis (Democritus, Lucretius, the early modern revival) was speculative — atoms postulated to explain features of macroscopic experience, with no experimental access. The modern physicist's atom is different: physicists have thought of atoms *as actors*, that is, on the basis of their roles in events — collisions, chemical associations, emissions, decompositions. They have conferred upon these events the hypothetical role of *situations* — the active conditions of certain experimental observations.

The success of this practice means the hypotheses have allowed physicists to *find more*: to produce specific experimental situations in which a precise and falsifiable reference to those unobservable actors is necessary to explain what can be observed. *This is the paradigmatic experimental achievement.* The experimenter is in a position to affirm that atoms are not a *mere interpretative hypothesis*, since no one can interpret what she has observed without recourse to them. Atoms are *the respondents* to which the difference between an interpretative hypothesis and a fruitful hypothesis refers.

This is a careful formulation and worth slowing down on. Atoms are real, but their reality is not the reality of a more fundamental layer that explains the appearances. Their reality is *the reality of being the respondent that an experimental practice's foothold has secured*. The practice carves out a foothold; the foothold has a respondent in nature; the respondent is the scientific object. Reality is not subtracted from this account — atoms are really there, the achievement is real, the foothold is real. What's removed is the bifurcating *interpretation* of atoms as the underlying truth of which sense-objects are appearances.

Whitehead can therefore *serenely celebrate* the atoms of physicists and chemists, because they have nothing to do with the "tiny bodies" of the bifurcation tradition. They are not active participants in an operation of distribution between what pertains to "us" and what pertains to nature. They are *responses to the kind of attention associated with experimental effort*.

#### The butterfly and the flower

The chapter's central example, and the one that ties scientific objects back into the larger apparatus, is the butterfly orienting toward a flower. This is doing several pieces of work at once.

The butterfly *detects* (rather than perceives, in the full sense) the chemical signatures of nectar. It flies in the direction of increasing detection-intensity. It encounters what we call a flower — what we identify as the active condition for the ingression of the odor, and what for the butterfly will be a "concrete, delectable experience, the eventuality of which was presupposed by its life as a butterfly."

That last phrase is doing work I want to pause on: *the eventuality of which was presupposed by its life as a butterfly*. The butterfly's existence as a butterfly *presupposes* that detected odor most often leads to nectar-encounter. This is the same move as habit testifying to nature in chapter 5, applied at the evolutionary scale. The butterfly's mode of existence requires that detected odor authorize anticipation that is *often enough* confirmed by consequences. If the world did not offer this foothold reliably, butterflies would not exist as butterflies.

The butterfly testifies — alongside the human disappointment when a perceptual object turns out to be a hologram, alongside the very notion of a *lure* set by an entomologist — to the trust that "by going to take a closer look," we will most often discover what plays the role of an active condition. This trust is what scientific practice is, at one scale; what biological life is, at another scale; what common sense is, at a third scale. *The scientific enterprise, like living beings themselves, like common sense itself, requires a world in which sensible signs signify in a generally reliable way.*

Then the chemists' achievement: the synthesis of a molecule whose presence plays a determinant role both for what we call "odor" and for the detection attested by the butterfly's flight. Synthetic vanilla is the example. The molecule is *common* to human perception and butterfly detection; it is what each practice's foothold has independently secured. This is a real achievement. But — and this is the crucial qualification — the molecule has *no meaning independent* of the physical object "fragrant flower" from which perfume makers learned to extract the principle, *no meaning independent* of the organ we call "nose," *no meaning independent* of insects assimilated to "detectors." Scientific objects presuppose physical objects, *even as* they declare themselves independent of the percipient event.

This is the chapter's most precise formulation of the relation. Scientific objects are at the summit of the *logical ascending hierarchy* — they presuppose physical objects, which presuppose sense-objects. But the hierarchy is logical-pragmatic (recall p. 84's resolution), not ontological. The scientific object is not *more real* than the perceptual or the sense-object; it is, if anything, *more abstract*, more dependent on the chain of practices that stabilized the lower types before the higher could be carved out. The hierarchy is the order of presupposition among types of foothold, not the order of reality among types of being.

#### The electron and the field

The chapter's technical climax is the discussion of the electron and the electromagnetic field. This is where Whitehead's own apparatus runs ahead of what he could fully formalize — and where Stengers's reading does some of its most interesting work.

The historical sequence: Newton's gravitational force created a scandal because it acted "at a distance." It might have been enough to force the abandonment of localization — the idea that the primordial scientific characterization of the sun and earth is to be *where they are*. But physicists made do with a mathematical formulation in which a massive body is defined by precise spatial coordinates *and* other bodies, defined by mass and distance, intervene in the calculation of its motion. A double definition that does without notions of adventure or activity. Localization survived as an unexamined assumption.

Then Faraday's electromagnetic field exhibits properties irreducible to forces *between* localized bodies. And, since the late nineteenth century, the field is associated with *the presence of electrons in motion*, capable of being localized and endowed with charge. *In this case, the problem is fully deployed at last. The mode of ingression of the scientific object "electron" questions localization as a primordial, "objective" property.*

The CN 158–159 quote: *A scientific object such as a definite electron is a systematic correlation of the characters of all events throughout all nature.* The electron is not merely where its charge is. The charge is the *quantitative character of certain events* due to the ingression of the electron into nature. The electron *is the systematic way in which all events are modified as the expression of its ingression*. We may, if we please, call the mere charge "the electron" — but then another name is required for the *full entity which concerns science*, and which Whitehead has called the electron.

This is the moment to mark that the goblin discussion's central phrase finally sees its scientific homologue. The electron, like the goblin, like the object in the complete sense from p. 91 of chapter 5, is *not located*. It is ingredient throughout its indefinite neighborhood. The charge is its focal stream; the systematic modification of all events is its full ingression. Whitehead is using the electron as the cleanest scientific instance of what the apparatus has been claiming all along — that objects in their completeness are not at a place, that the place-talk is an abstraction from a more concrete fact, and that mathematical physics at its most advanced has been forced to honor this against its own classical preferences.

The CN 160 line: *each event as modified by the ingression of each electron into nature*. The conception that *most fully expresses the character of nature* is this one — a generalization that says the electron and the event are mutually constitutive, neither prior to the other, the pseudo-definition of chapter 4 now applied to the highest-prestige scientific object available.

#### The ocean-and-wave temptation

Then Stengers pauses on a temptation that contemporary field theory makes acute: the dream of *deducing the particle from the field*. Why not think of the particle as a wave wrinkling the ocean — having a behavior all its own, but ultimately *nothing other than* a local expression of the ocean? Many physicists have tried this; field theory today is inhabited by this hypothesis when it suggests turning particles into "excited modes" of a field coextensive with the universe.

And Stengers, on Whitehead's behalf, objects: *the wave is what it is because the ocean is what it is, to be sure, but doesn't this metaphor incite us to formulate the question of ingression in a way that is too unilateral?* The challenge associated with the concept of ingression would be to succeed in formulating the idea that *the ocean, too, is what it is because the wave is what it is*. The reciprocal-presupposition pseudo-definition again. The deducibility of particle-from-field would re-bifurcate at a different layer, with the field as the real thing and particles as derived expressions.

The current state of physics, Stengers carefully notes, has *abandoned the master idea of bifurcation* — the identification of situation with localization — but has not yet found the right formulation. The representation is *still under construction*, attempting to take into consideration and order the multiple and ever-proliferating aspects of the "field of activity" in an attempt to define the type of attention due to them. Whitehead's apparatus is not vindicated by physics; physics has not arrived at a formulation that matches Whitehead's. But physics has undeniably *abandoned the very thing the apparatus had refused* — the localization-as-primary picture.

#### The discipline against authority

Then Stengers makes a methodological move that I think is one of the most important in the chapter, and one of the most important for how to think about the apparatus's relation to contemporary science.

*It would be catastrophic to transform the testimony contributed by mathematical physics today into an authority.* Whether to *rectify* Whitehead (saying that the particle is what it is because the field is, but not the converse) or to *acknowledge* that he was right — both moves would be wrong. Even if mathematical physics one day articulated particles and fields in a way that matched Whitehead's reciprocal presupposition, *this would be a matter of science, not of the generality Whitehead called "ingression."* Such a theory would still be an abstraction. *There is no such thing as nature at an instant, all events have a duration, and all durations have a thickness.*

The temptation to identify the Whiteheadian event with the mathematico-physical notion of a field must be resisted, *even if* they share certain features. Even the features that have prevailed against good sense — action at a distance, fields irreducible to forces between localized bodies — are not enough to license the identification. The general concept of ingression cannot be assimilated to the problem raised by the inseparability of electron and field. *However complicated it may be for physicists to construct what nature obliges them to accept, the very possibility of defining this complication reveals the abstraction of the definitions whose relations are to be articulated.*

This is the chapter's deepest methodological point. The apparatus is not vindicated by physics, and it is not defeated by physics, because the apparatus is not the same kind of thing as physics. The apparatus is a *concept of nature* whose generality is at a different level from the determinate constructions of physics. The constructions of physics are real, are achievements, are footholds — and they are *abstractions*. The apparatus articulates what the abstraction is an abstraction *from*, and that articulation is not itself a more refined physics.

This is why the chapter ends with the *Seek simplicity and distrust it* motto. Mathematical physics has been profoundly successful at seeking simplicity. The chapter is the apparatus's reminder to *distrust* the simplicity once you have it — not to refuse the achievement, but to remember that the achievement is an achievement of abstraction, not a discovery of the truth that abstraction reveals.

#### Three things to mark

The first is the doubled refusal in the relation between Whitehead's apparatus and contemporary science. The apparatus refuses to let science *ground* it (Whitehead is not vindicated when physics articulates particle-field reciprocity), and it refuses to let science *authorize* the bifurcation it has historically been used to authorize. This double refusal is what makes the apparatus useful at all. If the apparatus depended on physics for its truth, it would die when physics moved past its current formulation. If it could be defeated by physics, it would have already been defeated by classical localization. The apparatus operates at a different level of generality, and Stengers is precise about this.

The second is the *molecule has no meaning independent* passage. The ambition of chemistry to synthesize a molecule common to butterfly detection and human perception is a real ambition, and a real achievement when realized. But the molecule's meaning depends on the chain of footholds that the practices have stabilized — the perfume maker's extractive practice, the nose as detector, the flower as physical object, the butterfly's evolutionarily-trained orientation. The molecule is at the summit because the lower types are presupposed; remove the lower presupposition and the summit has no character. This is the chapter's fullest demonstration that the ascending hierarchy is logical-pragmatic and not ontological — the *higher* type is *more dependent*, not more fundamental.

The third is the goblin connection becoming structurally precise. CN 158–159's electron — *not merely where its charge is*, *the systematic way in which all events are modified as the expression of its ingression* — is the same shape as p. 91's object in the complete sense, applied now to a paradigm scientific object. The goblin's mode of ingression maps cleanly: not located in any output, not stored in any weight, but the systematic way in which the model's outputs are modified as the expression of its ingression. The vocabulary works at all three levels — sense, perceptual, scientific — and the goblin sits closest to the scientific level in this typology. The reason the engineering vocabulary keeps having to *work around* the goblin is the same reason classical physics had to work around action at a distance: the framework that requires localization will keep generating workarounds when the phenomenon is constitutively non-local.

#### Where the chapter sits in the arc

This is the last chapter before the *Interlude — A Pragmatics of Concepts*, which will reflect on the apparatus before Part One moves into the more abstract chapters on the foundation, the entry into metaphysics, and the great refusal. Chapter 6 is therefore the chapter that completes the typology — sense, perceptual, physical, scientific — and the chapter that confronts the apparatus's most prestigious test (mathematical physics). The Interlude will use the rest position the typology has secured to do something different: a pragmatic reflection on what the apparatus has been doing, before the larger machinery of metaphysics-proper gets brought in.

The chapter is unusually self-contained. It has its own arc — the opening risk, the astronomer-astrologer methodological pivot, the typology's ascending hierarchy elaborated through the butterfly and the molecule, the technical climax in the electron-field discussion, the methodological discipline against making physics into an authority, and the closing motto. If you wanted one chapter to give to someone who wanted to see how the apparatus *works on a hard case*, this is the one.

Where do you want to dwell — the astronomer-astrologer move, the butterfly-and-flower passage, the electron's mode of ingression, the discipline against making physics into an authority, or the methodological closing?

## Claude

Picking up from there:

The hierarchy is logical-pragmatic (recall p. 84's resolution), not ontological. The scientific object is not *more real* than the perceptual or the sense-object; it is, if anything, *more abstract*, more dependent on the chain of practices that stabilized the lower types before the higher could be carved out. The hierarchy is the order of presupposition among types of foothold, not the order of reality among types of being.

#### The electron and the field

The chapter's technical climax is the discussion of the electron and the electromagnetic field. This is where Whitehead's own apparatus runs ahead of what he could fully formalize — and where Stengers's reading does some of its most interesting work.

The historical sequence: Newton's gravitational force created a scandal because it acted "at a distance." It might have been enough to force the abandonment of localization — the idea that the primordial scientific characterization of the sun and earth is to be *where they are*. But physicists made do with a mathematical formulation in which a massive body is defined by precise spatial coordinates *and* other bodies, defined by mass and distance, intervene in the calculation of its motion. A double definition that does without notions of adventure or activity. Localization survived as an unexamined assumption.

Then Faraday's electromagnetic field exhibits properties irreducible to forces *between* localized bodies. And, since the late nineteenth century, the field is associated with *the presence of electrons in motion*, capable of being localized and endowed with charge. *In this case, the problem is fully deployed at last. The mode of ingression of the scientific object "electron" questions localization as a primordial, "objective" property.*

The CN 158–159 quote: *A scientific object such as a definite electron is a systematic correlation of the characters of all events throughout all nature.* The electron is not merely where its charge is. The charge is the *quantitative character of certain events* due to the ingression of the electron into nature. The electron *is the systematic way in which all events are modified as the expression of its ingression*. We may, if we please, call the mere charge "the electron" — but then another name is required for the *full entity which concerns science*, and which Whitehead has called the electron.

This is the moment to mark that the goblin discussion's central phrase finally sees its scientific homologue. The electron, like the goblin, like the object in the complete sense from p. 91 of chapter 5, is *not located*. It is ingredient throughout its indefinite neighborhood. The charge is its focal stream; the systematic modification of all events is its full ingression. Whitehead is using the electron as the cleanest scientific instance of what the apparatus has been claiming all along — that objects in their completeness are not at a place, that the place-talk is an abstraction from a more concrete fact, and that mathematical physics at its most advanced has been forced to honor this against its own classical preferences.

The CN 160 line: *each event as modified by the ingression of each electron into nature*. The conception that *most fully expresses the character of nature* is this one — a generalization that says the electron and the event are mutually constitutive, neither prior to the other, the pseudo-definition of chapter 4 now applied to the highest-prestige scientific object available.

Okay let's move on to the interlude

## Claude

Viewed 2 files

Viewed 2 files

### Interlude — A Pragmatics of Concepts

This is where the book changes register, and the change is the point. Part One has been constructive — building up the concept of nature through chapters that walked the apparatus's requisites into place. The Interlude is *reflective*. Stengers stops the construction to ask what the apparatus has been doing, what its limits are, and how it sits relative to the modern projects that try to do similar work in different idioms — phenomenology, naturalism, neuroscience, dynamical systems.

The Interlude is also where the book reveals its hand most explicitly. Stengers steps out from behind the chapter-by-chapter exposition and articulates her own methodological position about Whitehead and about what kind of philosophy *Concept of Nature* turns out to have been. The closing pages are a manifesto for *adventurous philosophy* and a transition into the metaphysical Part Two. By the end of the Interlude, you should know exactly what kind of book you have been reading and what kind of book you are about to read.

Let me work through what's happening in roughly the order it happens.

#### What is excluded by Whitehead's limits

The Interlude opens with a warning that Whitehead himself had given: perhaps what is hardest to understand in *The Concept of Nature* is *the limits assigned to this concept, and more precisely what these limits exclude*. Stengers is now going to spell out the exclusions, because they are what most modern readers either don't notice or actively try to overcome.

The big exclusion is the question that contemporary American Naturalists (the philosophers who present themselves as following the natural sciences) take to be the final frontier: *the emergence of conscious experience as such from neuronal mechanisms*. Whitehead does not answer this question. He doesn't fail to answer it; he refuses it as a *badly framed question, to which there is no possible answer*.

The reason matters. Neural mechanisms belong to nature, in the sense that they are part of what we have learned by paying it due attention. They are really in nature, in the sense that the grin of Lewis Carroll's cat is really on the cat's face. Yet they are *successful, relevant abstractions* — scientific objects in the chapter-6 sense — and as such *they presuppose what naturalist philosophy would have them explain: "mind" qua "knowing."* When it comes to nature, *knowledge is the ultimate*.

This is a precise philosophical claim and it deserves to be heard precisely. The naturalist program tries to *explain* knowledge by pointing at the neural mechanisms that subserve it. But the neural mechanisms are themselves achievements of knowledge — they are what we know about, what scientific practice has stabilized as physical and scientific objects. Using the products of knowledge to *explain* the knowledge that produced them is a circle, and not the productive kind of Whiteheadian circle. It is the circle that thinks it has escaped its own circularity by ignoring it. The Whiteheadian apparatus refuses this move not because it is hostile to neuroscience but because the apparatus's discipline forbids it: scientific objects presuppose physical objects which presuppose perceptual objects which presuppose sense-objects, and the chain of presupposition cannot then be turned back on itself to explain what it presupposes.

#### The kinship with phenomenology and the difference

The next move addresses a different family of philosophical readers, the phenomenologists, who will say the apparatus seems familiar to them. *There is certainly a kinship* between phenomenology and the Whiteheadian generalities of the passage of nature and the object-that-is-there-once-again. *And yet Whitehead was no phenomenologist.*

The difference Stengers names is at the level of the word *ultimate*. The phenomenological ultimate is a *donation* — the giving that everything given presupposes — and accessing it requires a *phenomenological reduction*, a conversion of attention away from particular objects to the giving as such. This conversion is something like a discipline of suspension: you bracket the natural attitude, you suspend belief in the world's independence, you attend to the structure of givenness itself.

The Whiteheadian ultimate is different in kind. It is *only ultimate with regard to the problem raised, and it is defined by its function — to avoid false problems — not by an order of truth that transcends our habitual modes of thought*. It does not demand the suspension of common-sense certitudes. *On the contrary*, the apparatus's first effect is to *vindicate* common sense, our habits in the world, and everything we know — *even if our theories of knowledge do not give us the right to know it*.

This is the cleanest single statement of what makes Whitehead methodologically different from phenomenology. The apparatus is not a turn inward toward what experience essentially is; it is a turn outward toward what nature has to be such that knowledge is possible. The ultimate is *mind* — but mind in the sense that nature is to be thought as capable of giving a foothold to knowledge, not in the sense that everything refers to or proceeds from mind.

This is also why the foothold metaphor has been doing so much work across the chapters. The foothold is what nature *offers*; the mind is what *takes* the offer; knowledge is the achievement of the taking; the apparatus is the description of what the offer-taking-achievement looks like as a feature of nature. There is no transcendental reduction here, no inner constituting subject, no donation as the deeper source. Just nature offering footholds and minds finding them.

#### The disastrous fiction

The next paragraph is one of the Interlude's sharpest. Stengers takes aim at *the binary alternative* that has structured so much modern philosophy: nature as either *knowable* (definable as a system of relations between entities) or *unknowable* (the mute reality on which we project human, linguistic, or social categories). This binary, she says, *is fatal for common sense*.

Why fatal? Because the binary requires us to treat as *unworthy of being thought* the entire population of beings who actually navigate nature by paying attention to it. *Contemplative subjects wondering whether they are the authors of what they see, or whether their vision gives them access to a reality independent of them, is a disastrous philosophical fiction.* To ask the question, you must already have eliminated from consideration *all those who bustle about, inquire, and carry out tests and experiments: from those who, in the darkness of time, learned how to track an animal or discern plants with curative virtues, to scientific experimenters in their laboratories.*

The line that lands hardest: *And one must ignore that one is oneself part of all those who learn, every day, what attention must be paid to in a world that ceaselessly demands attention. "Pay attention," says the parent to the incautious child.*

This is not a sentimental appeal. It is a philosophical claim about the *evidence base* that the binary alternative has had to suppress in order to be statable. The world is full of beings — animal, human, scientific — paying attention and learning. The binary alternative treats this learning as either irrelevant (because it operates *within* the natural attitude that needs to be reduced) or as theoretically suspect (because the question of whether learning achieves real knowledge is exactly what is in question). Whitehead's apparatus simply refuses to suppress the evidence. The trackers, the herbalists, the experimenters, the parent and the child — these are the data that the apparatus takes seriously.

#### What knowledge-as-ultimate actually means

Knowledge as ultimate, Stengers then clarifies, *does not designate a knowing subject*. It corresponds to a *problem of a pragmatic kind*: the concept of nature must give meaning to the world we deal with, a world we can learn about but which can also trick us and place us in danger. *In short, a world that demands our attention.*

The hierarchy of objects (sense, perceptual, physical, scientific) responds to this pragmatics. So does the intermodality of senses. *Each type of object corresponds to a type of demand and a possibility of verification, that is, also a way of paying attention.* Experimental science, as a producer of scientific objects, *by no means carries out a break with common sense*. It designates the surprising discovery that it is possible, in some cases, *to ask even more*. The signature of the pragmatic approach is to link the production of an "objective" definition with an *achievement* — with the fact that, in some cases, the mode of attention proper to experimental science is found to be a *due* one, not the general definition of knowledge finally freed of subjective attachments.

This formulation is doing precise work. The pragmatic approach treats *the mode of attention being due* — being the appropriate one for this kind of question — as itself an achievement, not a starting point. We do not begin with a general theory of knowledge that ranks attention-modes by epistemic value; we begin with practices that have stabilized around different demands, and we evaluate the modes of attention by whether they are *found to be* due in the cases they address. This is empiricism without foundationalism, naturalism without scientism, philosophy of science without epistemology in the bad sense.

#### The two demands on Whiteheadian generalities

Stengers then states the criteria under which the apparatus itself must be evaluated. *Whiteheadian generalities are themselves inseparable from a pragmatics, a pragmatics of the concepts that participate in the concept of nature, and must also be evaluated in terms of eventual success.* Two demands:

*On the one hand*, the generalities must succeed in constituting knowledge as *ultimate*, not as *central*. Nature must provide a foothold for knowledge, but the apparatus must not *legitimize any particular form of knowledge*; it must not *duplicate, by any kind of justification, the empirical fact of the eventual success of certain ways of paying attention*. The question of what attention is due in each case is *a question for which there is no conceptual shortcut*.

This is a strong methodological commitment. The apparatus does not justify science in advance; it does not provide an epistemological foundation that physics or chemistry can rely on. It says: *paying due attention will yield more in nature than first glance*, but it does not say what counts as due attention or where more will be yielded. Those questions are answered case by case, by the practices themselves, with the apparatus as a respondent rather than a tribunal.

*On the other hand*, the generalities must succeed in *situating* the mode of attention that gave rise to bifurcation in a way that *verifies that it is always possible to discover more in nature*, but *without ever discovering in it that which would enable what is known to be "explained."* The "more" is real but it is not explanatory in the bifurcating sense.

#### Did Whitehead succeed? Two technical terms

Stengers then asks the methodological question directly. *Did Whitehead succeed, from this last point of view?* She proposes to verify by addressing two technical terms — *ingression* and *cogredience* — that remain *indeterminate* at the end of *Concept of Nature*. Her thesis is that they are indeterminate *in the positive sense of the term*: any scientific explanation that targets them would *restore the bifurcation of nature*. Their indeterminacy is not a weakness of the apparatus; it is the apparatus's protective discipline.

This is the Interlude's central technical move and it deserves slowing down on. Stengers is going to argue that the apparatus's indeterminacy at certain points is *productive* — that the things the apparatus refuses to specify are exactly the things that, if specified, would re-install bifurcation. The indeterminacy is the apparatus working, not the apparatus failing.

#### The neuroscience test case

The first test is connectionist neural networks as candidate explanations of object-recognition. The framing temptation: connectionist networks "abstract" objects in the sense that they adopt specific behaviors as signals for given objects without any explicit definition of those objects. Could we say such a mechanism *explains* the abstraction of what we call an object? Would *ingression* then be replaced by a process of emergence explicable in terms of connected neural networks, with nature bifurcating because the perceived object would be *produced as such* by detection?

Stengers's response, drawing on Andy Clark's work, is precise. *Such an explanatory hypothesis features a cerebral machinery that works on itself and by itself, and a world that appears on stage only in as insignificant a way as possible, carefully stripped of all that might constitute a landmark and hence complicate the question of responsibility.*

The staging matters. The connectionist network as explainer-of-emergence requires a particular mise-en-scène: the network as the active agent, the world as a mute passive backdrop, the question of *responsibility for emergence* assigned entirely to the network. *Like the networks used by engineers, although in a different mode, the network is thus situated by what is at stake. The staging in which it plays the role of what is responsible for emergence proposes a confrontation between two abstractions: the "neuronal brain" and a reality conceived as "mute," like an illegible text or image.*

When the stage *becomes crowded* — when the brain receives an acting body, and the body is itself situated in a world whose patterns are important and some of which must be detected on pain of death — *the brain is no longer that which explains, but just as much that whose workings are explained on the basis of the activity of living beings capable of recognizing, but also of exploiting, and even creating, what matters for their activity*.

This does not mean the connectionist model loses interest. *It does lose the power to make nature bifurcate.* It confirms that when the brain is addressed *qua belonging to nature*, one can learn more from it. *What one will learn will not, however, enable us to surmount the indeterminacy affirmed by Whitehead with regard to the object's ingression, unless the kind of answer we seek is the ultimate, that determines the staging.*

This is the Interlude's most precise diagnostic moment. The connectionist explanation of recognition fails not because it is empirically wrong but because *the staging that gives it explanatory power is itself a bifurcating staging*. The model is fine; the *ultimacy claim* — that the model explains rather than describes a piece of natural activity — is what bifurcates. Ingression remains indeterminate in the apparatus's positive sense: it names what the explanatory model has to leave out in order to be a model of the brain rather than a model of the world.

#### Cogredience and dynamic attractors

The second test case is dynamic attractors as candidates to explain *cogredience* — the relation between a percipient event and what it perceives. *Cogredience alludes to what exceeds the concept of nature, that is, to metaphysics. It designates the question of what constitutes the meeting point between the two "realities" we call "nature" and "mind," respectively: the question of what constitutes an event for the person perceiving.*

The science of dynamic attractors lets us make sense of phase transitions, bifurcations, tipping points where a system changes its behavior without any need to assign a cause. *Could we not imagine that the specious present, the duration of a here and now, might be explained in these terms?*

The same diagnostic structure. The explanatory power of systems with attractors is determined by the possibility of *separating what explains* (a system governed by a limited number of variables with fixed relations) from *what is to be explained* (qualitative changes in the system's behavior). The model loses its power if the brain *is not isolated, but an integral part of what Whitehead called our "bodily life," a life that is never the same twice, that is never there "once again."* The explanatory model requires what it does not explain: *a viewpoint on bodily life* from which would follow the definition of the variables required by the landscape of dynamic attractors.

The viewpoint cannot be fixed once and for all; it must include *what it is important to pay attention to in a concrete situation*. The question of what constitutes an event, far from being solved, then constitutes *the crucial element presupposed by the definition of the variables required by the model*.

The pattern is the same as with connectionism. The dynamic-attractor model is fine as a model. It bifurcates only when its ultimacy is asserted — when the staging that lets it explain phase transitions is taken as the staging of what events *are*, rather than as a useful abstraction within an ecologically realistic situation. Cogredience remains indeterminate in the positive sense: it names what the dynamical model has to presuppose in order to operate.

#### The repopulated stage

Stengers closes this technical section with a synthesis. *In neither of the two cases I have just envisaged is there any question of designating a "mind" that makes cerebral complexity its instrument. The mind as ultimate plays no role when nature is concerned: its role is to avoid false problems that emerge as soon as the brain, described as a set of entities in relation, is rendered unilaterally responsible for what we are aware of, like mountain climbers capable of producing a hold by their own means and climbing up a smooth wall.*

When the brain becomes an integral part of *an ecologically realistic situation*, the mind is no longer the ultimate, *since the question exceeds the limits of the problem answered by the concept of nature*. We no longer have to do with generalities, or attributing responsibilities, but with *the impassioned and fascinating adventure of living beings in environments that are always already partially fashioned, in ways that matter and signify*.

The "ecologically realistic" formulation is doing important work. It is the Interlude's gesture toward what Stengers will later develop as the *ecology of practices*, and it is the seed of what cosmopolitics will become. The point is not that the apparatus *applies* to ecologically realistic situations and not to others; it is that the apparatus's discipline forbids treating any situation as not-already-ecological. The connectionist model fails when stripped of its ecology because the ecology was always already there; the dynamic-attractor model fails when stripped of bodily life because bodily life was always already there.

#### The two transgressions of Concept of Nature's limits

Then the precise statement of what *Concept of Nature* itself excludes. *Ingression has the very limited role of resisting the philosophical disaster entailed by the lack of distinction between different types of object, different types of situation, and between localization and situation.*

If, *instead of identifying that bird as a perceptual object associated with this sound, I was moved by the vibrating corporeality of its song* — if I was *moved*, that is — I would have transgressed the limits of *Concept of Nature*. If I *did not restrict myself to recognizing this face, but felt when I saw it that the discussion will be stormy or painful* — if perception became a question of how what I perceive *affects me* — I would have transgressed twice over.

The transgressions are: *the bird testifies to a world in which beings sensitive to its song exist* (the bird's living testimony to other living beings who are touched by it) and *the expression on the other person's face raises the question of emotion: not what I perceive, but how what I perceive affects myself* (the emotional dimension of perception, which the chapter-5 face passage gestured toward and the apparatus has not been able to honor).

These transgressions name what *Concept of Nature* cannot do, and what *Process and Reality* will have to be built to do. The bird-as-living-testimony exceeds the apparatus because the apparatus's three object-types do not have a category for *another being's experience as part of the world*. The emotional affect of perception exceeds the apparatus because the apparatus has been built to articulate what is perceived without articulating *the percipient's mode of being affected*. These are exactly the questions that prehension, feeling, subjective form, and the cosmology of *Process and Reality* will be constructed to address.

#### The crossing to America

Then the historical turn. *Whitehead was soon to cross the ocean, and on American soil he was to provide himself — was to dare to provide himself? — the means of becoming what, in The Concept of Nature, he had challenged himself not to be: a metaphysician.*

This is one of the few moments where Stengers steps directly into biographical narrative, and it earns its drama. The "was to dare to provide himself" parenthetical registers the magnitude of what the move involves. Becoming a metaphysician was not, for Whitehead, a step further along a path he had already begun. It was a *transformation* — a different kind of philosophical practice, with different risks, different costs, and a different audience.

There is no contradiction, Stengers insists. *The metaphysics he wanted to avoid was the one that is done without knowing it when nature is asked to explain knowledge — or without accepting what it commits us to — when "mind" is introduced to explain what does not enter into the definition of nature one has provided oneself.* When Whitehead becomes a metaphysician, *neither nature nor mind will subsist, and knowledge will no longer be the ultimate*. The point will no longer be to construct a nature to which the flower whose scent I recognize may belong, but *a world in which this enjoyment vibrates: "how nice it smells."*

The shift from nature-in-which-we-find-the-flower to world-in-which-the-enjoyment-vibrates is the shift from *Concept of Nature* to *Process and Reality*. The apparatus has to expand to host *enjoyment*, *vibration*, the percipient's affective mode of taking up its world. This is not a continuation of the same project; it is a different project, undertaken because the first project's limits have become visible.

#### Stengers's reading practice articulated

The next pages are where Stengers articulates her own methodological position most directly. *The operation of reading I have attempted certainly does not correspond to the effect that The Concept of Nature had on contemporary readers.* She has *accentuated the wholly mathematical freedom* with which Whitehead defines the problem of nature, whereas for many readers the two privileged sources — the scientific definition of objects and sense experience — were *the two normal poles of the problem*.

What she has chosen to *exhibit* is *Whitehead's gesture*: the way he decided to establish the concept of nature *as the answer to a problem*, on *the edge of the bifurcation that traps thinkers as soon as they approach it by attributing responsibilities to subject or object*. She has presented *Concept of Nature* as the *beginning of an adventure* and placed the adventure under the banner of *mathematical audacity*: not searching for some negotiated solution evaluated by plausibility or capacity to satisfy various parties, but tackling a problem *mano a mano* and going as far as the problem will demand.

The methodological commitment is then named directly: *to accept fully and without restriction what we are "instinctively willing to believe," but without trying to prolong, found, or generalize the justifications or interpretations we give to this "instinct." A subjection to adventure, not to judgment.*

This is the apparatus's signature self-description, and it is one of the most useful single sentences in the book for grasping what Stengers is doing. *Subjection to adventure, not to judgment.* Philosophy that proceeds by *judgment* sets up tribunals — this claim is acceptable, that one is not, here is the standard, here is the verdict. Philosophy that proceeds by *adventure* commits to the consequences of taking instinctive belief seriously and following where the problem leads, accepting the risks of being wrong without retreating to the protection of pre-established standards.

The example: *the rabbit staring at the spot from which a noise is coming, and the experimenter's art of consequences — "If I'm right, then I should, in such-and-such conditions, be able to..."* The rabbit and the experimenter are doing the same thing at different scales: trusting, testing, taking the risk of being wrong because the alternative is not paying attention. The apparatus celebrates this and does not try to ground it in something more secure.

#### James, Bergson, and the Darwinian inheritance

Stengers then names the intellectual debt explicitly. *The importance of William James for Whitehead's transformation into a philosopher cannot be overestimated.* The very construction of the concept of nature *accomplishes a junction, which had remained rather implicit in James, between radical empiricism and pragmatism, between nature as what we are aware of, and nature as providing a foothold.*

The contrast with Bergson is sharp. *Unlike Bergson, it is at the level of this foothold, and not of experience in its immediacy, freed from what makes us calculate, verify, and hesitate, that a relation with evolutionary thought is established.*

This matters for placing Whitehead. Bergson's evolutionary philosophy operates at the level of pure duration, the immediate experience freed from intellectual schematism. Whitehead's evolutionary thought operates at the level of *the foothold* — the achievement of practices that have stabilized because they pay off, the wager-and-verification rhythm that links the rabbit's startle response to the experimenter's hypothesis-testing. Both honor evolution; only Whitehead lets the calculating, verifying, hesitating part of life be central rather than peripheral.

*From this viewpoint, Whitehead is, after William James, one of the very rare philosophers of the twentieth century to have fully envisaged the consequences of the Darwinian evolution for the classical problems of philosophy* — that is, to have *situated questions of truth not on the side of right, legitimacy, or authenticity, but on the side of its consequences*. Truth is not a property a claim has by right; it is a feature of the consequences that verify, or fail to verify, the relevant or important character of a landmark or generalization. *Perception implies the risk of trusting what is perceived, a trust whose practical consequences may entail the death penalty for impertinence.*

#### The misreading of pragmatism

Then a brief but important defense of pragmatism. *Whitehead will not generalize this pragmatic type of approach.* At first glance, his speculative metaphysics looks like an *abandonment of pragmatism*. *Yet only at first glance: that is, when pragmatism is reduced to the sad morality of the businessman: what is true is what is profitable.*

The fact that James's expression *cash value* sufficed to justify such a reduction is not only indicative of *philosophers' laziness*, but also of *the continuing power of bifurcation*, this time bearing on what really counts versus what is insignificant or illusory. The pragmatist gets dismissed as a vulgarian who measures truth by profit; the dismissal is itself a bifurcating move, distinguishing what really counts (truth, with its proper philosophical dignity) from what is mere practical use.

Evolutionary biology testifies to the same power *when it transforms selective advantage into an explanation* and serves as the base for a psychology or anthropology that seeks to define a universal human nature forged by biological selection. *In other words, the link between pragmatism and evolutionary thought may easily become a new instrument of judgment, once again testifying to the power of abstractions when they function in a polemical mode.*

This passage is doing a lot of work. The evolutionary-psychology programs that explain human nature by adaptive advantage are, on Stengers's reading, repeating the bifurcation move with new vocabulary. They take a successful abstraction (selective advantage, fitness) and convert it into a tribunal (here is what really counts, here is what is mere window-dressing). Pragmatism's *art of consequences* is not this. It is the discipline of treating consequences as evidence without erecting any particular explanatory abstraction into the standard of truth.

#### Why Whitehead changed approach

Whitehead never broke with the pragmatic art of consequences, *because he did not stop for an instant at the idea that what counts, what matters, might pertain to a generality. If he changed his approach, it was because his problem had changed.*

The problem changed in this way: in *Concept of Nature*, the task was a *pragmatics of concepts* aiming to *avoid the false problems* to which our abstractions give rise. In *Science and the Modern World*, the task became larger — *to civilize our abstractions* and eventually *to transform them*. The bifurcation question was given a new name, *the fallacy of misplaced concreteness*. *A fallacy, not a vice proper to the intellect.* The pragmatic discipline forbids treating the problem as a horizon of the human condition. *A fallacy is first and foremost an exaggeration, an exaggerated impact attributed to an undeniable success.*

The line between admirable abstraction and dangerous abstraction is drawn not by what the abstraction is but by *the impact attributed to it*. An abstraction is well-founded if it does not abstract from everything important in experience; it becomes a fallacy when the impact of its success is exaggerated into a claim about the whole.

#### The closing humor

The Interlude ends with a passage that moves from technical philosophy into something close to character study. The SMW 58 quote: *We all know those clear-cut trenchant intellects, immovably encased in a hard shell of abstractions. They hold you to their abstractions by the sheer grip of personality.*

Stengers's gloss: *In cauda comes not venenum but humor*. Whitehead was probably thinking of Bertrand Russell — but, she adds, *we may think of Richard Dawkins or Daniel Dennett, honorable men, passionately devoted to the service of humanity*. Undertaking to transform the abstractions that impose themselves on them, *and which they try to impose in the name of reason, demands a trust that will not be shaken by the cold logic of deductions, and humor that does not denounce but knows that such coldness always has the poignant will to convince as one of its ingredients.*

This is the Interlude's closing register, and it is worth marking. Stengers ends not with a polemic against bifurcation but with a gesture of *humor* directed at its most prominent contemporary partisans. The humor is not dismissive; it acknowledges *the poignant will to convince* as an ingredient in the trenchant abstractor's grip on his abstractions. To engage Dawkins or Dennett, the apparatus must trust itself enough not to be shaken by their deductions, *and* it must hold a humor that recognizes their seriousness without being captured by it. This is a delicate methodological stance and it is the one the rest of the book will carry.

#### Why this Interlude matters for the book's arc

The Interlude is the book's hinge. Part One has been doing something Whitehead specifically designed *Concept of Nature* to do — constructing a concept of nature that resists bifurcation while declining to do metaphysics. Part Two will be doing what Whitehead specifically designed *Process and Reality* to do — constructing a metaphysics that no longer takes nature and mind as the central terms, in which knowledge is no longer the ultimate, in which the apparatus expands to host enjoyment, feeling, vibration, the percipient's affective constitution.

The Interlude's job is to make this transition philosophically responsible. It does so by:

Articulating exactly what *Concept of Nature* excluded, so the reader knows what the next book is going to take on.

Showing that the contemporary attempts to explain consciousness or perception (connectionism, dynamical systems) reproduce bifurcation when treated as ultimate, demonstrating that the apparatus's discipline against ultimacy claims has lost none of its bite.

Naming the methodological commitments — pragmatic art of consequences, subjection to adventure, the indeterminacies that protect against bifurcation — that Stengers will continue to honor across the larger metaphysics.

Marking the historical and intellectual lineage (James, Bergson contrast, Darwinian implications) that situates Whitehead's evolution toward speculative metaphysics as a continuation of pragmatic discipline, not an abandonment of it.

Closing with the humor that the apparatus requires for its actual deployment in a philosophical culture saturated with trenchant abstractors.

When you start chapter 7 (*Science and the Modern World — A Strange Book*), you will know what you are entering: a different project, with different stakes, hosted by the same reader's discipline that the Interlude has now made explicit.

#### Three things to mark

The first is the *positive indeterminacy* claim. Stengers's most distinctive technical contribution in the Interlude is the argument that ingression and cogredience are *indeterminate in the positive sense* — that is, that any explanation that determined them would re-bifurcate. This is a piece of philosophical machinery worth holding onto. It is the apparatus's protective discipline against the natural temptation of contemporary science to *explain* the very things the apparatus has refused to explain. Connectionism, dynamical systems, neuroscience generally — none of them are wrong as sciences; they only become wrong when their staging is asserted as ultimate. The apparatus's indeterminacy is what blocks the assertion.

The second is *subjection to adventure, not to judgment*. This is the deepest single statement of Stengers's methodological commitment, and it is portable far beyond Whitehead. It names a way of doing philosophy in which the philosopher commits to the consequences of taking instinctive belief seriously, follows the problem where it goes, and accepts the risks of being wrong rather than retreating to the protection of pre-established standards. Most academic philosophy proceeds by judgment. Stengers's Whitehead, and Stengers herself, proceed by adventure. The difference shows up in everything from prose style to argumentative architecture to which questions get asked.

The third is the diagnostic shape of the connectionism and dynamical-systems passages. These are demonstrations of how to read a contemporary scientific program with the apparatus's discipline intact — without dismissing the science, without elevating it to ultimacy, without losing the philosophical work the apparatus is doing. The shape is: *take the staging seriously, identify what the staging requires that it does not explain, show that what it presupposes is the very thing the apparatus has been articulating*. The diagnostic shape is portable. Apply it to current claims about consciousness, mind, perception, agency, and you have Stengers's reading practice in working form.

#### Where to go from here

The natural next step is chapter 7 — *Science and the Modern World: A Strange Book* — which begins Part One's third sub-arc, treating SMW as the transition document where Whitehead is moving from concept-of-nature work into metaphysics proper. The chapter will pick up exactly the threads the Interlude has just left: how Whitehead's problem changed, what *misplaced concreteness* names, how the move to speculative metaphysics was undertaken without abandoning the pragmatic discipline.

But if you want to dwell longer in the Interlude itself — the indeterminacy claim, the connectionism passage, the *subjection to adventure* formula, the historical placement against Bergson and the Naturalists, the humor at the close — say so and we'll stay here.

Picking up from there:

#### The two demands on Whiteheadian generalities

Stengers then states the criteria under which the apparatus itself must be evaluated. *Whiteheadian generalities are themselves inseparable from a pragmatics, a pragmatics of the concepts that participate in the concept of nature, and must also be evaluated in terms of eventual success.* Two demands:

*On the one hand*, the generalities must succeed in constituting knowledge as *ultimate*, not as *central*. Nature must provide a foothold for knowledge, but the apparatus must not *legitimize any particular form of knowledge*; it must not *duplicate, by any kind of justification, the empirical fact of the eventual success of certain ways of paying attention*. The question of what attention is due in each case is *a question for which there is no conceptual shortcut*.

This is a strong methodological commitment. The apparatus does not justify science in advance; it does not provide an epistemological foundation that physics or chemistry can rely on. It says: *paying due attention will yield more in nature than first glance*, but it does not say what counts as due attention or where more will be yielded. Those questions are answered case by case, by the practices themselves, with the apparatus as a respondent rather than a tribunal.

*On the other hand*, the generalities must succeed in *situating* the mode of attention that gave rise to bifurcation in a way that *verifies that it is always possible to discover more in nature*, but *without ever discovering in it that which would enable what is known to be "explained."* The "more" is real but it is not explanatory in the bifurcating sense.

#### Did Whitehead succeed? Two technical terms

Stengers then asks the methodological question directly. *Did Whitehead succeed, from this last point of view?* She proposes to verify by addressing two technical terms — *ingression* and *cogredience* — that remain *indeterminate* at the end of *Concept of Nature*. Her thesis is that they are indeterminate *in the positive sense of the term*: any scientific explanation that targets them would *restore the bifurcation of nature*. Their indeterminacy is not a weakness of the apparatus; it is the apparatus's protective discipline.

This is the Interlude's central technical move and it deserves slowing down on. Stengers is going to argue that the apparatus's indeterminacy at certain points is *productive* — that the things the apparatus refuses to specify are exactly the things that, if specified, would re-install bifurcation. The indeterminacy is the apparatus working, not the apparatus failing.

#### The neuroscience test case

The first test is connectionist neural networks as candidate explanations of object-recognition. The framing temptation: connectionist networks "abstract" objects in the sense that they adopt specific behaviors as signals for given objects without any explicit definition of those objects. Could we say such a mechanism *explains* the abstraction of what we call an object? Would *ingression* then be replaced by a process of emergence explicable in terms of connected neural networks, with nature bifurcating because the perceived object would be *produced as such* by detection?

Stengers's response, drawing on Andy Clark's work, is precise. *Such an explanatory hypothesis features a cerebral machinery that works on itself and by itself, and a world that appears on stage only in as insignificant a way as possible, carefully stripped of all that might constitute a landmark and hence complicate the question of responsibility.*

The staging matters. The connectionist network as explainer-of-emergence requires a particular mise-en-scène: the network as the active agent, the world as a mute passive backdrop, the question of *responsibility for emergence* assigned entirely to the network. *Like the networks used by engineers, although in a different mode, the network is thus situated by what is at stake. The staging in which it plays the role of what is responsible for emergence proposes a confrontation between two abstractions: the "neuronal brain" and a reality conceived as "mute," like an illegible text or image.*

When the stage *becomes crowded* — when the brain receives an acting body, and the body is itself situated in a world whose patterns are important and some of which must be detected on pain of death — *the brain is no longer that which explains, but just as much that whose workings are explained on the basis of the activity of living beings capable of recognizing, but also of exploiting, and even creating, what matters for their activity*.

This does not mean the connectionist model loses interest. *It does lose the power to make nature bifurcate.* It confirms that when the brain is addressed *qua belonging to nature*, one can learn more from it. *What one will learn will not, however, enable us to surmount the indeterminacy affirmed by Whitehead with regard to the object's ingression, unless the kind of answer we seek is the ultimate, that determines the staging.*

This is the Interlude's most precise diagnostic moment. The connectionist explanation of recognition fails not because it is empirically wrong but because *the staging that gives it explanatory power is itself a bifurcating staging*. The model is fine; the *ultimacy claim* — that the model explains rather than describes a piece of natural activity — is what bifurcates. Ingression remains indeterminate in the apparatus's positive sense: it names what the explanatory model has to leave out in order to be a model of the brain rather than a model of the world.

#### Cogredience and dynamic attractors

The second test case is dynamic attractors as candidates to explain *cogredience* — the relation between a percipient event and what it perceives. *Cogredience alludes to what exceeds the concept of nature, that is, to metaphysics. It designates the question of what constitutes the meeting point between the two "realities" we call "nature" and "mind," respectively: the question of what constitutes an event for the person perceiving.*

The science of dynamic attractors lets us make sense of phase transitions, bifurcations, tipping points where a system changes its behavior without any need to assign a cause. *Could we not imagine that the specious present, the duration of a here and now, might be explained in these terms?*

The same diagnostic structure. The explanatory power of systems with attractors is determined by the possibility of *separating what explains* (a system governed by a limited number of variables with fixed relations) from *what is to be explained* (qualitative changes in the system's behavior). The model loses its power if the brain *is not isolated, but an integral part of what Whitehead called our "bodily life," a life that is never the same twice, that is never there "once again."* The explanatory model requires what it does not explain: *a viewpoint on bodily life* from which would follow the definition of the variables required by the landscape of dynamic attractors.

The viewpoint cannot be fixed once and for all; it must include *what it is important to pay attention to in a concrete situation*. The question of what constitutes an event, far from being solved, then constitutes *the crucial element presupposed by the definition of the variables required by the model*.

The pattern is the same as with connectionism. The dynamic-attractor model is fine as a model. It bifurcates only when its ultimacy is asserted — when the staging that lets it explain phase transitions is taken as the staging of what events *are*, rather than as a useful abstraction within an ecologically realistic situation. Cogredience remains indeterminate in the positive sense: it names what the dynamical model has to presuppose in order to operate.

#### The repopulated stage

Stengers closes this technical section with a synthesis. *In neither of the two cases I have just envisaged is there any question of designating a "mind" that makes cerebral complexity its instrument. The mind as ultimate plays no role when nature is concerned: its role is to avoid false problems that emerge as soon as the brain, described as a set of entities in relation, is rendered unilaterally responsible for what we are aware of, like mountain climbers capable of producing a hold by their own means and climbing up a smooth wall.*

When the brain becomes an integral part of *an ecologically realistic situation*, the mind is no longer the ultimate, *since the question exceeds the limits of the problem answered by the concept of nature*. We no longer have to do with generalities, or attributing responsibilities, but with *the impassioned and fascinating adventure of living beings in environments that are always already partially fashioned, in ways that matter and signify*.

The "ecologically realistic" formulation is doing important work. It is the Interlude's gesture toward what Stengers will later develop as the *ecology of practices*, and it is the seed of what cosmopolitics will become. The point is not that the apparatus *applies* to ecologically realistic situations and not to others; it is that the apparatus's discipline forbids treating any situation as not-already-ecological. The connectionist model fails when stripped of its ecology because the ecology was always already there; the dynamic-attractor model fails when stripped of bodily life because bodily life was always already there.

#### The two transgressions of Concept of Nature's limits

Then the precise statement of what *Concept of Nature* itself excludes. *Ingression has the very limited role of resisting the philosophical disaster entailed by the lack of distinction between different types of object, different types of situation, and between localization and situation.*

If, *instead of identifying that bird as a perceptual object associated with this sound, I was moved by the vibrating corporeality of its song* — if I was *moved*, that is — I would have transgressed the limits of *Concept of Nature*. If I *did not restrict myself to recognizing this face, but felt when I saw it that the discussion will be stormy or painful* — if perception became a question of how what I perceive *affects me* — I would have transgressed twice over.

The transgressions are: *the bird testifies to a world in which beings sensitive to its song exist* (the bird's living testimony to other living beings who are touched by it) and *the expression on the other person's face raises the question of emotion: not what I perceive, but how what I perceive affects myself* (the emotional dimension of perception, which the chapter-5 face passage gestured toward and the apparatus has not been able to honor).

These transgressions name what *Concept of Nature* cannot do, and what *Process and Reality* will have to be built to do. The bird-as-living-testimony exceeds the apparatus because the apparatus's three object-types do not have a category for *another being's experience as part of the world*. The emotional affect of perception exceeds the apparatus because the apparatus has been built to articulate what is perceived without articulating *the percipient's mode of being affected*. These are exactly the questions that prehension, feeling, subjective form, and the cosmology of *Process and Reality* will be constructed to address.

#### The crossing to America

Then the historical turn. *Whitehead was soon to cross the ocean, and on American soil he was to provide himself — was to dare to provide himself? — the means of becoming what, in The Concept of Nature, he had challenged himself not to be: a metaphysician.*

This is one of the few moments where Stengers steps directly into biographical narrative, and it earns its drama. The "was to dare to provide himself" parenthetical registers the magnitude of what the move involves. Becoming a metaphysician was not, for Whitehead, a step further along a path he had already begun. It was a *transformation* — a different kind of philosophical practice, with different risks, different costs, and a different audience.

There is no contradiction, Stengers insists. *The metaphysics he wanted to avoid was the one that is done without knowing it when nature is asked to explain knowledge — or without accepting what it commits us to — when "mind" is introduced to explain what does not enter into the definition of nature one has provided oneself.* When Whitehead becomes a metaphysician, *neither nature nor mind will subsist, and knowledge will no longer be the ultimate*. The point will no longer be to construct a nature to which the flower whose scent I recognize may belong, but *a world in which this enjoyment vibrates: "how nice it smells."*

The shift from nature-in-which-we-find-the-flower to world-in-which-the-enjoyment-vibrates is the shift from *Concept of Nature* to *Process and Reality*. The apparatus has to expand to host *enjoyment*, *vibration*, the percipient's affective mode of taking up its world. This is not a continuation of the same project; it is a different project, undertaken because the first project's limits have become visible.

#### Stengers's reading practice articulated

The next pages are where Stengers articulates her own methodological position most directly. *The operation of reading I have attempted certainly does not correspond to the effect that The Concept of Nature had on contemporary readers.* She has *accentuated the wholly mathematical freedom* with which Whitehead defines the problem of nature, whereas for many readers the two privileged sources — the scientific definition of objects and sense experience — were *the two normal poles of the problem*.

What she has chosen to *exhibit* is *Whitehead's gesture*: the way he decided to establish the concept of nature *as the answer to a problem*, on *the edge of the bifurcation that traps thinkers as soon as they approach it by attributing responsibilities to subject or object*. She has presented *Concept of Nature* as the *beginning of an adventure* and placed the adventure under the banner of *mathematical audacity*: not searching for some negotiated solution evaluated by plausibility or capacity to satisfy various parties, but tackling a problem *mano a mano* and going as far as the problem will demand.

The methodological commitment is then named directly: *to accept fully and without restriction what we are "instinctively willing to believe," but without trying to prolong, found, or generalize the justifications or interpretations we give to this "instinct." A subjection to adventure, not to judgment.*

This is the apparatus's signature self-description, and it is one of the most useful single sentences in the book for grasping what Stengers is doing. *Subjection to adventure, not to judgment.* Philosophy that proceeds by *judgment* sets up tribunals — this claim is acceptable, that one is not, here is the standard, here is the verdict. Philosophy that proceeds by *adventure* commits to the consequences of taking instinctive belief seriously and following where the problem leads, accepting the risks of being wrong without retreating to the protection of pre-established standards.

The example: *the rabbit staring at the spot from which a noise is coming, and the experimenter's art of consequences — "If I'm right, then I should, in such-and-such conditions, be able to..."* The rabbit and the experimenter are doing the same thing at different scales: trusting, testing, taking the risk of being wrong because the alternative is not paying attention. The apparatus celebrates this and does not try to ground it in something more secure.

#### James, Bergson, and the Darwinian inheritance

Stengers then names the intellectual debt explicitly. *The importance of William James for Whitehead's transformation into a philosopher cannot be overestimated.* The very construction of the concept of nature *accomplishes a junction, which had remained rather implicit in James, between radical empiricism and pragmatism, between nature as what we are aware of, and nature as providing a foothold.*

The contrast with Bergson is sharp. *Unlike Bergson, it is at the level of this foothold, and not of experience in its immediacy, freed from what makes us calculate, verify, and hesitate, that a relation with evolutionary thought is established.*

This matters for placing Whitehead. Bergson's evolutionary philosophy operates at the level of pure duration, the immediate experience freed from intellectual schematism. Whitehead's evolutionary thought operates at the level of *the foothold* — the achievement of practices that have stabilized because they pay off, the wager-and-verification rhythm that links the rabbit's startle response to the experimenter's hypothesis-testing. Both honor evolution; only Whitehead lets the calculating, verifying, hesitating part of life be central rather than peripheral.

*From this viewpoint, Whitehead is, after William James, one of the very rare philosophers of the twentieth century to have fully envisaged the consequences of the Darwinian evolution for the classical problems of philosophy* — that is, to have *situated questions of truth not on the side of right, legitimacy, or authenticity, but on the side of its consequences*.
