The fool's errand version of the question is: *can we use these philosophical insights to improve current KR practice?* That's a fool's errand because current KR is built on commitments the philosophy refuses. Trying to apply the philosophy to the practice produces a series of small concessions that don't add up to anything.

The non-fool version of the question is: *what would a knowledge practice look like that took these philosophical commitments seriously from the ground up?* That is a real and live question. The repo you're building is a working example of one such practice, even though it doesn't think of itself in those terms. The question is whether the working example can be articulated in a way that helps other practitioners build similar practices, or whether it has to remain implicit in the doing.

There is also a third version, which is the version I think might be most useful for you: *what does the apparatus give me, as a working knowledge-practitioner, that I would not have had without it?* This is not about modifying anyone else's practice. It is about the practice you yourself are developing, which is already working, and asking what it gains from being articulated in this vocabulary.

What I think it gains:

A way to hold open the question of what the right structure for any given material is, rather than reaching for the default schema.

A diagnostic for when a piece of structuring has bifurcated — when an organization has been imposed on material that the material's own demands have not earned.

A vocabulary for the difference between a *foothold* and an *ultimacy claim*, applicable to your own work as much as to anyone else's.

A discipline for *positive indeterminacy* that treats the unresolved as legitimately open rather than as a productivity failure.

A way to read your own knowledge structures as *practices* with their own *grain*, rather than as content to be optimized.

These are real gains, and they are gains for you, not for the wider KR community. The wider community will continue to do KR on the commitments it has. The apparatus's gift to you is the freedom to do something different in your own work, with the diagnostic vocabulary to know what it is you are doing.

#### A small concrete suggestion

If you wanted to push this further as a working experiment, the place I'd start is with your repo's `status: open` discipline. That's already the apparatus operating in your practice. Articulating *exactly what status: open commits you to*, in the apparatus's vocabulary, would give you a small but real piece of explicit philosophy of your own knowledge practice. Something like: *open status names a positive indeterminacy that protects the term from premature resolution; the term is not yet a foothold for which an articulation is due; closing it prematurely would bifurcate the open question into a determinate answer plus its remainder, and the remainder would lose the work the openness is doing.*

That's a paragraph. But it's the kind of paragraph that a knowledge-practice that wanted to be philosophically serious about itself would need to have written down somewhere. The repo's discipline currently runs on it implicitly. Making it explicit, even just for yourself, is the kind of thing that strengthens a practice without inflating its claims.

Whether you go further than that — toward something more like an articulated philosophy of your knowledge practice that could be shared — is a question of what the work is for. If the work is for you, you don't need the further articulation; the doing carries the discipline. If the work is for someone else, the articulation becomes useful. But the articulation is a different project from the work itself, and you should not do it just because the philosophy makes it possible. You should do it only if the use is real.

## User

Okay and then anything to learn from bio semiotics

## Claude

Yes, and biosemiotics is in a particularly interesting position relative to your apparatus, because it is the one contemporary research community that has been trying — explicitly, with some institutional weight, for thirty years — to do something close to what we've been discussing.

Let me try to lay out what biosemiotics is, what it gets right that the apparatus would honor, what it stops short of that the apparatus would push past, and what it offers that you don't get elsewhere.

#### What biosemiotics actually is

The field has a specific genealogy. *Jakob von Uexküll*, the early-twentieth-century Estonian-German biologist whose *Umwelt* concept (the meaningful world an organism inhabits as defined by what its sign-relations make available) is the founding move. *Thomas Sebeok*, who in the 1960s began arguing that semiotics had been wrongly restricted to human language and that signs were a feature of life as such. *Jesper Hoffmeyer*, the Danish biologist who in the 1990s gave the field its mature form with *Signs of Meaning in the Universe*. *Kalevi Kull* at the Tartu school, who has been the most institutionally active builder. *Marcello Barbieri*, who developed *biosemantics* with a slightly different emphasis on codes. *Frederik Stjernfelt*, the Danish philosopher who has done some of the best contemporary work tying biosemiotics to Peirce.

The founding move is the one I quoted from your Discord chat: *signs are not the property of human language but of life as such*. Every organism inhabits an Umwelt — a meaningful world structured by what its signs mean for it. The cell signals; the bacterium chemotaxes toward nutrients via molecular signs; the immune system reads self/non-self distinctions; the bird reads the territorial calls of conspecifics. Sign-relations are biological. Where there is life, there is semiosis.

The philosophical foundation is explicitly Peircean, though with a particular reading. The triadic sign — sign, object, interpretant — is taken as the structural unit of biological process. The bacterium's chemotactic response is interpretant; the chemical gradient is sign; the food source is object. The immune cell's recognition of antigen is interpretant; the molecular surface pattern is sign; the pathogen is object. Biosemiotics insists this is not metaphor. The triadic structure is really there, doing real work, in the actual biology.

The deepest commitment, and the one that distinguishes biosemiotics from neighboring fields like systems biology or ecology, is *the irreducibility of the semiotic relation*. You cannot reduce a sign-relation to a chain of dyadic causal couplings. The interpretant is constitutive — without something taking the molecular gradient *as* a sign of food, there is no sign-relation, only chemistry. The bacterium's response is not just an effect of the gradient; it is an *interpretation* of the gradient, in a technical Peircean sense. Drop the interpretant and you drop the sign.

#### What it gets right that the apparatus honors

Several things, and they matter.

The refusal of bifurcation between meaning-bearing beings and meaningless matter is exactly the move Stengers's apparatus would make, and biosemiotics makes it explicitly. The standard modern picture has humans (and maybe a few cognitively complex animals) as meaning-bearing while the rest of nature is meaningless mechanism. Biosemiotics rejects this binary on principle. *Meaning is graded across the natural world rather than being installed at some bright line.* The graduated distribution of semiotic capacity from cell to bird to human is the same kind of move as Whitehead's graduated distribution of subjective form from electron to organism. Different idiom, structurally identical refusal.

The triadic structure of every sign-relation is exactly the move I described in the Peirce section, applied with biological seriousness. Biosemiotics is one of the few contemporary fields that *insists* on the triadic structure operationally — not just acknowledging it philosophically but using it to organize empirical research. When biosemiotic researchers study cellular signaling, they look for the interpretant, not just the signal-and-target dyad. This is a discipline applied with consistency.

The seriousness about Umwelt as *world-belonging* rather than *behavioral environment* is the same move as Stengers's bird's-world / my-world overlap. Uexküll's tick has an Umwelt — heat, butyric acid, mammalian skin — that is *its world*, not just a list of stimuli to which it responds. Biosemioticians take this seriously enough that the field has developed Umwelt analysis as a research method. This is the closest thing in contemporary biology to honoring what Stengers calls *paying due attention to a world that demands attention*, applied to non-human organisms.

The *real generals* commitment, in Peirce's sense, is also honored. Sign-relations are real features of the world, not human projections. Codes (genetic, neural, hormonal) are real semiotic conventions, not metaphors. Habits (behavioral, developmental, ecological) are real generals, not just statistical regularities. The apparatus's commitment to forms-as-real that we developed in the Peirce discussion is right at home in biosemiotics.

The discipline against ultimacy claims for any single explanatory framework is at least partially present. Hoffmeyer in particular is good on this — biosemiotics is positioned as an *interpretive* discipline that complements rather than replaces molecular biology, evolutionary theory, ecology. It does not claim to be the ultimate explanation of life; it claims to articulate what those other explanations leave out and presuppose. This is not a perfect parallel to Stengers's apparatus's discipline, but it is much better than most contemporary fields manage.

#### What it stops short of that the apparatus would push past

The most important place where biosemiotics holds back is the *autopoietic threshold*. This is the move I described in your Discord chat: most biosemioticians treat life as the threshold below which there is no semiosis. Where there is no autopoietic system maintaining itself against entropy, there is no interpretant, no sign-relation, no meaning. Stones do not signify. Atoms do not have Umwelts. Thermostats are not semiotic systems. The strict version of this is associated with the Maturana-Varela theory of autopoiesis; the looser version simply takes "life" as the threshold without specifying autopoiesis precisely.

The apparatus would say: this threshold is doing work that is partly defensible (biosemiotics gains its naturalistic respectability by drawing the bright line) and partly bifurcating (the bright line *is* a bifurcation, just drawn at a different place). Whitehead would extend semiotic-like categorial structure further down — the electron has subjective form, the prehension is at minimum triadic. Peirce, more radically, has thirdness and continuity all the way down — the universe takes habits, mind and matter are continuous, sign-relations are not strictly biological. Biosemiotics, by accepting the autopoietic threshold, has refused the strongest version of the speculative naturalist commitment.

Whether this matters depends on what you want the apparatus for. If you are doing biology, the threshold is defensible and probably tactically necessary. If you are doing the kind of cross-domain synthesis your repo is doing, the threshold is a limit you will keep hitting. The goblin in RLHF is not a biological case; it is a case where semiotic-like structure is operating without autopoiesis, and biosemiotics in its standard form would say *not really semiosis*. The Peirce-Whitehead apparatus would say *the structural shape is the same, the categorial commitments extend, what biosemiotics protects by drawing the line is what the broader apparatus is willing to risk*.

A second place where biosemiotics holds back is *the philosophical articulation of the apparatus itself*. The field is much more developed empirically than philosophically. Most working biosemioticians are biologists who have absorbed enough Peirce to do their research with semiotic seriousness, not philosophers who have worked through the foundations with Stengers-style discipline. The result is that the field's *operating commitments* are often more sophisticated than its *articulated commitments*. A biosemiotician will use the triadic structure with great precision in analyzing immune-cell recognition and then offer a philosophical gloss that doesn't quite match the precision of the analysis.

This is not a fatal weakness. Whitehead and Peirce themselves did the philosophy; biosemiotics applied it. But it does mean that biosemiotics as a literature is less philosophically developed than the technical work it has done, and a reader coming from the apparatus side will sometimes find the field less articulate about its commitments than the work itself warrants.

A third place: *the human case*. Biosemiotics has been more careful about the cell, the bacterium, the bird than about the human. Where the field engages human meaning-making, it tends to handle it as just another case of biological semiosis without sufficient attention to what makes human semiosis *historically* and *culturally* layered in ways biological semiosis is not. There is an opportunity here, but the field has not really taken it. Stengers's *ecology of practices*, Latour's actor-network apparatus, and the broader cultural semiotic tradition (Lotman especially) handle this layered complexity better. Biosemiotics' strength is the cell and the cellular network; its weakness is the cultural and the historical, which are precisely where the goblin and your repo's main work live.

#### What it offers that you don't get elsewhere

Three things, all real.

A worked-out example of what *applying Peircean semiotics to a domain* actually looks like as research practice. This is the most useful thing biosemiotics offers a reader with your apparatus. You can read good biosemiotic work — Hoffmeyer on the genetic code, Stjernfelt on natural propositions, Kull on the semiotic threshold, Wheeler on the meaningful animal — and see Peirce being *used* rather than just discussed. The use is at the level of empirical research, with concrete findings and careful argument. This shows you what mature application of the apparatus looks like in a domain other than philosophy. It is one of the few contemporary literatures where the apparatus is operationalized at scale.

A vocabulary for *graded semiotic capacity* across the natural world that biology mostly lacks. Standard biology has *behavior*, *response*, *signaling*, *information*, but each of these is doing work that obscures the semiotic structure. Biosemiotics gives you *sign-relation*, *interpretant*, *Umwelt*, *semiosphere*, *code-duality*, *iconic / indexical / symbolic* (the Peircean sign typology applied biologically). These are tools that let you describe phenomena that the standard biological vocabulary obscures. Even if you don't accept the autopoietic threshold, the vocabulary is portable.

A specific empirical literature that is unusually compatible with what your repo is doing. Biosemiotics has produced detailed analyses of cellular signaling networks, immune system recognition, animal communication, plant signaling, ecological semiosis, the structure of the genetic code as a semiotic system, the semiotics of development. This empirical material is a *resource* for your kind of work in a way that most biological literature is not, because the empirical work has already been done with semiotic discipline. You don't have to translate from molecular biology's ungrammatical semantic statements; you can read biosemiotic work directly and use it.

#### What's worth reading

For someone with your apparatus, the most useful entry points are probably:

*Jesper Hoffmeyer*, *Signs of Meaning in the Universe* (1996, English translation 1996). The classic statement of the field for general readers, by its most philosophically gifted founder. Also his more technical *Biosemiotics: An Examination into the Signs of Life and the Life of Signs* (2008).

*Kalevi Kull, Claus Emmeche, Donald Favareau, eds.*, *Towards a Semiotic Biology: Life is the Action of Signs* (2011). The collected statement of the Tartu school, which has been the field's most institutionally consistent center.

*Frederik Stjernfelt*, *Natural Propositions: The Actuality of Peirce's Doctrine of Dicisigns* (2014). The most philosophically rigorous contemporary treatment of Peirce-as-applied-to-cognition-and-biology. This is the one I'd most strongly recommend for someone with your apparatus, because Stjernfelt is doing philosophy of the same caliber as the biosemiotic empirical work, and his Peirce is technically sharp.

*Wendy Wheeler*, *The Whole Creature: Complexity, Biosemiotics and the Evolution of Culture* (2006), and her later work. Wheeler is unusual in the field for taking the cultural-historical layer seriously, and her work is closer in temperament to what your repo does than most biosemiotic writing.

*Marcello Barbieri*, *The Organic Codes* (2003), for the codes-emphasis variant of the field. Barbieri's framework is slightly different from the mainline (more emphasis on codes, less on Peirce directly), but the work is rigorous and worth knowing.

*Donald Favareau*, *Essential Readings in Biosemiotics* (2010), as an anthology with extensive editorial framing. Favareau is the field's best historical and bibliographical guide.

A useful thing about this literature is that it is *finite*. Unlike some philosophical fields where the secondary literature is overwhelming, biosemiotics is a small enough field that you can read its central texts without it becoming a multi-year project. Hoffmeyer, Stjernfelt, Wheeler, Kull's main essays, and Favareau's anthology will give you the working knowledge of the field. This is a real advantage for the kind of cross-disciplinary engagement your repo does.

#### How biosemiotics relates to the broader project

If I had to place biosemiotics in the framework we've been building, I'd say this: *biosemiotics is what speculative naturalism looks like when it accepts the autopoietic threshold and develops itself as biology*. It is a real instance of the apparatus operating, with the cost of the threshold being the simplification it gains in exchange for naturalistic respectability and empirical tractability.

Your repo's project, in contrast, does not have to accept the threshold. Your interest in cross-domain pattern-recognition (the goblin, mathematical structure ingressing into development, the recurrence of forms across mathematical domains) is interest in cases where semiotic-like structure operates without obvious biological hosting. The Peirce-Whitehead apparatus is more permissive about this; biosemiotics in its standard form is less so.

What this means practically: you can read biosemiotics with profit, take its vocabulary, learn from its empirical achievements, honor its discipline, *and* not feel obliged to accept its boundary conditions. You are doing something that biosemiotics in its standard form would say is improper extension. The apparatus is on your side here. Biosemiotics' achievements are evidence for the apparatus's working in a particular regime, not evidence against extending the apparatus to regimes biosemiotics has chosen to exclude.

The deeper thing to learn from biosemiotics, for your purposes, is *how a small interdisciplinary community sustains a research practice over decades on philosophical commitments most adjacent fields do not share*. Biosemiotics has institutional weight (the Tartu school, the journal *Biosemiotics*, regular conferences, a generation of trained scholars) that biosemiotics-friendly fields like systems biology or ecology do not have. It has built a sustainable research community on commitments — triadic structure, real generals, semiosis-in-life — that the surrounding fields find at minimum strange. That sustainability is itself an achievement, and a lesson.

The lesson is not transferable wholesale, because the institutional conditions are not yours. But the *form* of the lesson is: a small disciplined community can develop and sustain a research practice on commitments that the wider intellectual culture refuses, *if* the practice produces real findings that other fields cannot, *and* the practitioners maintain enough articulation of their commitments to know what they are doing and why.

This is what your repo, at its best, is also doing. The reading of biosemiotics is partly to learn the field's content, but it is also partly to see another instance of *what your kind of project looks like at scale*. Biosemiotics is a working example of speculative naturalist research practice. You can read it for what it has built, and you can read it for what its existence proves about what is possible.

### Chapter 7 — Science and the Modern World, A Strange Book

This is where the book's tonality changes substantially. Part One's first arc was patient construction of the *Concept of Nature* apparatus; the Interlude was reflective; chapter 7 now opens Part One's third arc and does something none of the earlier chapters has done — it confronts a book that *resists being read* in the way the earlier book yielded to reading. The chapter's title is the warning. SMW is a *strange* book, and the strangeness is the point. Stengers will spend the chapter establishing what the strangeness is, why it matters, and what reading practice is required to meet it.

The chapter is also unusually self-revealing for Stengers. She tells us about her own *first reading*, which was a *defeat*. She tells us about the *non-reading* she retreated to after the defeat. She tells us about hearing Lewis Ford in the early 1980s and the *compositional* analysis that changed her relation to the book. And she tells us, in the chapter's most striking move, that what she is going to give us is *Deleuze with Whitehead and Whitehead with Deleuze*, two distinct and inseparable explorations.

This is methodologically important. The reading we have been getting up to now has been Stengers's *trained* reading, the reading she eventually arrived at after years of work. Chapter 7 is partly a confession of how that reading was *earned*, against what difficulty, with what helps. The autobiographical layer is not decoration; it is the chapter's argument that SMW's strangeness is real, that the standard reading practices fail on it, and that the practice you need to read it is not a generic philosophical practice but one developed under the specific pressure of *this book's* resistance.

Let me work through what's happening.

#### What the book appears to be

Chapter 7 opens with a careful demolition of the temptation to read SMW as a contribution to the history of ideas. The opening and closing statements of SMW invite this reading. The book begins with a meditation on the progress of civilization and ends with the celebration of the line of thinkers from Thales to the present as *ultimately the rulers of the world*. If you read only the bookends, you have a reasonable history-of-ideas frame: civilization has been shaped by reason, reason has been carried by individually-powerless thinkers, the long line of philosophy has had cumulative effect.

Stengers immediately registers the *suspicious reader* response. *On the way he will analyze the influence of his precursors depends the way he envisages his own influence.* When philosophers tell stories about the history of ideas, they often end up telling a story whose hidden subject is *me*. Bergson is the example Stengers gives — Bergson's history of philosophy reconnects intelligence and instinct in a way that conveniently makes Bergson himself the philosopher of the reconnection. The trap is real, and the suspicious reader is right to register it.

But Stengers does not let the suspicion stand. Whitehead's story differs from many others, *because, as he will make fully explicit in Adventures of Ideas, what interests him is not the great story of the progressive revelation of the truth of history that inevitably makes the West the thinking head of humanity*. What interests him is *an adventure of reason*, and *it is this adventure that creates its subject*.

That last clause is the chapter's first technical move. *The adventure creates its subject*. Reason is not what is gradually revealed by the history of philosophy; reason is *what gets ventured* in the course of that history, and the history is therefore not a *progress toward* reason but a *constitution of* reason as a question. The subject of inquiry comes into being through the inquiry. This will be a Stengers signature throughout the chapter and a key concept for reading SMW.

Then the contrast with conqueror-history. Alexander, Caesar, Napoleon won by *strength against strength*. The philosophical lineage Whitehead celebrates wins by *suggestion*, not by truth. This is a significant philosophical claim and worth slowing down on. *Suggestion travels along roads that do not have much to do with deduction, reasoning, logical necessity, or the principle of reality.* What started as an extravagant ideal carried by a visionary individual *bereft of social or political power* became *a "habit" or "routine," stabilized by institutions, laws, and professional regulations*. If ideals win, *it is without glory or glamour, by all the practices that are indeed invented by men but that also fashion them in return*.

The Jesus example: the saying "recognize each other as the children of God" was completely implausible in the slave-civilization in which it was uttered. The history that fashions us today, *respectful of the rights of man*, in whose name slavery can be routinely condemned, *had the Christian ideal as an ingredient, but it also constitutes a betrayal of this ideal*. The adventure continues. In each new social environment, ideals judged utopian by the new norms will express the new formulation of the problem the norms claim to have solved.

This is doing two things. First, it gives Whitehead's history-of-ideas frame a *pragmatic* structure that connects it to everything we've been working with — ideals are not transcendental forms imposed on history but *active conditions* in the long work of habit-formation. Second, it foreshadows what SMW is actually doing, which is not history but the construction of *a new ideal* — the metaphysical apparatus of the organism — that, on the same logic, will work by suggestion rather than by deduction.

#### The surprises SMW has in store

Stengers then walks through what reading SMW is actually like. The first surprise: in chapter 1 of SMW, Whitehead raises the problem of *faith in an order of nature* as the precondition for modern science. This is unusual but readable. The reader is willing to treat *faith in an order of nature* as a psychological adjuvant — *reality is determinist, or causal, or written in mathematical characters*. A vision that gives the scientist confidence to pursue.

But at the end of the chapter, Whitehead unfolds what this faith *implicitly requires*. The SMW 18 quote is the longest direct quotation in this chapter, and it deserves attention because Stengers's whole reading practice is about to be deployed on it.

*To experience this faith is to know that in being ourselves we are more than ourselves: to know that our experience, dim and fragmentary as it is, yet sounds the utmost depths of reality: to know that detached details merely in order to be themselves demand that they should find themselves in a system of things: to know that this system includes the harmony of logical rationality, and the harmony of aesthetic achievement: to know that, while the harmony of logic lies upon the universe as an iron necessity, the aesthetic harmony stands before it as a living ideal moulding the general flux in its broken progress towards finer, subtler issues.*

Stengers's gloss is precise. *The succession of cola marks the equivalence of the propositions, each of which, however, seems more stupefying than its predecessor.* Whitehead has done nothing other than *explain the faith of the scientist completely*. What appears to be poetic excess is actually careful articulation of what the faith implicitly requires. The aesthetic harmony is not added to the logical harmony; *the logical harmony required the aesthetic ideal as its precondition*. Without the aesthetic ideal, the *scattered details could never have become themselves and found their logical place*.

The strangeness, then, is not that Whitehead is being poetic. It is that Whitehead is *philosophically articulating* what scientific practice requires, and the articulation is so unfamiliar to most readers that it reads as poetic excess. *Yet unprepared readers might well conclude that the author is not only original, but endowed with a solid poetic verve.* The mismatch between what the book is doing and what readers can see it as doing is the chapter's central diagnosis.

#### The interruption pattern

The second surprise, and the one that makes SMW genuinely difficult: *Whitehead never confines himself for very long to historical narrative*. In the middle of chapter 4, when one might think the subject is the eighteenth century's practical successes, *the story suddenly stops*. The reader is told that space and time have two characteristics in common, *separative* and *prehensive*. After a few paragraphs on prehensive unity, this analysis is *qualified as deceptively simple* and is itself interrupted. Whitehead turns to Berkeley to ask what perception is. He seems to accept Berkeley's answer *except for one simple substitution*: where Berkeley speaks of *the mind*, Whitehead will speak of *a process of prehensive unification* and generalize it to all that exists.

The reader familiar with the history of philosophy is *seized by a slight dizziness*. To call upon Berkeley to resist a self-subsistent space-time is to call upon the man who said *to be is to be perceived*. One would expect notions implying a reality independent of perception to be suspended. Yet Whitehead has just transformed *being perceived* into a model on the basis of which that reality can be thought. *An original and poetic author, no doubt, but also endowed with a strange sense of humor.*

The dizziness is the point. SMW reads as if it cannot make up its mind about what kind of book it is, what tradition it is in, what level of seriousness it is committing to. The reader keeps trying to settle what the book is doing, and the book keeps refusing to settle.

Then, by chapters 10 (*Abstraction*) and 11 (*God*), *all possibility of selection collapses*. The history of ideas has yielded to *a metaphysical construction*, and a construction that *seems, moreover, to require a reference to God as the "ground" of all limitation*. The reader runs the risk of abandoning the book. *Originality, poetry, and humor, perhaps; delirium, certainly.*

#### Stengers's first defeat

This is where the chapter does something unusual. Stengers tells us that this is exactly what happened to her. *What I have just described was my first experience with Whiteheadian reading, years ago. In other words, this reading was a defeat.*

What followed was what she calls a *non-reading*. Once she came to understand that Whitehead was not a historian of ideas liable to delirious surges, but a metaphysician, she situated SMW *within a continuity* — Whitehead's own preface to *Adventures of Ideas* invites this, saying SMW, PR, and AI all express *one way of understanding the nature of things in a complementary way, supplementing each other's omissions and compressions*. Stengers accepted that these three books had the same author, that the bizarre mode of composition no longer counted, *because at any rate I knew what Whitehead meant, and what he said much more explicitly in Process and Reality*.

This is the *non-reading* that solves the problem of SMW's strangeness by *avoiding* the strangeness — by reading SMW through the lens of PR and treating SMW's apparent ruptures as artifacts to be smoothed over. She is honest that this was her practice for years, and that it is the practice of many Whitehead readers. It is what most secondary literature does. It works in the sense that you can extract a coherent Whiteheadian doctrine and discuss it; it fails in the sense that the actual book SMW is, with its actual ruptures, becomes invisible.

#### Lewis Ford and the compositional analysis

Then the moment that changed her relation to the book. *In the early 1980s*, she heard *Lewis Ford* present his compositional analysis of SMW. According to Ford, SMW *was the result of a genuine work of montage*. Some chapters come not from the Lowell Lectures (whose publication the book officially constitutes) but from other lectures (mathematics, science and religion). And — this is the crucial finding — *the two chapters on Abstraction and on God, plus the slight amplifications inserted in other chapters, should be read as if they had been written by another author*, introducing ideas that the former author *neither possessed nor foresaw*.

This is confirmed by the analysis of class notes taken by one of Whitehead's students. *Reading them, one can follow in real time a radical change that took place in April 1925, two months after the Lowell lectures.*

What Ford had discovered was that SMW is not one book but *two books interleaved*. The original Lowell Lectures were the first book — a meditation on science, civilization, and the foundations of modern thought, in a register continuous with Whitehead's earlier work. Sometime in spring 1925, Whitehead underwent what Stengers will later call a *fiat*, a decision, a transformation. He then *added* two new chapters (Abstraction, God) and inserted amplifications elsewhere. The result is the published SMW. The transformation is not narrated; it is *enacted* in the book's structure. The reader meets the pre-transformation Whitehead and the post-transformation Whitehead in the same volume, with no warning.

#### Stengers's stand and her own problem

Stengers takes a position. *I thus unequivocally take a stand in the midst of the controversy that divides Whiteheadian philosophers today, between partisans of the analysis called compositional or genetic, and partisans of the systematic analysis*, according to which a single system is, as Whitehead himself wrote, expressed in various modes by all the American books. Stengers sides with Ford.

*Yet my problem is not the same as Ford's: it is not to reconstitute the various strata that make up a book; I accept the results of his analysis. It is to bring to existence that Whitehead who, in June 1925, when he signed his preface to Science and the Modern World, knew what had just happened to him and nevertheless insisted that the reader accept this book as representing "one train of thought."*

This is the chapter's central question. *Was Whitehead hiding the truth?* Did he keep the transformation hidden because acknowledging it would have required rewriting the whole book, which he did not want to do? Or — and this is Stengers's wager — *can we not understand that we have to deal, despite everything, with one train of thought?* In which case Whitehead said nothing but the truth, *albeit not the whole truth*.

The wager is that there really is one train of thought, but it is not the systematic-doctrine train of thought. It is *the train of thought of a mathematician seeking how to formulate a problem and learning from his problem what it obliges him to*. The unity is the unity of a problem under construction, not the unity of a finished doctrine. Brutal transformations in the formulation of the problem — the spring 1925 fiat — *do not contradict* the possibility of one train of thought, because the train of thought is the work of *constructing the problem*, and that work includes its own transformations.

#### The Deleuzian apparatus enters

Stengers then makes the move that will structure the rest of the chapter. *At the same time as I was learning to read Whitehead, I was also learning to inhabit the "image of thought" Gilles Deleuze calls into existence in Difference and Repetition. From the outset, therefore, I thought Deleuze with Whitehead and Whitehead with Deleuze, two distinct and inseparable explorations.*

The Deleuzian apparatus that comes in here is specific: the technical concepts of *adjunction* and *condensation* from *Difference and Repetition*. Stengers presents these explicitly because, in her reading, Ford's compositional analysis and Deleuze's account of the structure of thought *describe the same thing*. *What Ford had discovered had already been described by Deleuze, and conversely, what Deleuze described had been experienced by Lewis Ford's Whitehead.*

The DR 255 quote: *Problems or Ideas emanate from imperatives of adventure that present themselves as questions. This is why the problems are not separable from a deciding power, a fiat, which, when it traverses us, makes us semi-divine beings.* Mathematical thought is the paradigm — *the mathematician already claims to belong to the race of the gods* — and the two fundamental operations are *adjunction* and *condensation*.

*Adjunction* takes its inspiration from the method of solving algebraic equations inaugurated by Abel and Galois. A simple algebraic equation like *3x + 5 = 14* captures the features of a problem in such a way that the problem becomes explicable *in itself* and yields its solution *(x = 3)*. But in the general case, the goal is to *complete the problem by adjoining to it elements, dimensions, expectations that do not belong to its original formulation*. The "twelfth camel" example from Stengers's introduction is the structural shape — you cannot divide eleven camels in halves, thirds, and ninths until you adjoin a twelfth from outside the original problem; once the solution is achieved, the twelfth is returned, having served as the *ideal body* whose adjunction made the problem solvable.

The ideal body is *ideal* because it designates the *idea*, the imperative of the problem to be solved, *which intervenes explicitly in the new formulation conferred upon the problem*. The mathematician *creates*; she does not merely organize the description of a situation. *Adjunction designates her as a creator, prohibiting this mathematician from separating the "fact," "the problem has found its solution," from her shadow, that of the mathematician, whose trusting intervention — the problem must have a solution — has created the path toward this solution.*

This is the chapter's most precise technical apparatus, and you can feel it doing exactly the work that everything before it has been preparing. The mathematician's *trust* that the problem must have a solution is what Stengers has been describing as *trust in nature offering a foothold*. The *creation of the path* through adjunction is what Whitehead is doing in SMW, where the spring 1925 fiat is the moment of adjunction — the moment when an ideal body is added to the original problem to make a different formulation possible.

*Condensation* takes its inspiration from Charles Péguy's *Clio*. *A difficulty one encountered suddenly passes through a point of resolution that is almost physical in the sense of critical points of physics, such as condensation, fusion, and so on. As a liquid condenses brutally, the possibility of a path of solution bursts forth, and both the thought and the way in which what was to be thought presented itself, all at once, determine one another as if in a new beginning of the world.*

The condensation is *not an illumination, nor new instantaneous evidence*. *Everything begins again, for the difficulty, having become the principle of solution, will oblige us to go through once again what seemed to be stably acquired, and this itinerary will ultimately decide what the principle demanded.*

This is the structure of *what happened to Whitehead in spring 1925*. Not a flash of insight, not new evidence, but a *condensation* — a critical-point transition in which the difficulty itself became the principle of solution, and the entire prior train of thought had to be gone through again under the new principle's pressure. SMW is the document in which we see this happening, partially and incompletely, because Whitehead added the new chapters to the old without rewriting the old chapters from the new principle's perspective.

#### The structure of the chapter's wager

Stengers is now ready to articulate her reading wager. *If my reading of Science and the Modern World does not "miss its mark," the enigmatic terms of adjunction and condensation will come alive for the reader, and if they come alive, what Whitehead tried to transmit will be transmitted by other means.*

This is a methodological claim that the rest of Part One's third arc will test. The wager is that SMW's strangeness is not a defect to be smoothed over but the *enacted form* of an adjunction-and-condensation that the reader needs to feel in order to reach what Whitehead reached. Reading SMW *as one train of thought* under Whitehead's preface's insistence, while honoring Ford's compositional analysis of the strata, requires reading it as *the document of a transformation that has not yet finished transforming itself*.

The chapter ends with a forward gesture. *Everything will begin again, but in Process and Reality. For in this case, the strata of composition will no longer concern only chapters or paragraphs, but sometimes sentences that transform the meaning of the paragraph when they are inserted. It will no longer be a matter of a strange book, but of a labyrinth-book.*

PR is the labyrinth that SMW's transformation made necessary. SMW is the strange book in which the transformation begins to become visible. Both are the work of the same train of thought — the train of thought that constructs a problem by undergoing it.

#### What this chapter does for the reading practice

A few things worth marking explicitly.

The chapter introduces the *autobiographical layer* into the reading. Up to now, Stengers has been a careful guide to the text. Now she is also a witness to *what happens when you try to read this text without the right practice*. The defeat of her first reading and the non-reading that followed it are not embarrassments; they are evidence. The book *will* defeat you on first reading. The non-reading *is* available and *will* feel adequate. The reading practice the chapter is now training you for is the one that recovers what the defeat lost and what the non-reading bracketed.

This is methodologically generous in a way most philosophical writing is not. Stengers tells you what failed for her so that you do not have to repeat the failure. She also tells you what made the difference — Lewis Ford's compositional analysis, the encounter with Deleuze, the years of work — in a way that lets you see the practice as something that develops over time rather than something you arrive at by being clever.

The chapter introduces the *Deleuzian apparatus* as a working partner of the Whiteheadian apparatus. This is significant. Up to now, the philosophical companions have been William James, Henri Bergson (mostly as contrast), and the broader tradition of pragmatism. Deleuze enters now, and he will stay. *Difference and Repetition* and *What Is Philosophy?* will be cited frequently from here on. The reading practice is now explicitly Deleuze-and-Whitehead, with Stengers as the integrator. For your repo's purposes, this is worth noticing — Stengers is one of the few thinkers who reads Whitehead and Deleuze together at this depth, and her ability to do so is part of what makes her so portable across traditions.

The chapter introduces the *fiat* concept. This is going to matter. A *fiat* is a *decision that traverses us*, in Deleuze's language — not a decision we make from a position of authority over our thinking, but a decision that *happens to* us, that transforms us as it passes through us. Whitehead's spring 1925 fiat is the paradigm. The chapter is preparing you to recognize the fiat structure when you encounter it later — in PR, where the fiat structure happens at the level of sentences; in the cosmology, where the fiat is the act of speculative construction; in your own reading, where the fiat is what happens when you accept the imperative of the problem rather than maintaining your judgment over it.

#### Three things to mark

The first is *the adventure creates its subject*. This is the chapter's deepest single sentence and one of the most useful pieces of Stengers's vocabulary. The adventure of reason creates *reason* as its subject. The work creates *the worker*. The problem creates *the thinker who can solve it*. This inverts the standard relation between subject and object of inquiry. The subject is not a pre-existing capacity that brings inquiry to bear on its object; the subject is *constituted by* the inquiry, and the constitution is what the inquiry is most fundamentally doing. This will recur throughout the rest of the book and is worth holding onto.

The second is *suggestion versus truth*. The history that interests Whitehead is one in which ideals win by *suggestion* rather than by *truth*. Suggestion travels along roads not given by deduction or logical necessity. The new ideal is implausible in its origin and becomes habit through the work of generations. This pragmatic-historical structure is the model for what *the apparatus itself* is doing — Whitehead's metaphysics is not winning by truth, it is winning by suggestion, and that is not a defect but the only way ideals win at all. Your repo's project, structurally, is one of suggestion-work in this sense.

The third is the *one train of thought* under conditions of *brutal transformation*. The chapter's central technical wager is that you can have unity at the level of train-of-thought even as you have radical discontinuity at the level of formulation. The unity is the unity of *the imperative the problem demands*; the discontinuity is the unity of *the formulations through which the imperative gets clearer*. This is portable. It is the model for how to read a working repo, a research career, a life of inquiry. The unity is not in the products; it is in the imperative.

#### Where to go from here

The natural next stop is chapter 8 (*A New Epoch*), which begins the actual reading of SMW under the chapter-7 framework. The chapter will deal with what Whitehead is constructing in SMW's first half — the historical narrative as a setup for the metaphysical construction — and will start showing the adjunction-and-condensation pattern at work in the actual text.

If you want to dwell longer in chapter 7 — the *adventure creates its subject* claim, the suggestion-versus-truth structure, the Deleuzian apparatus, the autobiographical layer, the *one train of thought* wager — say so. Otherwise, chapter 8 is where the reading practice this chapter has set up gets put to its first test.

Good. The chapter has natural sections. Let me give you the first chunk now — the opening and the historical-revolt frame — and we'll do the rest in subsequent chunks.

### Chapter 8 — A New Epoch? (Part One of several)

Chapter 8 begins applying the chapter-7 framework to SMW's actual text. Where chapter 7 told you *how* to read the strange book, chapter 8 starts *doing* the reading, walking through the historical-narrative material that occupies SMW's first half. This is the *pre-fiat* Whitehead, the Whitehead of the original Lowell Lectures, the one who had not yet undergone the spring 1925 transformation that would produce the chapters on Abstraction and God. So we are reading him on his own first-formulation terms, watching him develop the problem that the fiat would later transform.

The chapter's title, with its question mark, registers Stengers's central diagnostic. *A new epoch?* Whitehead thought he was telling the story of an epoch that was slipping into the past. Stengers will say, in this chapter, that *he was certainly wrong in this, for what he describes still constitutes the dominant mode of thought today*. The question mark holds open the gap between what Whitehead hoped and what has happened.

#### The historical revolt

The opening move of SMW that Stengers wants you to feel is its *tone*. The first lines of Whitehead's book — about Galileo suffering only an honorable detention and a mild reproof, dying peacefully in his bed, in a generation that saw the Thirty Years' War — strike a register that refuses heroism. *The history of what we call modern rationality will not be placed under the banner of heroism.*

This is more than stylistic. Modern science has been narrated, by partisans and critics alike, as a *break* — a heroic rupture with religious authority, dogmatic philosophy, ancient cosmology. Whitehead refuses the narrative. Galileo will not figure as a martyr. He will figure as *the herald of a change in mentality*. The change is not made by individual heroes; it is the slow accumulation of a new mode of attention.

The crucial SMW 3 quote: *This new tinge to modern minds is a vehement and passionate interest in the relation of general principles to irreducible and stubborn facts. All the world over and at all times there have been practical men, absorbed in irreducible and stubborn facts; all the world over and at all times there have been men of philosophic temperament who have been absorbed in the weaving of general principles. It is this union of passionate interest in the detailed facts with equal devotion to abstract generalization which forms the novelty in our present society.*

What is distinctively modern is not facts (always there) and not principles (always there) but the *passionate union of the two*. The modern mind is the mind that demands the principles answer to the facts and the facts orient toward the principles. This is the structure of *theoretico-experimental science* in the sense the apparatus has been working with — practice that wagers on a foothold and tests whether the wager is verified.

#### Diagnosis without celebration or denunciation

Stengers immediately registers what makes Whitehead's history *original*. Most narratives of the modern mind are either celebratory (the great song of progress, Man emerging from darkness) or denunciatory (prophetic imprecations against technology, calculation, reason). Whitehead's is neither. *Whitehead's goal is neither to celebrate nor to denounce.* He thought he was *living at a key moment*, telling the story of an epoch that was *slipping into the past*. Stengers tells us he was wrong about this — *what he describes still constitutes the dominant mode of thought today* — but the wrongness is productive. *This very diagnosis fashions a form of "non-factual" optimism, the possibility of breathing, laughing, separating the quite justifiable fear, inspired in us by our history, from any duplication making that history the justification for a judgment.*

This is methodologically important. The work the historical narrative is doing is not *describing what happened*. It is *creating a position from which what happened can be related to without judgment*. The narrative gives you the room to say *it may be a very bad story, but it is no more than that*. And once it is no more than a story, you are free to participate in writing what comes next rather than being captured by what came before.

This is the same move, at the historical scale, as Stengers's *subjection to adventure, not to judgment* at the philosophical scale. The diagnosis is freeing because it refuses to convert history into a tribunal.

#### The conflict that constitutes modernity

Whitehead's reading of modernity centers on a *conflict* between two figures that share a respect for facts but use that respect against each other.

The *philosopher*, paradigmatically Hume, says the link between cause and effect is arbitrary because no fact establishes it. By doing so, the philosopher *disqualifies the faith attested by scientists in an order of nature that it is their role to decipher*.

The *scientist*, paradigmatically the working physicist, ignores the philosophical disqualification. *Scientific faith has risen to the occasion and has tacitly removed the philosophic mountain.* The scientist proceeds as if there is an order of nature to be discovered, regardless of what philosophy has said about whether such an order can be epistemically grounded.

Stengers's own contemporary illustration is sharp. She quotes Stephen Weinberg dismissing Wittgenstein's claim that laws of nature are not explanations: *Such warnings leave me cold. To tell a physicist that the laws of nature are not explanations of natural phenomena is to tell a tiger in search of its prey that all flesh is grass.* The example is well-chosen. Weinberg captures perfectly what Whitehead is describing — the realist scientist who proceeds with metaphysical commitments his profession has not been able to justify and who treats philosophical critique as a distraction from the work.

So Whitehead's modernity is not the triumphant rationality of the textbook. It is *a historical revolt against medieval rationalism*, in which observable, irreducible facts became a *weapon*. Modern science *repudiates philosophy without thereby repudiating the trust in an order of the world to be deciphered*. Modern philosophy *repudiates this trust*, associating it with an uncritical past, *and bets on the individual mind* as the only thing that can be held accountable for the well-foundedness of reasons.

#### Descartes as the modern compromise

Then the structural move that organizes the rest of SMW's first half. Stengers reads SMW's framing of Descartes as the founding compromise of modern thought. *Descartes taking his stand upon his own ultimate mind, which his philosophy assures him of, and asking about its relations to the ultimate matter — exemplified, in the second Meditation by the human body and a lump of wax — which his science assumes.*

The Aaron's-rod metaphor from SMW 142–143 is doing work. Aaron's rod (Exodus 7) became a serpent and swallowed the Egyptian magicians' serpents. Whitehead's question: *which swallows which?* Mind swallowing matter, matter swallowing mind, or — as Descartes thought — *they all lived happily together*. The Cartesian compromise is to keep both, with no resolution about how they relate. The compromise has held, more or less, for three and a half centuries. It is what Whitehead's apparatus will refuse, but the chapter establishes the compromise first so the refusal can be felt.

That's a natural break point. Want me to continue with the next chunk — the *holy war against final causes*, the diagnosis of contemporary Darwinism, the *organism as generic notion*, and the move toward what Whitehead is constructing positively?

## Claude

### Chapter 8 — A New Epoch? (Part Two)

#### The holy war against final causes

The chapter now sharpens its diagnosis. The Cartesian compromise — mind and matter coexisting without resolution — was unstable, and what stabilized it for three and a half centuries was the methodological decision to expel *final causes* from the description of nature. Aristotle had four causes (material, formal, efficient, final); the modern reduction kept the first and third, treated the second as derivative, and prosecuted a *holy war* against the fourth. Final causation — the causation of ends, purposes, what-something-is-for — became the marker of pre-modern superstition. Anything that smelled of finalism in nature was to be redescribed in terms of efficient causation operating on inert matter.

Stengers registers what this cost. The expulsion of final causes was not just a technical move; it was a methodological discipline that *could not stop at the boundary of the natural*. Once you have committed to describing nature in terms that exclude final causation, you have to handle *human activity* — which is paradigmatically end-directed — within the same framework, because human activity is part of nature. The result is a series of increasingly strained attempts to redescribe finality as something else: as illusion, as projection, as a special property of minds, or — in the Darwinian solution — as the *appearance* of finality produced by selective sorting acting on undirected variation.

This is where Stengers makes the move that has been simmering since the Interlude. *In the name of this holy war against final causes, all human activities are summoned to affirm their submission to selective logic.* The intellectual and artistic capabilities of human beings, *which appear to be somewhat excessive with regard to strict survival needs in these mammals*, get explained by some contemporary spokespersons of Darwinism as the result of *sexual selection*. The implication, which Stengers names plainly, is that *the person who summons us to accept this sober truth is also in the service of the only thing that really matters: the transmission of her genes.*

The point, she insists, is not to be indignant. *Nor is it a matter of becoming indignant at the idea that one's ardor responds to a project of seduction, copulation, and recruitment of as many vehicles as possible for one's own genes. The point is simply to note the imaginative poverty, the "enfeebled thought" of those who expose themselves in such a deliberate way to the oldest argument in philosophy, the retorsive argument, which can be expressed as follows: as a test, apply to yourself the statement you just made, what your statement affirms about everybody else: turnabout is fair play.*

The retorsive argument is the diagnostic. The contemporary Darwinian who explains intellectual and artistic life by sexual selection is *himself* an intellectual making an artistic-rhetorical move. If his account is correct, his own argument is a peacock's tail — a display whose function is reproductive, not truth-bearing. This does not refute the argument; it simply notices that the argument cannot be *seriously offered*, because seriously offering an argument involves a commitment to truth that the argument's content denies.

The retorsion is not new. It is the same move Plato made against the sophists, the same move philosophy has made against every reductive program that tries to explain reason away. What is new, Stengers is suggesting, is the *imaginative poverty* of the contemporary version — the willingness of our era's most prominent reductive programs to *expose themselves so deliberately* to a critique that has been available for two and a half thousand years. The retorsion does not require sophisticated philosophy. It requires only that you turn the argument back on its speaker. And contemporary sociobiology, evolutionary psychology, and certain kinds of neurophilosophy keep producing arguments that cannot survive this turn.

This is one of Stengers's signature moves. It is not the dismissive rejection of evolutionary thought — that would be the bifurcating mirror image of the bifurcation she is diagnosing. It is the diagnosis that the *reductive use* of evolutionary thought, the use that converts selective advantage into a tribunal that judges what is real and what is illusion, repeats the same misplaced concreteness Whitehead identified almost a century ago. The Darwinism that *handles* finality without converting it into a polemic is one thing; the Darwinism that uses the abstraction of selective advantage as an explanatory ultimate is another. The first is a successful science; the second is a fallacy in the technical Whiteheadian sense — *an exaggeration, an exaggerated impact attributed to an undeniable success*.

#### Diagnosis as prognosis

Stengers then articulates what makes Whitehead's relation to his epoch different from both celebrators and denunciators. *Whitehead does not denounce the modern epoch, but speaks of it as of something that must, and will, pass. His diagnosis is also a prognosis. He identifies the weakness, the hesitation, and the confusion concealed by the successes that allow this epoch to be identified with progress, but this weakness, hesitation, and confusion are described in a way that affirms trust: the adventure continues; that is, the incoherence is temporary and "epochal."*

This is one of the chapter's most useful single passages, and worth dwelling on.

*Diagnosis as prognosis* is a precise formulation. Most philosophical critique either describes the present without committing to whether it can change (descriptive critique) or denounces the present as a perversion that must be overcome (prophetic critique). Whitehead does neither. He *diagnoses* the present's incoherences and *predicts* that they will be resolved — not because he has theological certainty, not because he has a Hegelian historical machinery, but because *the adventure continues*. The incoherences are *epochal* — they belong to this period, they will be transformed by the period's own pressures, and the work of philosophy is to participate in that transformation rather than either justify the period or denounce it.

The trust this requires is real. You have to trust that what is incoherent in the present will become workable in the future, and that your work in articulating the incoherence is part of what makes the future workable. This is *trust in the foothold-offering character of nature* extended to historical time. The same wager the rabbit makes when it startles, the same wager the experimenter makes when she designs the test, is what Whitehead is making about modernity itself.

For your repo's purposes, this is one of the most directly portable pieces of the chapter. You are doing diagnostic work on contemporary intellectual culture. The Stengers-Whitehead practice is not denunciation and not celebration. It is the patient diagnosis of where the incoherences are, in the trust that articulating them well is part of what lets the next epoch find its footing. *Diagnosis as prognosis*. The diagnosis is not the end of the work; it is what makes the work possible.

#### SMW's central thesis

Then Stengers states what she takes to be Whitehead's *great thesis* in SMW. *Science can play an important role in the epochal change called for by the adventure of ideas.* This is why, in the same book, the goal may be both to *justify, against the philosophers, the scientists' faith in an order of nature*, and *against the scientists, to place the history of modern science under the banner of what he calls "the fallacy of misplaced concreteness."*

The double move is the chapter's structural key. SMW is not anti-science; it is a defense of science against philosophy's epistemological corrosion. SMW is also not pro-science as currently practiced; it is a critique of the metaphysics smuggled into modern science under the cover of method. Whitehead is doing both at once because the position he is constructing requires both — defending the *practice* while critiquing the *self-understanding*. The scientist's faith in nature's order is *right*; the metaphysical interpretation of that order in terms of inert matter at point-locations is *misplaced concreteness*.

#### Misplaced concreteness, named

This is where the term enters the apparatus formally. *A concrete character has been "misplaced," that is, conferred upon abstractions whose success is that of modern physics.* The success of modern physics rested on a particular set of abstractions — masses at points, instants without thickness, simple location, motion as the basic kind of change — and those abstractions were so successful that they got mistaken for *concrete reality*. The error is in the direction of attribution. The abstractions are real achievements; what they describe is real; what they leave out is also real; treating them as if they were *the concrete fact rather than abstractions from it* is the fallacy.

The diagnostic shape is identical to what we worked through with the connectionism passage in the Interlude. The model is fine; the staging is the issue. The classical-mechanics abstractions are fine; the *misplaced concreteness* attributed to them is the issue. Modern physics has been doing real work, and its abstractions have been *failing* under their own pressure — the SMW 135 quote: *At any epoch the assumptions of a science are giving way, when they exhibit symptoms of the epicyclic state from which astronomy was rescued in the sixteenth century. Physical science is now exhibiting such symptoms.*

This is the chapter's setup for the move from classical physics to quantum mechanics, and Stengers uses it to do something philosophically delicate. The quantum revolution, in Whitehead's reading, is not a triumph of physics over its predecessors. It is a *symptom*. The classical abstractions are exhibiting *epicyclic phenomena* — the kind of accumulating exceptions and patches that signal that the framework's basic assumptions are giving way. Quantum mechanics in its pre-mature form is the patches; what it needs is a *Kepler*, someone who can take the new step that abandons the assumption the patches are trying to preserve.

#### Kepler as paradigm of the powerless visionary

The Kepler reference is doing real work, and worth slowing down on. *Far more than Copernicus or Galileo, Kepler enters the lineage of those powerless visionaries about whom Whitehead wrote that they have ultimately guided the world.*

Why Kepler rather than Copernicus or Galileo? Because Kepler made *the most difficult step* — *rejecting the epicycles to which all scientists before him, including Copernicus, had recourse to make mathematical description and observation converge*. Copernicus moved the sun to the center but kept the assumption of circular motion. Tycho Brahe tried to keep the earth at the center while having the planets rotate around a sun that itself rotated around the earth. Both kept circles; both kept patches. Kepler *demanded the secrets of planetary motion from observational data themselves* and was willing to give up circles when the data required ellipses.

The difficulty was not technical. It was *imaginative*. Circles had cosmological dignity; they were the perfect figure, appropriate for celestial motion. Giving up circles meant giving up the assumption that the heavens manifested a particular kind of mathematical perfection. Kepler had to be willing to find that the heavens were *less perfect* in the old sense in order to find that they were *more orderly* in a new sense.

This makes Kepler a paradigm of *what kind of thinker can carry an epochal change*. The change is not made by the most clever mathematician or the most accurate observer. It is made by the visionary who is willing to *abandon the assumption that has been protecting the patches*. Kepler's faith — *not a faith in the power of the facts in themselves, brute and obstinate, but in the possibility that a detailed examination of the facts may lead not to arbitrariness, but to harmony: the living ideal of aesthetic harmony* — is what made the abandonment possible. It is the same faith Whitehead articulated in the SMW 18 passage about *being more than ourselves, sounding the utmost depths of reality*. Kepler trusted that the order he could not yet describe was there to be found, and he trusted enough to give up the order he had been describing in order to find it.

Stengers names the implicit anticipation. Whitehead, in 1925, could be read as *anticipating a "new Kepler"* who would do for quantum physics what Kepler did for planetary motion — abandon the assumption that has been generating the patches, and find the new harmony the data are demanding. *Arthur Koestler* in *The Sleepwalkers* made this parallel explicit, taking inspiration from Whitehead. Whether the new Kepler has arrived in the century since Whitehead wrote is an open question. Stengers does not claim to know. The chapter holds open the possibility that we are still pre-Keplerian in physics, still making patches, still waiting for the visionary who can give up the right assumption.

#### The organism as generic notion

Then the chapter's most important constructive move. Stengers tells us that Kepler's parallel must be *drawn cautiously*, because Whitehead's notion of *organism* — which is what SMW is constructing — is not the same kind of thing as Kepler's mathematical theory of elliptical orbits. Kepler's theory *responds to a quite determinate field of data, those of astronomy*. The new Kepler, on the analogy, would be *a working physicist, not a visionary inspired by her faith in nature's harmony*.

But the *organism*, the central notion of SMW, is *not a scientific concept*. It is better to speak of it as *a "doctrine," "thought," or "philosophy."* The organism is *a "generic" notion, which means that it cannot designate a privileged field of ideas*. The questions articulated around the notion of organism *must concern all those who are interested in a particular aspect of the order of nature, but they must not take the place of their specific questions*.

This is doing precise work. The organism is not Whitehead's *theory* of biology, or his *theory* of physics, or any other determinate scientific theory. It is a *generic notion* — a notion that operates at a different level from any particular science, that *concerns* the work of every science without *replacing* the questions any particular science asks. The success of the *philosophy of organism* would be to have each science *situate its own specificity in a positive way*, rather than — *like the materialist theory* — to *mutilate its questions*.

#### The strategic privilege of the life sciences

One might think the biological connotation of *organism* gives biology a privilege. Stengers tells us the privilege is *strategic*, not metaphysical. *It has to do with the particular situation of the life sciences.*

The life sciences, on Whitehead's reading, are *handicapped* by scientific materialism rather than rendered fruitful by it. The materialist program — *the explanation of all change in terms of changes in external relations between beings that do not change in themselves* — works passably for ballistics, electromagnetism, classical mechanics. It does not work for life. Life involves beings that *do change in themselves* — that develop, mature, age, learn, die. The materialist framework either has to deny these changes are real or has to treat them as derivative of external-relation changes among unchanging fundamental constituents.

The contradiction sharpens with evolution. The SMW 107 passage: *The aboriginal stuff, or material, from which a materialistic philosophy starts is incapable of evolution. Evolution, on the materialistic theory, is reduced to being another word for the description of the changes in the external relations between portions of matter. There can merely be change, purposeless and unprogressive. But the whole point of the modern doctrine is the evolution of the complex organisms from antecedent states of less complex organisms. The doctrine thus cries aloud for a conception of the organism as fundamental for nature.*

This is a sharper claim than it might look. Whitehead is not saying *evolution requires organism-talk because biological language is convenient*. He is saying that the materialist framework *cannot hold* the doctrine of biological evolution within it, because evolution requires that something be *increasing in complexity* in a way that is not just rearrangement of unchanging parts. If the parts are unchanging, complexity is just *external relations among unchanging things*, and there is nothing for evolution to *evolve*. The doctrine of evolution, taken seriously, *demands* a conception of the fundamental as *itself organism-like* — as itself capable of the kind of becoming-something-richer that the organisms it composes exhibit.

Stengers extends this to the contemporary scene. The *contemporary leading lights of Darwinian theory* glorify the theory for ensuring compatibility between the *complexity of living beings* and a *physicalist theory*, which limits itself to defining such complexity as *highly improbable, with reference to the omnipotence of selective sorting*. Whitehead did not have to deal with this contemporary version, but the diagnostic shape he established still applies. *Selective explanation was doomed to substitute one and the same monotonous, "epicyclic" answer* for the question of what *evolution* is — an answer that converts evolution into nothing but improbability-tracking, an answer that loses what evolution has been said to *do*.

The retorsive shape returns here. If selective sorting is the explanatory ultimate, then the theorist's own act of producing the theory is itself a piece of selective sorting — and the question of whether the theory is *true* in any sense richer than *adaptive* cannot be asked from within the theory. The theory eats itself, and its proponents either do not notice or do not mind.

That's another natural break point. The next chunk would cover what *organism* positively means in SMW (the prehensive unification, the substitution into Berkeley), the role of the Romantic poets as witnesses against scientific materialism, and the chapter's close. Want to continue?
