### Chapter 8 — A New Epoch? (Part Three)

#### The vibratory organism

The chapter now does something unexpected. Stengers tells us that *the first appearance of a reference to the organism in Science and the Modern World does not concern biology but physics itself* — specifically, quantum theory as organized around Bohr's model of the atom.

This is structurally important and worth slowing down on. When Whitehead introduces the *organism* concept, he does not introduce it via biology. He introduces it via quantum physics. The Bohr atom, with its discrete electron orbits and its electrons that *do not have a continuous motion* but occupy *a series of discrete positions in space during successive durations in time*, is the case that prompts the introduction. And the move Whitehead makes is striking: *the permanence of the electron must be understood not as the permanence of something that remains "the same," but as the permanence of a self-repeating pattern*.

The electron *does not exist in a given moment*. *What exists takes the time required by a vibration or a succession of contrasts.* This is where Whitehead introduces, *without the least warning*, the term *vibratory organism* — and concludes, *abruptly, in a mode that is as hyperbolic as it is out of place* (the chapter officially deals with mathematics in the history of thought) with the SMW 36 declaration: *The field is now open for the introduction of some new doctrine of organism which may take the place of the materialism with which, since the seventeenth century, science has saddled philosophy. Such a displacement of scientific materialism, if it ever takes place, cannot fail to have important consequences in every field of thought.*

This is a chapter-7 trap-and-resolution rhythm operating at a higher scale. The reader thought she was reading about mathematics; she is suddenly reading about a metaphysical proposal that responds to quantum mechanics; the proposal is offered as a *displacement of scientific materialism* whose consequences extend *to every field of thought*. The local context is mathematics in the history of thought. The reach of the proposal is everything.

What makes the move philosophically careful is that Whitehead is *not* generalizing biology to physics. He is *generalizing the structural form* of organism — *self-repeating pattern, taking the time required by a vibration, becoming-as-fundamental* — to whatever exists at any scale. The *vibratory organism* of the electron is not a biological metaphor pasted onto physics. It is a structural claim that *physics, taken seriously, is already encountering organism-form at the most fundamental level*. The biological privilege Stengers warned against earlier is genuinely strategic, not metaphysical. The fundamental case is the *vibratory organism* of physics; the biological organism is one elaboration among others; the human organism is a further elaboration; all are instances of the same structural form.

#### What Whitehead wanted and what physics did

Stengers then registers, with characteristic directness, what happened next. *We need hardly point out that quantum mechanics has by no means taken the road Whitehead hoped for.* Whitehead read the Bohr atom as *announcing a new realism* — a realism that would *abandon the vision of a world described as a continuous succession of instantaneous material configurations*. The actual quantum mechanics of Heisenberg, Born, Dirac, von Neumann *preserved continuity and let go of realism*.

This is one of the more candid moments in the book. Whitehead's wager about quantum physics did not pay off in the way he expected. The development of quantum mechanics in the late 1920s and the subsequent Copenhagen interpretation took the path of *epistemological retreat* — questions about what the wave function *really is* were declared illegitimate, the formalism was treated as predictively adequate without committing to an underlying ontology, and the realism Whitehead wanted from physics was abandoned by physics itself.

*As far as I know, Whitehead never commented on this turn taken by physics.* But Stengers's diagnosis is that *everything indicates that he would have maintained his judgment of 1925 concerning the epicyclic symptoms exhibited by physics*. The Copenhagen interpretation, on this reading, is itself a kind of epicycle — a way of preserving the continuity-and-instantaneous-state framework of classical physics by sacrificing realism rather than the framework. The new Kepler, on Whitehead's terms, would still be the physicist who abandoned the framework and accepted the realism the data demand. We are, ninety-plus years on, still waiting for that physicist.

(There is a real question, which Stengers does not quite raise but which is worth marking, about whether contemporary work in quantum foundations — Bohmian mechanics, GRW, many-worlds, retrocausal approaches, the various realist programs that have proliferated in the last forty years — represents the long-delayed Keplerian move. None of these has dominant uptake, but the territory is more contested than the Copenhagen consensus of mid-century would have suggested.)

#### Organism does not unify

Stengers then closes off a misreading that the *vibratory organism / biological organism* parallel might invite. *Whitehead does not envisage any "theory" enabling the definition of both the (quantic) vibratory organism and of the living organism.* The proposition has *no ambition to provide a unifying point of view, as was successively proposed by the notions of energy, system, or information*.

This is sharp. Twentieth-century intellectual history is littered with would-be unifying frameworks: thermodynamics-as-master-discipline in the late nineteenth century, general systems theory in the mid-twentieth, cybernetics, information theory, complexity, the various flavors of computationalism. Each promised a vocabulary in which physics, biology, mind, and society could all be described under a single rubric. Each succeeded as a productive research program in particular domains. Each failed as the unification it was sometimes claimed to be.

Whitehead is not offering organism as a successor in this lineage. *The organism does not designate a possible object of knowledge, it does not constitute an answer around which the sciences would converge.* It is, instead, *what should oblige us to think about the divergence of their questions as a reflection of the "living values" that constitute the order of nature*.

The technical claim is precise. The organism is not a *unifying* concept; it is a *generic* concept that *honors divergence*. It is what makes you take seriously that physics has its question, biology has its question, the human sciences have their question, and that the divergence among these questions is *not a temporary defect to be overcome by a future unification*. The divergence is what nature has produced as its various footholds for various practices, and the *living values* of nature are what those divergent questions are tracking.

This is one of the most useful pieces of the chapter for your repo's project. The temptation in cross-domain work is to find a *common framework* that lets all the domains be read in the same vocabulary. Whitehead's organism is a counter-temptation. It says: the divergence is real, the divergence is what nature affords, the work is to *honor the divergence* while finding a generic notion that lets the divergent questions be related without being unified. This is a different kind of cross-domain work from the one most syntheses attempt, and it is the one your repo's discipline tends toward when it is working at its best.

#### The living value, the shadow

Then a Stengers signature passage, dense and worth slowing down on. *The living value, here, is none other than the affirmation that refuses to be separated from its own shadow, simultaneously affirming what it has succeeded in producing and the ideal without which this success would not have been possible.*

The metaphor of the *shadow* recurs through the book. To affirm something *with its shadow* is to affirm both *what was achieved* and *what made the achievement possible*, refusing to reify the achievement into something that stands alone. The scientist who *separates* her result from her shadow treats the result as a piece of bare knowledge that anyone could have discovered if they had only looked. The scientist who keeps her shadow with her recognizes that the result was the achievement of a particular practice, with a particular faith, in a particular ecology of attention, and that the *result is real, but it is not separable from what made it real*.

The *thought of the organism* is itself a *living value* in this sense. It is not a theory standing alone; it is the affirmation of a way of taking up the world, with all that this way presupposes and creates. *In the vocabulary of The Concept of Nature, the organism is what will prevent nature from bifurcating and will articulate what modern thought opposes: the free subject, assigning itself goals that affirm the supremacy of final causes, and the indifferent objective world, which affirms the supremacy of physical causes.*

This is the precise diagnostic. Modern thought split nature into two domains and made each domain the supremacy of one kind of causation. The organism, as a generic notion, refuses the split. Final and physical causation are not opposed; they are aspects of how organisms-at-various-scales become what they become. *It is thus not a unifying vision of the world that is proposed with the organism, but what Whitehead designates as a new mode of abstraction, capable of reconciling science and philosophy.*

#### Philosophy as the critic of abstractions

The SMW 59 quote that follows is one of Whitehead's most quotable passages and one of the chapter's most important.

*You cannot think without abstractions; accordingly, it is of the utmost importance to be vigilant in critically revising your modes of abstraction. It is here that philosophy finds its niche as essential to the healthy progress of society. It is the critic of abstractions. A civilisation which cannot burst through its current abstractions is doomed to sterility after a very limited period of progress.*

Stengers's gloss makes the role explicit. Philosophy's job is *not to provide foundations*, not to *adjudicate truth-claims*, not to *clarify language*. Philosophy's job is to be the *critic of abstractions* — the discipline that keeps watch over the abstractions a civilization has been building and identifies when they have become *traps* rather than *tools*.

The railway-engineer analogy is telling. *An active school of philosophy is quite as important for the locomotion of ideas, as is an active school of railway engineers for the locomotion of fuel.* The definition of *fuel* is inseparable from the techniques that define motors, gears, and infrastructures. *Coal, gas, and electricity only became resources thanks to the ceaselessly renewed invention of what a railway is.* According to Whitehead, *our modes of abstraction have not benefitted from such creative and critical efforts, and ideas have become unable to catalyze a "healthy progress of society."*

The diagnosis is that philosophy in the modern era has *failed at its job*. It has either become technical (analyzing language, producing theory of mind, doing meta-ethics) or it has become absorbed in critique that does not propose new abstractions to take the place of those it dissolves. The result is a civilization whose *modes of abstraction have stiffened* — whose railway engineers have stopped inventing new locomotives but whose railroads keep running on the old ones, with diminishing returns.

This is also a self-diagnosis on Stengers's part. Her own work — and *Thinking with Whitehead* is the central instance — is the kind of philosophy that takes the railway-engineer role seriously. She is not analyzing concepts; she is *reconstructing the conditions under which new modes of abstraction can be invented*. The whole apparatus we have been working with is in service to this. *Subjection to adventure, not to judgment* is what this kind of philosophy practices. *Critic of abstractions* is what this kind of philosophy is.

Your repo, by structural similarity, is doing the same kind of work. The discipline of disciplined cross-domain translation, the slow accumulation of vocabulary, the refusal of premature synthesis, the *status: open* mechanism — these are *abstraction-engineering* in Whitehead's sense. They are not the application of existing abstractions; they are the patient work of building the abstractions that will let new questions move.

#### Stengers's editorial caution

Stengers then makes a methodological note that is worth marking because it tells you something about how she is reading the chapter. *We do not know when Whitehead began to conceive of the notion of organism in relation to the question of the order of nature. What seems to me certain is that the question of "social progress," that is, also of the diagnosis given of "progress" as defined by the modern world, is considerably earlier.*

The new mode of abstraction Whitehead names *organism* must therefore be approached *twice*. *What it proposes to set in motion the ideas concerning nature, to be sure, but the proposed mode of setting in motion is inseparable from the possible setting in motion of many other things as well.*

The railway-engineer analogy is extended. *A railway engineer is active in solving problems involving motion and fuel, but he also dreams of all that can also be set in motion thanks to this solution: not only travelers, merchandise, minerals, and soldiers, but also ways of living, ways of communication, modes of distribution of the possible and the impossible.*

Whitehead is doing both at once. He is engineering a new abstraction (the organism) for the technical problem of nature, *and* he is dreaming about everything else this abstraction could set in motion if it took hold — religion, education, art, social order. *In Science and the Modern World, Whitehead has absolutely no concern for this kind of difference, but "thinking with Whitehead" does not mean authorizing oneself all the liberties that Whitehead took with his readers.*

This is Stengers exercising editorial judgment about her own reading practice. She is going to *separate* what Whitehead did not separate — the technical work on the order of nature, on the one hand, and the *dreams* that surround it, on the other — because *thinking with* Whitehead is not the same as *imitating his liberties*. She has chosen *to bring Whitehead's "dreams" to life*, treating the social and religious vision as part of the apparatus's stakes without letting it drown out the technical work.

For your repo's purposes, this is a methodological model. The disciplined cross-domain translator does not simply transcribe what the source said. She makes editorial choices about what to surface, what to keep in the background, how to honor the source's ambition without inheriting its rhetorical excesses. Stengers is doing this in front of you, openly, and the openness is part of the lesson.

#### Religion and the diagnosis of the epoch

The next section of the chapter (pp. 132–135 in Stengers's text) takes up Whitehead's diagnosis of religion in the modern epoch. The SMW 188 passage is the one Stengers quotes. The structure of religious decline that Whitehead identifies is *the undignified retreat*: religion takes up a position in a context, the context changes (often through scientific advance), religious authorities resist, the resistance fails, the position is modified, the next generation of apologists congratulates itself on the deeper insight gained, and the whole pattern repeats with diminished credibility.

Stengers updates the example. *It may amuse free thinkers that the succession of Catholic popes since Paul VI, instead of accepting the need to proceed to the retreat that everybody judges necessary and has, at any rate, come much too late as far as the marriage of priests, contraception, or abortion are concerned, hang on to these prohibitions against all comers.* The prediction is that some successor will yield. The successors *too belong to the history marked by the degeneration of religion, the undignified retreat from the positions it defined as vital*.

The diagnosis is not anti-religious. It is a diagnosis of *what religion has been doing* in the modern epoch — undergoing the epoch as if it poses no other alternatives than *defeat or resistance to change*. The SMW 189 line: *Religion will not regain its old power until it can face change in the same spirit as does science.*

#### Whitehead's agnostic constructivism

Stengers is careful here about Whitehead's own position. *The author of these lines does not define himself as a Christian, nor even as a believer.* He will *soon find himself constrained to involve God in the conceptual construction of the order of nature*, but he will *never be tempted by any confusion between this necessity and a justification of religion as such, much less of any specific religion*.

This is an important distinction. Whitehead's metaphysics will include *God* as a technical term — primordial nature, consequent nature, the role in providing initial subjective aims — but the metaphysical role is not the same as religious adherence. The God of the metaphysics is not the God of any particular tradition's worship.

The SMW 192 line is the deepest of Whitehead's claims about religion. *The power of God is the worship He inspires.* Stengers's gloss is precise: this *operates in immanence*. *That which demands assimilation has no distinct identity. Of course, Whitehead speaks of a force, but this force, which he calls mutual love, is not the force of God's love, a God who might demand our surrender. This love belongs to the very experience of worship, and the effect called surrender is not explained by a cause, but characterized by a style.*

The move, in technical terms, is that Whitehead is treating *religious vision* as a *fundamental experience of humanity* that is more basic than any particular religion that claims to express it. *No religion can be the privileged expression* of this vision, *although each one develops and collapses, from epoch to epoch, according to whether its doctrine, its rites, its commands, or its definitions do or do not evoke this vision, revive it, or inhibit it*.

Stengers calls this position *positively constructivist* and *also pragmatic*. *The way religion is described commits the person who describes it, implies a wager on what can "make a religion hold," on what the religious feeling might indeed be capable of.* This is *agnosticism* in a careful sense — not the dismissive agnosticism that refuses to have an opinion, but the constructive agnosticism that refuses to settle the question while taking the religious vision seriously enough to wager on what it can do.

She names the move *constructivism squared*. *By providing a constructivist version of religion, Whitehead does not stop with the operation of demystification with which the statement "religion is a construction" is immediately associated, but he fights for the future of this construction, for the statements that will one day inspire a new immediate assent and provide religious vision with the words and practices capable of stabilizing their collective importance, irreducible to a question of psychological comfort.*

This is, I think, one of the more interesting moments in Stengers's project, and one that distinguishes her from much contemporary continental philosophy. The standard demystifying move says *religion is a construction*, with the implication that demonstrating constructedness completes the philosophical task. Whitehead does not stop there. He says *religion is a construction, and the work is to construct it well, in the trust that the religious vision is real and can be given epochally adequate expression*. This is constructivism that *takes the construction seriously enough to wager on it*, rather than constructivism that *uses construction-recognition as a way to dismiss what was constructed*.

The SMW 192 passage that follows is one of Whitehead's most beautiful. *Religion has emerged into human experience mixed with the crudest fancies of barbaric imagination. Gradually, slowly, steadily the vision recurs in history under nobler form and with clearer expression. It is the one element in human experience which persistently shows an upward trend. It fades and then recurs. But when it renews its force, it recurs with an added richness and purity of content. The fact of the religious vision, and its history of persistent expansion, is our one ground for optimism. Apart from it, human life is a flash of occasional enjoyments lighting up a mass of pain and misery, a bagatelle of transient experience.*

Stengers notes that Whitehead writes this *in all diffidence*, and that the diffidence is not weakness but methodological honesty. He is making a *witness* claim — testifying to what he believes his epoch is capable of — without claiming foundational certainty for it. This is the same shape as everything else in the apparatus: trust in the foothold, wager on what attention will yield, refusal to convert the wager into a tribunal.

That's a good break point. The chapter still has the closing section to cover — the SMW 192 passage about the vision *which stands beyond, behind, and within*, the role of the Romantic poets as witnesses against scientific materialism, and the chapter's transition to chapter 9. Want me to continue with the final chunk?

## User

Picking back up from the living value The Shadow

## Claude

Picking up from there:

#### The living value, the shadow

Then a Stengers signature passage, dense and worth slowing down on. *The living value, here, is none other than the affirmation that refuses to be separated from its own shadow, simultaneously affirming what it has succeeded in producing and the ideal without which this success would not have been possible.*

The metaphor of the *shadow* recurs through the book. To affirm something *with its shadow* is to affirm both *what was achieved* and *what made the achievement possible*, refusing to reify the achievement into something that stands alone. The scientist who *separates* her result from her shadow treats the result as a piece of bare knowledge that anyone could have discovered if they had only looked. The scientist who keeps her shadow with her recognizes that the result was the achievement of a particular practice, with a particular faith, in a particular ecology of attention, and that the *result is real, but it is not separable from what made it real*.

The *thought of the organism* is itself a *living value* in this sense. It is not a theory standing alone; it is the affirmation of a way of taking up the world, with all that this way presupposes and creates. *In the vocabulary of The Concept of Nature, the organism is what will prevent nature from bifurcating and will articulate what modern thought opposes: the free subject, assigning itself goals that affirm the supremacy of final causes, and the indifferent objective world, which affirms the supremacy of physical causes.*

This is the precise diagnostic. Modern thought split nature into two domains and made each domain the supremacy of one kind of causation. The organism, as a generic notion, refuses the split. Final and physical causation are not opposed; they are aspects of how organisms-at-various-scales become what they become. *It is thus not a unifying vision of the world that is proposed with the organism, but what Whitehead designates as a new mode of abstraction, capable of reconciling science and philosophy.*

#### Philosophy as the critic of abstractions

The SMW 59 quote that follows is one of Whitehead's most quotable passages and one of the chapter's most important.

*You cannot think without abstractions; accordingly, it is of the utmost importance to be vigilant in critically revising your modes of abstraction. It is here that philosophy finds its niche as essential to the healthy progress of society. It is the critic of abstractions. A civilisation which cannot burst through its current abstractions is doomed to sterility after a very limited period of progress.*

Stengers's gloss makes the role explicit. Philosophy's job is *not to provide foundations*, not to *adjudicate truth-claims*, not to *clarify language*. Philosophy's job is to be the *critic of abstractions* — the discipline that keeps watch over the abstractions a civilization has been building and identifies when they have become *traps* rather than *tools*.

The railway-engineer analogy is telling. *An active school of philosophy is quite as important for the locomotion of ideas, as is an active school of railway engineers for the locomotion of fuel.* The definition of *fuel* is inseparable from the techniques that define motors, gears, and infrastructures. *Coal, gas, and electricity only became resources thanks to the ceaselessly renewed invention of what a railway is.* According to Whitehead, *our modes of abstraction have not benefitted from such creative and critical efforts, and ideas have become unable to catalyze a "healthy progress of society."*

The diagnosis is that philosophy in the modern era has *failed at its job*. It has either become technical (analyzing language, producing theory of mind, doing meta-ethics) or it has become absorbed in critique that does not propose new abstractions to take the place of those it dissolves. The result is a civilization whose *modes of abstraction have stiffened* — whose railway engineers have stopped inventing new locomotives but whose railroads keep running on the old ones, with diminishing returns.

This is also a self-diagnosis on Stengers's part. Her own work — and *Thinking with Whitehead* is the central instance — is the kind of philosophy that takes the railway-engineer role seriously. She is not analyzing concepts; she is *reconstructing the conditions under which new modes of abstraction can be invented*. The whole apparatus we have been working with is in service to this. *Subjection to adventure, not to judgment* is what this kind of philosophy practices. *Critic of abstractions* is what this kind of philosophy is.

Your repo, by structural similarity, is doing the same kind of work. The discipline of disciplined cross-domain translation, the slow accumulation of vocabulary, the refusal of premature synthesis, the *status: open* mechanism — these are *abstraction-engineering* in Whitehead's sense. They are not the application of existing abstractions; they are the patient work of building the abstractions that will let new questions move.

#### Stengers's editorial caution

Stengers then makes a methodological note that is worth marking because it tells you something about how she is reading the chapter. *We do not know when Whitehead began to conceive of the notion of organism in relation to the question of the order of nature. What seems to me certain is that the question of "social progress," that is, also of the diagnosis given of "progress" as defined by the modern world, is considerably earlier.*

The new mode of abstraction Whitehead names *organism* must therefore be approached *twice*. *What it proposes to set in motion the ideas concerning nature, to be sure, but the proposed mode of setting in motion is inseparable from the possible setting in motion of many other things as well.*

The railway-engineer analogy is extended. *A railway engineer is active in solving problems involving motion and fuel, but he also dreams of all that can also be set in motion thanks to this solution: not only travelers, merchandise, minerals, and soldiers, but also ways of living, ways of communication, modes of distribution of the possible and the impossible.*

Whitehead is doing both at once. He is engineering a new abstraction (the organism) for the technical problem of nature, *and* he is dreaming about everything else this abstraction could set in motion if it took hold — religion, education, art, social order. *In Science and the Modern World, Whitehead has absolutely no concern for this kind of difference, but "thinking with Whitehead" does not mean authorizing oneself all the liberties that Whitehead took with his readers.*

This is Stengers exercising editorial judgment about her own reading practice. She is going to *separate* what Whitehead did not separate — the technical work on the order of nature, on the one hand, and the *dreams* that surround it, on the other — because *thinking with* Whitehead is not the same as *imitating his liberties*. She has chosen *to bring Whitehead's "dreams" to life*, treating the social and religious vision as part of the apparatus's stakes without letting it drown out the technical work.

For your repo's purposes, this is a methodological model. The disciplined cross-domain translator does not simply transcribe what the source said. She makes editorial choices about what to surface, what to keep in the background, how to honor the source's ambition without inheriting its rhetorical excesses. Stengers is doing this in front of you, openly, and the openness is part of the lesson.

#### Religion and the diagnosis of the epoch

The next section of the chapter takes up Whitehead's diagnosis of religion in the modern epoch. The SMW 188 passage is the one Stengers quotes. The structure of religious decline that Whitehead identifies is *the undignified retreat*: religion takes up a position in a context, the context changes (often through scientific advance), religious authorities resist, the resistance fails, the position is modified, the next generation of apologists congratulates itself on the deeper insight gained, and the whole pattern repeats with diminished credibility.

Stengers updates the example. *It may amuse free thinkers that the succession of Catholic popes since Paul VI, instead of accepting the need to proceed to the retreat that everybody judges necessary and has, at any rate, come much too late as far as the marriage of priests, contraception, or abortion are concerned, hang on to these prohibitions against all comers.* The prediction is that some successor will yield. The successors *too belong to the history marked by the degeneration of religion, the undignified retreat from the positions it defined as vital*.

The diagnosis is not anti-religious. It is a diagnosis of *what religion has been doing* in the modern epoch — undergoing the epoch as if it poses no other alternatives than *defeat or resistance to change*. The SMW 189 line: *Religion will not regain its old power until it can face change in the same spirit as does science.*

#### Whitehead's agnostic constructivism

Stengers is careful here about Whitehead's own position. *The author of these lines does not define himself as a Christian, nor even as a believer.* He will *soon find himself constrained to involve God in the conceptual construction of the order of nature*, but he will *never be tempted by any confusion between this necessity and a justification of religion as such, much less of any specific religion*.

This is an important distinction. Whitehead's metaphysics will include *God* as a technical term — primordial nature, consequent nature, the role in providing initial subjective aims — but the metaphysical role is not the same as religious adherence. The God of the metaphysics is not the God of any particular tradition's worship.

The SMW 192 line is the deepest of Whitehead's claims about religion. *The power of God is the worship He inspires.* Stengers's gloss is precise: this *operates in immanence*. *That which demands assimilation has no distinct identity. Of course, Whitehead speaks of a force, but this force, which he calls mutual love, is not the force of God's love, a God who might demand our surrender. This love belongs to the very experience of worship, and the effect called surrender is not explained by a cause, but characterized by a style.*

The move, in technical terms, is that Whitehead is treating *religious vision* as a *fundamental experience of humanity* that is more basic than any particular religion that claims to express it. *No religion can be the privileged expression* of this vision, *although each one develops and collapses, from epoch to epoch, according to whether its doctrine, its rites, its commands, or its definitions do or do not evoke this vision, revive it, or inhibit it*.

Stengers calls this position *positively constructivist* and *also pragmatic*. *The way religion is described commits the person who describes it, implies a wager on what can "make a religion hold," on what the religious feeling might indeed be capable of.* This is *agnosticism* in a careful sense — not the dismissive agnosticism that refuses to have an opinion, but the constructive agnosticism that refuses to settle the question while taking the religious vision seriously enough to wager on what it can do.

She names the move *constructivism squared*. *By providing a constructivist version of religion, Whitehead does not stop with the operation of demystification with which the statement "religion is a construction" is immediately associated, but he fights for the future of this construction, for the statements that will one day inspire a new immediate assent and provide religious vision with the words and practices capable of stabilizing their collective importance, irreducible to a question of psychological comfort.*

This is, I think, one of the more interesting moments in Stengers's project, and one that distinguishes her from much contemporary continental philosophy. The standard demystifying move says *religion is a construction*, with the implication that demonstrating constructedness completes the philosophical task. Whitehead does not stop there. He says *religion is a construction, and the work is to construct it well, in the trust that the religious vision is real and can be given epochally adequate expression*. This is constructivism that *takes the construction seriously enough to wager on it*, rather than constructivism that *uses construction-recognition as a way to dismiss what was constructed*.

The SMW 192 passage that follows is one of Whitehead's most beautiful. *Religion has emerged into human experience mixed with the crudest fancies of barbaric imagination. Gradually, slowly, steadily the vision recurs in history under nobler form and with clearer expression. It is the one element in human experience which persistently shows an upward trend. It fades and then recurs. But when it renews its force, it recurs with an added richness and purity of content. The fact of the religious vision, and its history of persistent expansion, is our one ground for optimism. Apart from it, human life is a flash of occasional enjoyments lighting up a mass of pain and misery, a bagatelle of transient experience.*

Stengers notes that Whitehead writes this *in all diffidence*, and that the diffidence is not weakness but methodological honesty. He is making a *witness* claim — testifying to what he believes his epoch is capable of — without claiming foundational certainty for it. This is the same shape as everything else in the apparatus: trust in the foothold, wager on what attention will yield, refusal to convert the wager into a tribunal.

That's a good break point. Want me to continue with the final chunk — the *vision which stands beyond, behind, and within*, the Romantic poets as witnesses, and the chapter's transition to chapter 9?

## Claude

### Chapter 8 — A New Epoch? (Final Part)

#### The vision that stands beyond, behind, and within

The chapter pivots on Whitehead's own description of religious vision, the SMW 191-192 passage that Stengers quotes in full: *Religion is the vision of something which stands beyond, behind, and within, the passing flux of immediate things: something which is real, and yet waiting to be realised: something which is a remote possibility, and yet the greatest of present facts; something that gives meaning to all that passes, and yet eludes apprehension; something whose possession is the final good, and yet beyond all reach; something which is the ultimate ideal, and the hopeless quest.*

This is one of the great passages of Whitehead's prose, and Stengers handles it carefully. The triple preposition — *beyond, behind, and within* — is doing precise work. The vision is *beyond* the passing flux (transcendent in some sense), *behind* it (presupposed by it), and *within* it (immanent). All three at once. The series of antinomies that follows — *real and yet waiting to be realised, remote possibility and yet greatest present fact, gives meaning and yet eludes apprehension, final good and yet beyond reach, ultimate ideal and hopeless quest* — is not poetic excess. It is the careful articulation of a structure that any reduction would falsify.

Stengers's framing is important. *When he was writing Science and the Modern World, Whitehead did not yet know that this "something" would soon be situated at the very heart of a mode of abstraction aiming to "set in motion" an idea that he had not yet constituted as a resource, that of a "cosmos."* The 1925 fiat had not yet transformed the religious vision into the technical apparatus that would describe it. SMW is the *transition document*. The vision is articulated; the technical apparatus to host it is not yet built. PR will be the apparatus.

But the SMW philosophy of the organism, *for its part, has no direct incidence on the religious vision*. The organism designates the question of *the order of nature, not of what may be situated beyond it*. The incidence is *indirect, but it is clear*. What Whitehead is undertaking, *like a railway engineer*, is *to critically (re)set in motion ideas that are not themselves religious but which, beyond their contradictions, have settled into an agreement that defines as progress the denial of religion as an expression of living values*.

This is precisely positioned. The work on the order of nature is not religious work. But the modern epoch's denial of religion-as-living-values has been part of *the agreement of progress* that the apparatus is trying to disturb. Restoring the order of nature so that it can host living values is, indirectly, restoring conditions under which religion-as-living-value might recover. The relation is not direct, and Stengers will not let Whitehead's metaphysics be read as religious apologetics.

#### Capitalism and the bifurcation

Then Stengers does something striking. *There would be nothing overly catastrophic in the privatization of moral worlds, God speaking in the intimacy of each person's heart, if other names than that of God had come to stabilize what matters to human life beyond itself.*

The implication is sharp. Privatized religion would be tolerable if *other names* had stabilized the public sphere of mattering-beyond-self. They have not. The names that have stabilized the public sphere are those of *progress*, *rationalization*, *competitiveness*, *efficiency* — names that *reduce* the public sphere to instrumental calculation rather than enriching it.

Stengers brings in Weber as the implicit interlocutor. *Whitehead does not cite Max Weber, who had proposed to associate the rise of capitalism with the anguished question of salvation with which Protestants found themselves confronted, a question addressed to each individual alone and undecidable by definition. If he had done so, he would certainly not have been moved by the somber grandeur of habits of thought that make the accumulation of capital an end in itself, but would have read in it the best testimony to the barbarous imagination, featuring a despotic God and a nature that has bifurcated.*

This is one of the more politically pointed moments in Stengers's reading. Capitalism is read as the *cash-out* of the bifurcation of nature in the social field. *The world is reduced to a scene bereft of meaning in itself, in which the only thing that matters, the individual's salvation, is won or lost.* The Protestant ethic that Weber traced converts metaphysical bifurcation into economic compulsion. The same structure that empties nature of inherent meaning empties the social field of inherent meaning, leaving individual accumulation as the only available index of significance.

Whitehead, Stengers tells us, *preferred to frame the question in a different way*. *The question that worries him, as always, is that of abstraction* — specifically, *the lack of resistance characteristic of the modern epoch to the intolerant rule of abstractions that declare everything that escapes them frivolous, insignificant, or sentimental*. The diagnosis is not Marxist (capitalism as the master abstraction) and not Weberian (Protestantism as the sociological cause). It is *Whiteheadian*: the modern epoch is characterized by a *failure to resist intolerant abstractions*, and capitalism, Protestantism, scientific materialism, and the bifurcation of nature are all instances of the same failure.

#### The Charing Cross railway bridge

Then the example that does the most concrete work. The SMW 196 passage about *the marvelous beauty of the estuary of the Thames, as it curves through the city, wantonly defaced by the Charing Cross railway bridge, constructed apart from any reference to aesthetic values*.

Stengers's gloss is precise. *Here, Whitehead could be considered a precursor of ecology. For him, the ugliness of the Charing Cross railway bridge testifies to a catastrophic divorce, not from "purely aesthetic" values but from values that are aesthetic in the sense that they testify to the reverence of human beings toward their environment, and therefore to the intrinsic value they recognize in their environment.*

The aesthetic, here, is not decoration. It is *the discipline of recognizing intrinsic value in one's environment*. The Charing Cross bridge is a documentary record of what happens when an engineering culture has been trained to abstract away from aesthetic considerations as a *mark of professional seriousness*. The bridge does its job. It carries trains. It is *also* a piece of brutalism imposed on a landscape that had a beauty the engineers were proud of being able to ignore.

Stengers continues: *He might also be considered a Marxist without knowing it, for the domination of abstraction is what is presupposed and realized by the process of commodification, when all concrete production is reduced to its exchange value in a regime of generalized equivalence, and when the living labor of human beings is evaluated as "labor force."*

But she immediately resists letting Whitehead be read as either ecologist or Marxist. *For Whitehead, one should not attack one intolerant abstraction by another intolerant abstraction. When they turn the capitalist redefinition of things and of social relations, or its contempt for the environment, into the privileged key for a reading of the modern epoch, both the militant revolutionaries and the protectors of nature rely on an abstraction that is in danger of being intolerant.*

The diagnostic phrase is doing real work: *whoever says "key" says "mode of reading that is blind to its own selectivity," for the key designates its lock as the door's only relevant element*. A *key* metaphor for understanding history privileges one feature as the explanatory feature and dismisses everything else. Marxists and ecologists, however well-founded their interventions and however relevant their denunciations, *risk* having a moral horizon as limited as that of their adversaries, because they are using the same mode of abstraction — the *intolerant abstraction* that converts insight into tribunal.

This is one of the most useful pieces of Stengers's apparatus for contemporary purposes. The diagnostic shape applies to many contemporary debates. The reductive cognitive scientist treats neural mechanisms as *the key*. The reductive evolutionary psychologist treats selective advantage as *the key*. The militant decolonial theorist treats colonization as *the key*. The committed environmentalist treats ecological collapse as *the key*. Each may be right about something important. Each, when the insight becomes a key, has converted something the apparatus could honor into something it must refuse.

The discipline is to hold on to the insight without letting it become the key.

#### Habits of thought and protected environments

Stengers then articulates the chapter's most important methodological move. *What he calls the "history of thought" is a history that is above all collective and anonymous, which must be stated in terms of "habits of thought," that is, of the organizations that stabilize those habits as well, providing them with a protected environment in which they can be perpetuated even when their destructive consequences are obvious.*

The phrase *protected environment* is doing a lot of work. Habits of thought do not survive on their own merit. They survive because the social organizations around them — universities, professional bodies, journals, hiring committees, training programs — *protect them* from the pressures that would otherwise force their revision. The undignified retreat of religion that we discussed earlier is what happens to a habit when its protected environment fails. The persistence of bifurcation in scientific materialism is what happens when the protected environment is robust.

Stengers tells us this is a forward gesture. *Whoever speaks of a "stable habit implying a protected environment" refers to the general notions articulated by the "philosophy of the organism."* The organism, in its mature form, will be the apparatus that lets us think about how habits stabilize through their protected environments — how organisms-at-various-scales sustain themselves by maintaining the conditions that make their persistence possible. The diagnostic apparatus and the constructive apparatus are continuous.

The methodological consequence: *the philosophy of the organism is indeed an abstract, not a salvific doctrine. Abstraction is necessary for setting resources in motion, but it does not dictate the direction this motion must take.* The organism does not tell us *what to do*; it lets us *think about what has happened to us in a way that is not the fatality or routine refrain about the normal price to pay for progress*. The apparatus is diagnostic, not prescriptive. It will not give you ethical or political directives. It will let you see what has happened to your epoch and what your epoch is doing to you, in terms that allow other things to happen.

#### The betrayal of the visionaries' trust

Then the line that lands hardest. *In fact, what those visionaries of the origins — Galileo, Newton, Leibniz — could scarcely have dared to hope for has indeed become a daily routine in our universities. And this routine has also betrayed the trust that bore them, the trust in a global order to be deciphered in the detail of phenomena.*

The early visionaries trusted that paying attention to nature would yield more. The successors have inherited the *technique* of paying attention without inheriting the *trust*. The result is a routine of investigation that has lost contact with what made investigation worth doing. *The routine has betrayed the trust.* This is the diagnostic that the chapter's whole arc has been building toward — modern science has *succeeded* at the visionaries' techniques while *betraying* the visionaries' commitments.

The betrayal is structural, not moral. Individual scientists have not done anything wrong. The institutions of science have stabilized a habit of thought that no longer requires the trust that made the habit possible. *The protected environment* sustains the technique even after the trust has evaporated. This is what the modern epoch has accomplished, and it is what is now exhibiting epicyclic symptoms.

#### Education as the requisite for social progress

The chapter's closing turns to Whitehead's last SMW chapter, *Requisites for Social Progress*, which deals *mainly with the question of education*.

This is not arbitrary. Whitehead from 1910 to 1924 *abandoned his "gown" and devoted himself to "the town"*, taking on heavy academic and administrative responsibilities and participating in movements for educational reform. The educational concern was not a late add-on; it was part of what had been forming his philosophical project for fifteen years before SMW.

Stengers is careful about *how* Whitehead approaches education. *The way Whitehead raises the problem of education is not at all that of a professional pedagogue.* His guiding idea is that *children are lively and interested, and that the first problem in education is to nourish interest without killing life*. Education is not *reliable transmission of information*, not *training in skills that could be evaluated*, and not *the opposite slogan, the respect of differences and the autonomy of the "learner"*. It is something more demanding than any of these.

*Education mobilizes all the themes that will oblige him to think as a philosopher: coherence, interest, importance, life, values, emotions, specialization, abstraction, and above all trust.*

This list deserves slowing down. Each of these themes will become a technical term in PR or AI. *Importance*, especially, is one of the deepest concepts in Whitehead's late vocabulary. The fact that they all arise *first* in the context of education is significant. The question of how to nourish life-and-interest in a child is the same question, at a different scale, as the question of how to construct a metaphysics that does not bifurcate. The pedagogical and the metaphysical are continuous because both are about *what kind of attention can be sustained without killing what it attends to*.

This connects directly to the discussion we had earlier about the apparatus's portability — the discipline you have been training is, structurally, a pedagogical discipline. It is what an educator must develop to nourish her students' minds without indoctrinating them. It is what a researcher must develop to engage frontier material without forcing it into pre-existing categories. It is what a philosopher must develop to construct an apparatus that hosts the world rather than reducing it.

#### The Bergson contrast on habit

Stengers makes one of her sharpest distinctions in passing. *It was, moreover, on this point that Whitehead explicitly refused to follow Bergson, who proposed to identify the fascination with abstraction with a feature of human intelligence as such, or a trap into which intelligence falls almost necessarily. Whitehead was not fighting against habits, nor did he call for a future in which everyone would be a visionary.*

The Bergson contrast is precise. Bergson treats abstraction as a *necessary trap* — the way intelligence works produces abstraction-fascination almost inevitably, and what is needed is to *escape* abstraction back to the immediacy of intuition. Whitehead refuses this. *He had the kind of trust that the craft of an educator presupposes and requires* — trust *in the possibility of a transformation of habits*. Habits are not the enemy. Bad habits can be replaced by good habits. The visionary is not a higher kind of being than the habit-bearer; the visionary is what the habit-bearer becomes when her habits have been adequately re-formed.

This is one of the most encouraging moves in Stengers's framework. You do not have to escape habit to do good thinking. You have to develop *good habits of thought* that include the capacity for productive transformation when the habits' protected environment can no longer sustain them. This is precisely the discipline your repo has been building.

#### Education's diagnosis of the epoch

Stengers names the chapter's deepest claim. *For Whitehead, thinking about what social progress requires designates education as a crucial site, in which an epoch judges itself on the basis of the way it fashions those who will prolong its choices, strengths, and weaknesses.*

Education is where the epoch reveals what it values, what it can sustain, and what it cannot. *Education can create the habit of appreciating concrete facts, complete facts.* Or it can *create the opposite habit, as is the case with the education that produces professionals, the habit of yielding in the face of what is unacceptable, of adhering to what is incredible*.

The diagnostic line lands hard. *The link is obviously direct between the blind way in which thinkers who stuck to secure and definite habits of thought, that is, professionals, have subscribed to the concrete unacceptable consequences of industrial development, and the way in which other thinkers, just as "serious," have prolonged, in a routine way, the incredible theses that made nature bifurcate and reduced reality to the agitation of stupid, insensate matter.*

The Charing Cross bridge engineer and the analytic philosopher of mind are doing the same kind of professional work. Both have been trained to *yield in the face of what is unacceptable* and to *adhere to what is incredible*, in the name of the discipline's seriousness. The metaphysical bifurcation and the aesthetic violation of the Thames are aspects of the same epochal failure.

#### The factory as organism

Then the SMW 199-200 passage, one of Whitehead's most surprising and one of the most important. *When you understand all about the sun and all about the atmosphere and all about the rotation of the earth, you may still miss the radiance of the sunset. There is no substitute for the direct perception of the concrete achievement of a thing in its actuality. We want concrete fact with a high light thrown on what is relevant to its preciousness. What I mean is art and aesthetic education. "Art" in the general sense that I require is any selection by which the concrete facts are so arranged as to elicit attention to particular values which are realisable by them.*

The SMW 200 line that follows is the one that justifies the chapter's title's question mark. *A factory, with its machinery, its community of operatives, its social service to the general population, its dependence upon organising and designing genius, its potentialities as a source of wealth to the holders of its stock is an organism exhibiting a variety of vivid values. What we want to train is the habit of apprehending such an organism in its completeness.*

Stengers's gloss: *The "organism" has made its appearance, unexpectedly, in the context of a factory, its workers, its stockholders, its executives, and its environment, indicating the end of the diagnostic itinerary and the moment when it will finally become necessary to envisage what Whitehead understands by an organism.*

This is the chapter's payoff. The organism is *not* primarily biological. It is *not* primarily quantum-physical (though that was the first place we met it). It is not even primarily natural. The organism is *whatever is to be apprehended in its completeness as a structure of vivid values*. A factory is an organism. The Charing Cross bridge is an organism (a depressing one). The order of nature is an organism. The aesthetic education Whitehead is calling for is the discipline of *training the habit of apprehending such organisms in their completeness*.

The provocation lands. *Today, when "rationalization," "competitiveness," and "flexibility" reign, this emergence at the end of an itinerary may raise a smile, and this may already have been the case in his time.* The factory-as-organism reading sounds, to professionalized contemporary ears, like sentimentality. Stengers refuses to let it be that. *The enjoyment of vivid values does not imply a praise of reality.* It is the difference between *vivid* and *dead* ideas — the former *setting the students' minds in motion*, the latter being *synonyms of indoctrination and passivity*.

#### The pendulum and the clockmaker

The chapter closes with one of the most beautiful passages in Stengers's writing, and one that is unusually personal. She tells us about the person who *years ago made this difference a vivid idea for me when he forced me to set in motion what I thought I knew well, in this case the equivalence between kinetic energy and potential energy as illustrated by the perfect pendulum*.

The objection she received was that the perfect pendulum is a *dead, scholastic example, which silences what this equivalence does not say, but which is attested by the pendulum clockwork mechanisms*. The point is that the abstract physics of the pendulum is *one thing* — kinetic energy converts to potential energy and back — but *the technical detail of how a pendulum becomes a timekeeper* is *another thing*. *It is when the pendulum has fully unfolded its motion that it enters into the twofold contact, with the mechanism of the needles and with a falling weight (or a relaxing spring) that makes it a "timekeeper."*

The dynamics-textbook treatment of the pendulum *abstracts away* from what the pendulum actually does in its concrete realization as a clock. The escape mechanism, the need for the pendulum to reach the end of its ascent before doing the work of advancing the timekeeping, the multiple inventions that take advantage of *what only a pendulum coming to the end of its ascent is able to do* — all of this is the *concrete fact* that the abstraction *had to discard* to do its work. And recovering it, *making it vivid*, is what aesthetic education demands.

Stengers calls this *a genuine "ecology" of abstraction*. The abstract law of the pendulum *can elicit the attention it is due without denying the rest, in a way that creates the possibility of a mutual aesthetic appreciation between specialists of precision and adventurers of generalization*.

This is the chapter's quiet manifesto. The work is not to refuse abstraction. The work is to do an *ecology* of abstraction — to honor each abstraction's grain, to surface what each abstraction has to ignore in order to operate, and to refuse to let the abstraction's *success* become the warrant for ignoring everything its success required it to overlook.

The closing line: *The question is no longer of knowing "who is right," but of what each one of them has "done" with the vivid experience that nourished them all.*

#### What chapter 8 has done

Three things to mark from the chapter as a whole.

The first is the *organism as generic notion*. The chapter's most important conceptual move is the establishment of *organism* as a notion that is generic across scales — vibratory at the quantum scale, biological at the life scale, social at the institutional scale, factory-and-bridge at the industrial scale. The organism does not unify. It honors divergence. The reading practice it makes possible is one in which each domain's specificity is preserved while a generic vocabulary lets the domains be related without collapsing.

The second is *philosophy as critic of abstractions, in the railway-engineer sense*. Philosophy does not analyze, adjudicate, or clarify. It engineers new abstractions for the locomotion of ideas. The diagnosis of the modern epoch is that abstraction-engineering has failed; ideas have stopped moving; civilization is sustaining itself on locomotives that no longer pull. The constructive task is to invent new locomotives. This is what your repo is doing, and it is what Stengers is modeling in front of you.

The third is *education as the diagnosis of an epoch*. An epoch reveals itself in how it educates the next generation. The modern epoch produces *professionals* who are trained to *yield in the face of what is unacceptable* and to *adhere to what is incredible*. The diagnostic of the bifurcation of nature, the Charing Cross railway bridge, and the contemporary university are the same diagnostic. Re-forming education is the work of re-forming the epoch.

#### The transition to chapter 9

The chapter ends with the organism *named but not yet defined*. The diagnostic itinerary is complete. The constructive work — *what Whitehead understands by an organism* — is what chapter 9 will take up. Chapter 9 (*An Adventure of the Mind*) will start working through the technical apparatus of SMW: the prehensive unification, the substitution into Berkeley, what *organism* means as a mode of abstraction.

You have now finished Part One's third arc's first chapter, which is also the chapter that completes the *diagnostic* phase of SMW. From here, the work is constructive. The trap-and-resolution rhythm will continue, but the traps will increasingly be technical traps within the construction itself, and the resolutions will be elements of the metaphysical apparatus that PR will inherit and expand.

Where do you want to go — chapter 9 next, or dwell longer on chapter 8's themes (the factory-as-organism move, the railway-engineer figure of philosophy, the Charing Cross bridge diagnostic, the pendulum-as-ecology-of-abstraction)?

### Chapter 9 — From the Concept of Nature to the Order of Nature (Part One)

The chapter title states the transition precisely. *Concept of Nature* asked: how do we construct a concept of nature that resists bifurcation? The new question — the question SMW takes up — is: how do we construct an account of the *order* of nature, the why, the reason, the self-explanatoriness that the earlier book deliberately bracketed? This is the move from *what is given in experience* to *what experience presupposes about how things hold together*. It is the move from descriptive metaphysics to speculative metaphysics, in roughly the terms Strawson would later use, but with a different methodology — speculative not in the sense of arbitrary construction but in the sense of constructing under specific obligations.

The chapter does several distinct things. It first establishes the methodological shift from *requisites* (chapter 6's vocabulary) to *job specifications* (the new vocabulary). It then introduces *prehension* as the core technical term that will replace ingression in the new register. It then introduces *the eternal object* and the *substitution into Berkeley* that gives the apparatus its first technical articulation. Then it begins working through what *the order of nature* means as a methodological commitment.

Let me work through the opening, which is dense.

#### The organism as generic, scientific reasons preserved

The chapter opens with a careful repetition of where we landed in chapter 8. *The Whiteheadian notion of organism is not, as such, scientific, unlike the definitions that each specialized branch of knowledge might be inclined to give to what this notion comprehends.* The philosophy of the organism *constructs the abstract concepts that constitute each specialized version of the organism as "cases," exhibiting in a more or less partial way what these concepts, for their part, will designate as a "complete and concrete fact."*

This is the *generic* notion at work. The organism does not compete with biology's organism, with physics's organism, with sociology's organism. It provides the abstract concepts that let each specialized version be seen *as a case*. The specialized sciences keep their authority in their domains; the philosophy of the organism does not adjudicate their truth-claims. *Scientific reasons, as demanding determination, will still be theirs and will not be judged in terms of a more powerful philosophical reason to which they must submit.*

Stengers then sharpens the relation between Whitehead and the scientific tradition. *Whitehead does not intend to return to the past.* Modern science *was above all a historical revolt against inflexible medieval rationalism*. Galileo played *the stubborn facts* against reason, asking *how* things happen rather than *why* they happen. The philosophy of the organism *will not seek assistance from ancient reasons against Galilean abstraction*. *Quite the contrary, its job will be to confirm the trust in the facts that animates modern science.*

This is methodologically important. The organism is *not* a pre-modern recovery, *not* an Aristotelian return, *not* a vitalism. It is a continuation of the modern scientific commitment to the stubborn facts, with an enrichment of the apparatus that lets the facts be honored more completely. The *dissatisfactions* expressed by those who have protested against modern abstractions — *the poets, the religious thinkers, the idealist philosophers, the ordinary people who feel that something is missing* — must be listened to, but as *witnesses* to what the abstractions had to leave out, not as advocates for a return to pre-modern frameworks.

#### The mind-body problem as social, not conceptual

Then a sharp methodological note. *The obstacle in question is, in fact, less "conceptual" than social, as is attested by the interminable debates over the mind-body problem.* The mind-body debate proceeds by representing *body* as state of the central nervous system, decipherable in principle as one physico-chemical system, while *mind* is reduced to *qualia* (perceiving a frequency of electromagnetic radiation as red). This already presupposes that *everyone seems to be able to grasp with impunity what is designated by "body" and "mind" in order to caricature them in terms that repeat the bifurcation of nature over and over again*.

The diagnosis is that the mind-body problem is not stuck because the problem is hard. It is stuck because the *social organization of philosophy of mind* has produced caricatures of body and mind that *protect the bifurcation* against the very pressures that would force its revision. This is the same diagnostic shape as chapter 8's *protected environment* of habits of thought. The mind-body problem will not be solved by another paper; it will be transformed when the social conditions that protect the caricatures fail.

This gives us the crucial methodological move. *Whitehead's procedure should be approached while keeping this aspect of the situation in mind: trust in ideas, to be sure, but above all in the possibility of a transformation of habits.* The philosophical work is not to *prove* one position over another. Proofs in this domain do not change minds. The work is to *transform habits*, and habits transform when the imagination is given new material — when new abstractions become available that make the old abstractions look avoidable.

*This is why Whitehead will not base himself on dissatisfactions and obstacles, but will place them in the service of a strategy addressing imagination, the only eventual producer of a transformation in habits. The goal is neither to prove nor to refute, but to transform what was intended to be a proof that there is room for dissatisfaction, or a refutation based on an obstacle reputed to be insurmountable, into contributions to the "job specifications" that his conception of the order of nature will have to fulfill.*

This is one of the most important methodological passages in the chapter, and it deserves slowing down. Stengers is articulating a third position between *proof* and *refutation*. The third position is *job specification*: the dissatisfactions and obstacles that have accumulated against modern abstractions are not arguments to be wielded; they are *requirements* that any new construction must satisfy. The poet's protest against scientific reduction, the religious thinker's testimony to depth, the idealist philosopher's resistance to materialism — none of these prove anything, but all of them *constrain* what an adequate construction must be able to host.

The work of the philosopher is to *gather the constraints* into a list of *job specifications* and then to construct an apparatus that meets them. This is a different mode of philosophical work from either critique or defense.

#### From requisites to job specifications

Stengers makes the vocabulary shift explicit. *To speak of "job specifications" is to avoid speaking either of "requisites" or of "concepts."*

*Requisites* were appropriate for *Concept of Nature* because they were *immanent to the problem Whitehead had decided to raise in that book*. The requisites of the concept of nature were what the problem of avoiding bifurcation demanded once Whitehead had committed to it. They were internal to the philosophical problem.

*Job specifications* are different. *Here, it is the epoch that raises its (nonphilosophical) problems.* The dissatisfactions, the protests, the testimonies of the witnesses — these come from *outside* the philosophical problem in any narrow sense. They come from the experience of an epoch that has been organized around bifurcation and that has accumulated multiple forms of evidence that bifurcation does not work. The job specifications are *the demands that the epoch makes on any adequate construction*, regardless of whether the construction's author approves of those demands.

*The requisites will have to wait for the completion of the inquiry resulting in the "job specifications," for these requisites will reflect the way Whitehead defines, through the obligations imposed by these job specifications, what is required by the order of nature.*

This is a precise sequence. First, gather the job specifications by listening to the witnesses. Then, transform the job specifications into requisites of *the philosophical problem of the order of nature*. Then, construct the conceptual apparatus that meets the requisites. The order matters. If you skip to the conceptual construction without gathering the job specifications, you produce a metaphysics that does not host the experience the epoch has accumulated. If you stop at the job specifications without transforming them into requisites, you produce a survey of complaints rather than a constructive proposal.

For your repo's purposes, this distinction is portable. Your atelier work is *gathering job specifications* — the constraints that any adequate cross-domain articulation must satisfy. The deeper synthetic work — when it eventually happens — would be the transformation of those constraints into *requisites of a philosophical problem* and then into *concepts*. Most of your work should currently be at the first stage. Stengers is modeling, here, what it looks like to be patient with the gathering before pressing toward the construction.

#### Self-explanatory nature

Then the chapter's central technical commitment. Whitehead will demand that nature *show itself as self-explanatory*. The SMW 92-93 quote: *We have to search whether nature does not in its very being show itself as self-explanatory. By this I mean, that the sheer statement, of what things are, may contain elements explanatory of why things are. Such elements may be expected to refer to depths beyond anything which we can grasp with a clear apprehension. In a sense, all explanation must end in an ultimate arbitrariness. My demand is, that the ultimate arbitrariness of matter of fact from which our formulation starts should disclose the same general principles of reality, which we dimly discern as stretching away into regions beyond our explicit powers of discernment.*

This is a strong commitment and Stengers handles it carefully. *Where scientific materialism postulates localized entities as the ultimate reference for all explanation, the philosophy of the organism will ask that the concepts to be constructed exhibit the way nature "explains itself."*

The phrase *all in the same boat, to sink or swim together* is one of Whitehead's signature methodological commitments. It means: the categorial scheme must apply uniformly. If you appeal to one set of categories at the macroscopic level (organisms, perceptions, intentions) and another at the microscopic level (atoms, forces, fields), you have bifurcated. The same boat metaphor demands that the same categorial structure operate at all scales. If atoms are described one way and humans another way, you have given up on self-explanation; the explanation of humans will require an extra layer that atoms don't have, and that layer will be doing the bifurcating work.

But the *all in the same boat* metaphor is here being modified. The new formulation is about *the position of the category of the ultimate*. *Since the problem will henceforth be the ultimate of our explanations, what is presupposed by all our explanations, we may take arbitrariness as a name for this ultimate, but here the arbitrariness does not correspond to a limit, imposing a renunciation: it is defined by a demand.*

The arbitrariness Whitehead names is not the *limit* of explanation (where explanation ends). It is *what every explanation has to start from*. Every explanation starts from a brute fact that is not itself explained — *this is the universe, these are the things that are, this is what happens*. The brute fact at the start of explanation is its arbitrariness. Whitehead's demand is that *this arbitrariness be the same kind of arbitrariness across all explanations*.

This is methodologically important. It is not a denial that there are unknowns. It is a refusal to *privilege* certain unknowns over others. The arbitrariness of *qualia* (why is red the way it is and not some other way?) is the same kind of arbitrariness as the arbitrariness of *initial conditions* in cosmology (why was the universe set up this way and not another way?) is the same kind of arbitrariness as the arbitrariness of *natural laws* (why these laws and not others?). All explanations start from arbitrariness, and the arbitrariness is the same kind across the board.

#### The great refusal

The chapter then sharpens the diagnostic. The bifurcating moves all share a structure: when explanation reaches its limit, *some kind of unknowable is invoked* that protects the framework. Scientists invoke chance or God's will when their *why* questions hit a wall. Critical philosophers invoke the unknowable thing-in-itself when their phenomenology hits a wall. Theorists of the mind-body problem invoke the *emergence of qualia* as the unbridgeable mystery.

*The way in which a difficulty, which follows from the framing of a problem, is avoided by means of submission to the unknowable is just as unacceptable for Whitehead as the theory of "psychic additions": it corresponds to "rationality's great refusal to insist on its rights."*

This phrase — *rationality's great refusal* — is one of Whitehead's sharpest. Rationality has *rights*. Among them is the right to refuse being told that the difficulty its framing produced is the limit of intelligibility. When a problem hits an unknowable, the move is not to genuflect before the unknowable but to *interrogate the framing that produced the unknowable*. The unknowable is usually the framing's failure mode, not the world's actual mystery.

Stengers states the constructive position. *Concepts will thus ensure that rationality can affirm its rights, all its rights, but not the right to an ultimate and self-sufficient explanation.* The rights of rationality are real. The right of rationality is *not* to claim that rationality alone reaches the bottom. *Every explanation has its premises, and every premise designates the irreducible responsibility of whoever demands the explanation, this explanation and no other. The premises are part of the work.*

The explanation's premises are *part of the work* — meaning the premises are not external to the explanation, they are constituted by the work of explaining. There is no neutral ground from which to start; the choice of premises is itself the responsibility of the inquirer, and the inquirer's responsibility for those premises is part of what makes the explanation an explanation rather than a discovery.

*The right claimed by conceptual work is the right of not turning what is ultimate into a limit of thought, where it is a specialized thought that meets its limits.*

This is the chapter's first technical principle. The ultimate is not what *specialized* thought meets when it runs out. The ultimate is what *all* thought presupposes. To make the ultimate into a limit *of a specialization* is to import bifurcation back into the apparatus by giving one specialization the right to say "here is where the world ends." All in the same boat: every specialization has the same access to the same ultimate, because the ultimate is what all specializations are equally specializations *from*.

That's a natural break point. Want me to continue with the next chunk — the Berkeley substitution, the introduction of prehension as the central technical term, and the move into eternal objects?

#### Self-explanatory nature

Then the chapter's central technical commitment. Whitehead will demand that nature *show itself as self-explanatory*. The SMW 92-93 quote: *We have to search whether nature does not in its very being show itself as self-explanatory. By this I mean, that the sheer statement, of what things are, may contain elements explanatory of why things are. Such elements may be expected to refer to depths beyond anything which we can grasp with a clear apprehension. In a sense, all explanation must end in an ultimate arbitrariness. My demand is, that the ultimate arbitrariness of matter of fact from which our formulation starts should disclose the same general principles of reality, which we dimly discern as stretching away into regions beyond our explicit powers of discernment.*

This is a strong commitment and Stengers handles it carefully. *Where scientific materialism postulates localized entities as the ultimate reference for all explanation, the philosophy of the organism will ask that the concepts to be constructed exhibit the way nature "explains itself."*

The phrase *all in the same boat, to sink or swim together* is one of Whitehead's signature methodological commitments. It means: the categorial scheme must apply uniformly.
