All explanations start from arbitrariness, and the arbitrariness is the same kind across the board.

#### The great refusal

The chapter then sharpens the diagnostic. The bifurcating moves all share a structure: when explanation reaches its limit, *some kind of unknowable is invoked* that protects the framework. Scientists invoke chance or God's will when their *why* questions hit a wall. Critical philosophers invoke the unknowable thing-in-itself when their phenomenology hits a wall. Theorists of the mind-body problem invoke the *emergence of qualia* as the unbridgeable mystery.

*The way in which a difficulty, which follows from the framing of a problem, is avoided by means of submission to the unknowable is just as unacceptable for Whitehead as the theory of "psychic additions": it corresponds to "rationality's great refusal to insist on its rights."*

This phrase — *rationality's great refusal* — is one of Whitehead's sharpest. Rationality has *rights*. Among them is the right to refuse being told that the difficulty its framing produced is the limit of intelligibility. When a problem hits an unknowable, the move is not to genuflect before the unknowable but to *interrogate the framing that produced the unknowable*. The unknowable is usually the framing's failure mode, not the world's actual mystery.

Stengers states the constructive position. *Concepts will thus ensure that rationality can affirm its rights, all its rights, but not the right to an ultimate and self-sufficient explanation.* The rights of rationality are real. The right of rationality is *not* to claim that rationality alone reaches the bottom. *Every explanation has its premises, and every premise designates the irreducible responsibility of whoever demands the explanation, this explanation and no other. The premises are part of the work.*

The explanation's premises are *part of the work* — meaning the premises are not external to the explanation, they are constituted by the work of explaining. There is no neutral ground from which to start; the choice of premises is itself the responsibility of the inquirer, and the inquirer's responsibility for those premises is part of what makes the explanation an explanation rather than a discovery.

*The right claimed by conceptual work is the right of not turning what is ultimate into a limit of thought, where it is a specialized thought that meets its limits.*

This is the chapter's first technical principle. The ultimate is not what *specialized* thought meets when it runs out. The ultimate is what *all* thought presupposes. To make the ultimate into a limit *of a specialization* is to import bifurcation back into the apparatus by giving one specialization the right to say "here is where the world ends." All in the same boat: every specialization has the same access to the same ultimate, because the ultimate is what all specializations are equally specializations *from*.

#### The Berkeley substitution

Now the technical move. Stengers tells us we are about to encounter *the most extraordinary example* of how Whitehead handles his witnesses — the treatment of Bishop Berkeley.

*Whitehead is not concerned with Berkeley's doctrine, or his usual historical inheritance by Hume, then Kant.* He intends to *go back to what is, for him, Berkeley's key problem, the critique of the mode of abstraction that confers a misplaced concreteness upon simple localization.* Berkeley asked why we confuse what we perceive *here* with real things that we suppose to exist at a distance, *there*. This is, in Whitehead's reading, *Berkeley's contribution to the critique of misplaced concreteness*, even though Berkeley would not have used those terms.

The point is no longer to fight against bifurcation in the way *Concept of Nature* did. *For what is at issue now is the order of nature, and experience will have to exhibit the way it participates in this order.*

So Whitehead *registers Berkeley's testimony among his job specifications, as a protest against reducing the concrete fact of perceptive experience to the abstraction of a space in which things "in themselves" are situated, a "reality" to which we could refer while forgetting the perceiving mind*.

He will *affirm, with Berkeley, that what constitutes the realization of natural entities, castle, cloud, or planet, is the fact of being perceived* — *but on the condition of carrying out one small modification*. The point is to avoid passing, *as Berkeley did, from the idea that natural entities are "realized" in the unity of a perception to the idea that they owe their reality to that perception*. Berkeley made the slide from *realized in the unity of a perception* to *realized by the unity of a perception*, and the slide is what produced his idealism. Whitehead avoids the slide by *adding* something to Berkeley's testimony.

The SMW 69 quote, which is one of the most important single passages in the chapter and one of the most important passages in the whole book: *that the realisation is a gathering of things into the unity of a prehension; and that what is thereby realised is the prehension, and not the things. This unity of a prehension defines itself as a here and now, and the things so gathered into the grasped unity have essential reference to other places and other times. For Berkeley's mind, I substitute a process of prehensive unification.*

Read that slowly. It is the apparatus's first major technical articulation in SMW. The Berkeleyan claim *to be is to be perceived* is replaced with: *what is realized is not the things, but the prehension of the things*. The unity that perception was thought to provide is *the unity of the prehensive event*, not the unity of the things prehended. The things prehended *retain their reference to other places and other times* — they are not absorbed into the perceiver's unity, they are gathered into a prehensive event whose unity is its own and whose contents reach beyond it.

This is the move that breaks the idealism without falling into materialism. Berkeley was right that *some kind of perceiving-realizing* is constitutive of natural entities. He was wrong about *what* gets realized in the realizing. What gets realized is *the prehension*, the gathering, the event-of-taking-into-account. The things themselves remain *in their own places and their own times*; they are not made up by the perceiver; their gathering into the prehensive unity is one event among others in their continuing existence.

#### What prehension is

Stengers then unpacks the technical term. *The key term is, of course, "prehension." Prehension is a "taking into account," and this neologism indicates that Whitehead intends to free this term from all subjective or intellectual connotations, as well as from everything such a connotation implies.*

This is structurally important. *Apprehension* in ordinary English is what minds do — they apprehend something, take it into the mind, attend to it. The prefix *ap-* is doing the cognitive work. Whitehead drops the *ap-* and keeps the rest. *Prehension* is a stripped-down apprehending — taking-into-account *without* the cognitive specialization that apprehension implies.

The reason this matters is that the apparatus needs a term that *includes* cognition as a special case but does not *require* cognition. The earth's trajectory takes account of the sun. The bacterium takes account of the chemical gradient. The cell takes account of its neighbor. The human mind takes account of the world it inhabits. All of these are *prehensions* in the technical sense. None of them require the cognitive apparatus that *apprehension* would imply.

Stengers's example: *If I take into account the possibility that it may rain at the moment I step outside, and stop to get my coat, I do not by any means have the impression that I am producing myself in this operation. Prehension, in contrast, should make the operation and the production of reality coincide.* The ordinary *taking into account* presupposes that there is a *me* that is doing the taking and a *something* that is being taken. Prehension dissolves this presupposition. The prehensive event is the event in which the *prehending* and the *prehended* are simultaneously realized. There is no prior prehender that subsequently takes account of something. The taking-account is what produces the prehender as the prehender of this prehended.

This is one of the more difficult moves in the apparatus to feel correctly, and it is worth dwelling on. Most of our vocabulary about cognition presupposes a *subject that exists prior to cognition* and *acts of cognition* that the subject performs. Whitehead inverts this. The subject is *constituted* in the cognitive event, not its precondition. The cognitive event *is* the subject becoming what it is in this instance of cognition.

This is exactly the same move as *the adventure creates its subject* from chapter 8's opening, applied at the metaphysical scale. Chapter 8 said that the subject of the adventure is constituted by the adventure. Chapter 9 says that the subject of any prehensive event is constituted by that event. The principle is the same; the scale is different. Chapter 8 was about reason as the subject of the adventure of philosophy. Chapter 9 is about every prehending entity as the subject of its prehending.

#### Prehension as primary term

*Prehension is thus a primary term, as was perception for Berkeley.* It does not require explanation but must enable the *exhibition of the common feature of all situations in which something makes a difference for something else, including the least "psychological" ones.*

The earth's trajectory, Stengers tells us, *takes account of the sun*. The mathematical function with which celestial mechanics associates this trajectory *testifies to a remarkably stable mode of prehension, referring to the sun qua endowed with a mass and situated at a determinate distance*. The identification of the sun's distance and mass as variables of the earth's acceleration *explicates, in an abstract way, this aspect of the "prehensive unification" that we call acceleration*.

This is a striking move and worth feeling carefully. Newton's law of gravitation is, in this reading, an *abstract description of a specific mode of prehension*. The earth's prehending of the sun has a definite mathematical character — the sun's mass and distance enter as variables in the earth's acceleration. Newton's equations *capture this prehension* abstractly. They do not *explain why* the earth prehends the sun this way; they *describe* the form of the prehension once you assume it occurs. The fact that the earth prehends the sun at all is, in the apparatus, a more basic fact than the equations that describe the prehension's mathematical form.

This is the methodological key. *Scientific laws are descriptions of prehensions.* They are not *descriptions of brute regularities* that the world exhibits. They are abstract characterizations of *how things take each other into account*. The reciprocal-presupposition shape we worked through in chapter 4 (no events without ingression of objects, no objects without events that carry their ingression) gets a new home here: no laws without the prehensions they describe, no prehensions without something taken into account, with the law and the prehension and the things being mutually constitutive of the prehensive event.

The methodological consequence Stengers names: *every connection that has been recognized as an "explanation" can find a new vocation: that of designating in an abstract way a stable mode of prehension*.

This is a major reframing of what *scientific explanation* is. It is no longer the discovery of how brute matter behaves; it is the abstract characterization of how things take each other into account, with the abstract characterization being one *aspect* of a fuller fact that includes everything the abstraction had to leave out. Every successful science has produced abstractions that *describe modes of prehension*. The success of the abstractions is real. The abstractions are *not* the full description; they are characterizations of *one aspect* of the prehensive events the world exhibits.

#### Modes of abstraction

The chapter then makes the connection to chapter 8's *modes of abstraction* explicit. *We are now in a position to understand what Whitehead called for in Science and the Modern World: a change in our modes of abstraction.*

The classical mode of abstraction takes brute regularities — masses at points, charges with charges, particles in trajectories — as the basic facts, and treats *the rest* (perceptions, qualities, meanings, values) as either reducible to the basic facts or as illegitimate additions imposed by minds. The new mode of abstraction takes *prehensions* as the basic facts, and treats both the *physical regularities* and the *qualitative experiences* as different aspects of prehensive events. Both are real; neither is more basic than the other; the abstractions of physics and the abstractions of psychology are *different cuts* through the same prehensive reality.

Stengers's version: *We are no longer dealing with a so-called objective world, in which our experience is a mere addition of psychic life. We are dealing with a world that is itself prehensive, and we ourselves prehend by participating in a process that has its forms at all scales.*

This is the apparatus's first articulated commitment to *panexperientialism* in the technical sense — the view that prehensive structure operates at all scales, with electron events, molecular events, biological events, and cognitive events all being instances of the same categorial structure. The differences are differences of *grade* and *complexity*, not differences of *kind*. The electron's prehension of its electromagnetic environment is not consciousness in the human sense; it is the same categorial structure of taking-into-account that consciousness is also an instance of, with consciousness being the much-elaborated specialized case.

This is also where the goblin discussion from earlier in our conversation finds its home. The goblin's mode of ingression — distributed across an indefinite neighborhood, ingredient throughout the events its presence modifies — is a particular kind of prehensive event. The training process is a prehensive event in which a reward signal becomes prehended as a lure, and the model's outputs become prehensions of the lure with the goblin as the form realized. The apparatus extends to cases Whitehead never imagined because the apparatus is not about humans-and-things; it is about the categorial structure of *taking-into-account* wherever it occurs.

That's another natural break point. The chapter still has substantial material — the introduction of *eternal objects*, the relation between events and eternal objects in the SMW vocabulary, the move to *the order of nature* proper, and the closing material on the SMW apparatus's limits. Want me to continue with the next chunk?

### Chapter 9 — Part Two

#### Perception as cognition of prehension

The chapter develops the apparatus through a series of careful technical moves. The SMW 71 quote is first: *Perception is simply the cognition of prehensive unification; or more shortly, perception is cognition of prehension. The actual world is a manifold of prehensions: and a "prehension" is a "prehensive occasion," and a prehensive occasion is the most concrete finite entity, conceived as what it is in itself and for itself, and not as from its aspect in the essence of another such occasion.*

This passage reads as *a veritable eruption of metaphysics*, in Stengers's phrase, and you can feel the temptation to read it as if Whitehead were doing meditative seventeenth-century philosophy. Stengers blocks the reading. *Whitehead is not doing history of philosophy. He treats Berkeley, and soon Leibniz and Spinoza, as a mathematician treats his colleagues.* The mathematician engaging with predecessors does not *defer* to them as authorities; she reads their work for what can be *respected* and what must be *generalized in another way*. Berkeley's testimony is respected; Berkeley's conclusions are generalized differently.

The technical move now becomes crystalline. The actual world is *a manifold of prehensions*. Each prehension is *a prehensive occasion*, the *most concrete finite entity*, conceived as what it is *in itself and for itself*. This last phrase is doing weight. *In itself* means as it is, in its own becoming. *For itself* means in its own act of being-for-itself, with subjective character of its own. Whitehead is committing here to *every prehensive occasion having something like a perspective* — a here-and-now from which the prehensive unification occurs.

But the *for itself* does *not* mean *for a separate cognitive subject*. It is *internal* to the prehensive occasion. The earth's prehension of the sun is a prehensive occasion *for itself* in the technical sense — the prehending that occurs has a unity that is its own, with its own here-and-now. This is not consciousness. It is the categorial structure that consciousness, at the human grade of complexity, is also an instance of.

#### Aspects, perspectives, and Spinoza's modes

The next SMW 69-70 passage is one of the most important in the chapter. It introduces the technical vocabulary that will carry the apparatus forward.

*The things which are grasped into a realised unity, here and now, are not the castle, cloud, and the planet simply in themselves; but they are the castle, the cloud, and the planet from the standpoint, in space and time, of the prehensive unification. In other words, it is the perspective of the castle over there from the standpoint of the unification here. It is, therefore, aspects of the castle, the cloud, and the planet which are grasped into unity here.*

Slow down on the *aspects*. What gets gathered into a prehensive unity is not the *whole castle* but the *castle from the standpoint of the prehending event*. The castle has multiple aspects; this prehending captures one aspect — the perspective of *the castle over there* from *here*. Other prehensions, from other standpoints, capture other aspects. The castle, in its full reality, is what gets *aspected* by all the prehensions that take it into account.

This is Whitehead borrowing Leibniz's monadology, with a critical modification. Leibniz had the universe mirrored in each monad — every monad reflects the entire universe from its perspective, and the totality of perspectives is the world. Whitehead *tones down* the monads into *unified events in space and time*. Whitehead's events are not eternal monads with all-perspectives-at-once. They are temporally located prehensive occasions, each with its definite here-and-now, each capturing aspects of the things it gathers.

Then the move to Spinoza, which is more important than it might first appear. *In some ways, there is greater analogy with Spinoza's modes; that is why I use the terms mode and modal.* Whitehead is drawing on Spinoza's distinction between *substance* and *modes*. Spinoza had one substance with infinite modes; Whitehead substitutes for Spinoza's one substance *the one underlying activity of realisation individualising itself in an interlocked plurality of modes*.

The key phrase: *the one underlying activity of realisation individualising itself*. This is the apparatus's deepest commitment in SMW. There is *one activity* — call it *realisation*, *creativity*, *underlying substrate-activity*. This activity is not a thing; it is a *process*. It *individualises itself* into an *interlocked plurality of modes*. Each mode is an individual prehensive event. The events are *individualisations* of the substrate activity, not *substantial individuals* that pre-exist the activity and then participate in it.

*Concrete fact is process. Its primary analysis is into underlying activity of prehension, and into realised prehensive events. Each event is an individual matter of fact issuing from an individualisation of the substrate activity. But individualisation does not mean substantial independence.*

That last sentence is the technical kernel. The events are individuals — each is itself, distinct from others. But individualisation does *not* mean substantial independence. The events do not exist as independent atoms that subsequently relate. They exist as *the relating itself*, as the substrate activity individualising itself. This is the metaphysical move that breaks the substance-and-attribute framework that has organized Western philosophy since Aristotle. The fundamental category is not *substance* but *activity*; the individuals are not *substances* but *individualisations of activity*; the relations are not external connections among substances but *modes of the activity's individualisation*.

#### Wishful thinking or betting on a comet

Stengers is honest about what this is at this stage in SMW. *It is hard, and no doubt futile, to know whether Whitehead thought he possessed the essential part of the solution at the time he wrote these lines.*

The proponents of *systematic reading* — those who think SMW, PR, and AI all express one philosophy — *can affirm that everything is already here, summarized in brief form*. The individual prehensive event has the features Whitehead later judges essential to any realism: *memory of the past, immediacy of realisation, and indication of things to come*. The event reflects the unifications of contemporaries, predecessors, and the modes of unification by which present determines future.

But, Stengers writes, *one can just as well speak of "wishful thinking" or "betting on a comet"*. The notion of *substrate activity that is individualised* is *a black box, a placeholder for a solution*. The terms are doing programmatic work — they declare what is refused (substantial independence), they introduce an initial *specification to be fulfilled* (relations come first; the question of how individuals relate is malformed because it presupposes pre-existing individuals). But *these are still mere words, promising to be sure, but which have not undergone any of the tests capable of determining what they commit us to*.

This is one of the chapter's most honest moments. Stengers is showing you, in real time, the difference between *naming a problem* and *solving it*. SMW names the problem with extraordinary clarity. SMW does not yet have the conceptual apparatus that will later, in PR, articulate what these terms commit us to. The reader who skips to PR loses what is happening here, which is the *naming* of the problem in a form that will require PR's machinery to satisfy.

#### Self-knowledge of the bodily event

Whitehead is clear about his own method, in the SMW 73 passage: *I have started from our own psychological field, as it stands for our cognition. I take it for what it claims to be: the self-knowledge of our bodily event. I mean the total event, and not the inspection of the details of the body. This self-knowledge discloses a prehensive unification of modal presences of entities beyond itself. I generalise by the use of the principle that this total bodily event is on the same level as all other events, except for an unusual complexity and stability of inherent pattern.*

Two methodological moves in this passage. First, the starting point is *the psychological field* — the field of immediate experience as we have it. Second, the generalisation is by the *all-on-the-same-level* principle. The total bodily event — what the human person *is* as a prehensive occasion — is *on the same level as* all other events. Other events differ in *complexity and stability of inherent pattern*, but not in *kind of being*. The electron and the human are the same kind of thing, with the human being an extraordinarily complex and stable instance of what the electron is more austerely.

The *psychological field* phrase deserves attention. Stengers tells us it refers, *fairly transparently*, to William James's analyses, particularly to James's article *Does 'Consciousness' Exist?* — *to whose ambition Whitehead subscribes*. James closes the philosophical epoch inaugurated by Descartes; Whitehead picks up where James left off.

James's claim was that *consciousness does not exist as a fictitious unit*; that *thoughts are fully real and made of the same "stuff" as things*; that consciousness *stands for a function* that thoughts *perform*, called *knowing*. Whitehead inherits all of this. *Self-knowledge of our bodily event* is the function-of-knowing performed by certain prehensive events of unusual complexity. There is no *substance* called *consciousness* doing the knowing; there is the prehensive event of the bodily-knowing-itself, and *consciousness* is the name for what that prehensive event does.

The *cautionary note*, Stengers writes, *is already struck*: consciousness is not to be reduced to an *epiphenomenon*. The reductive program that explains consciousness by neuronal firing is wrong, but it is wrong not because consciousness has some special privileged status, but because the *whole picture in which neurons are described independently of consciousness is itself a bifurcation*. There is no separable *body as object of scientific definition* on one side and *consciousness as inner life* on the other. There is the *total bodily event*, prehensively self-knowing in its unusual complexity. The neurons, properly described, are aspects of that total event, and the description of the neurons that takes them as objects-independent-of-experience has already lost what made them aspects of an experience.

#### Whitehead as coming after James

Stengers then makes a crucial historical-philosophical claim. *In Process and Reality, Whitehead will implicitly present himself as the one who "comes after William James." Implicitly, because it is precisely insofar as Science and the Modern World had turned William James into the inaugurator of a new philosophical epoch* that Whitehead's later remark about the rhythm of philosophical history takes its full meaning.

*Every philosophical school in the course of its history requires two presiding philosophers* — one who initiates the school's questions and one who satisfies them by constructing the conceptual apparatus they require. Plato and Aristotle. Descartes and (in some readings) Spinoza. James and Whitehead. The first articulates the questions but lacks the conceptual machinery to answer them; the second inherits the questions and builds the machinery. Whitehead is positioning himself as the *second* philosopher relative to James — the one who comes after, who supplies what James's questions require.

This is also why Stengers says *Whitehead's writing reduces this supple and vulnerable thought to a rigid coherence*. James's pragmatism could be misread as the businessman's truth-is-utility because James's prose is *supple and vulnerable* — easy to take apart and simplify. Whitehead's prose is *rigid coherence* — every concept depends on every other; the apparatus cannot be partially extracted; the writing *actively produces a strange inseparability between the experience demanded by reading and the experience proposed by concepts*.

The implication for reading practice: PR is hard *because* it has to be hard. The looseness that made James readable also made James reducible. The tightness that makes Whitehead unreadable is what makes Whitehead un-reducible. The hardness is the apparatus's protection against the simplifying misreading James suffered.

#### Continuity with Concept of Nature

Stengers then makes a careful note about where Whitehead is in SMW relative to where he will be in PR. *At this stage, however, the division in question is far from having been actually surmounted. Whitehead is announcing an ambition that he still lacks the means to satisfy, for the problem as he frames it is situated in the strict prolongation of his position in The Concept of Nature.*

This is honest. SMW is the *transition document*. It announces the metaphysical ambition and starts to articulate the technical vocabulary, but it does not yet have the apparatus that will satisfy the ambition. The work to come — primarily in PR — is the construction of the apparatus.

Whitehead is *not unaware of this*. He takes care to *specify the continuity of his thought*, to articulate the new notion of prehension with the old notion of an object making ingression. The SMW 70-71 quote: *I will say that a sense-object has ingression into space-time. The cognitive perception of a sense-object is the awareness of the prehensive unification (into a standpoint A, including the sense-object in question) of various modes of various sense-objects [...] I am merely describing what we do perceive: we are aware of green as being one element in a prehensive unification of sense-objects; each sense-object, and among them green, having its particular mode, which is expressible as location elsewhere.*

So far, Stengers tells us, Whitehead has *limited himself to repeating what we already knew*: ingression is a relation that has various modes. *But he has done so in such a way that observation is no longer connected with experience, but with "reality," or the process of realization.* The multiplicity of modes of ingression *will have to be understood on the basis of the new questions of realization and individualization*. We no longer have *experience of an event*; our sensible experience *is a typical case of an event*.

This last formulation is the crucial inversion. CN's apparatus had perception as the access to events. SMW's apparatus has perception as one event among others. The shift is from *perception-of-event* to *perception-as-event*. Once perception is itself an event, the asymmetry between perceiver and perceived dissolves, and the relation between them becomes a relation between two events whose prehensive unification is one act of realization.

#### The Romantic poets enter

The chapter then makes a turn that might be the most surprising in SMW. The seventeenth-century thinkers (Berkeley, Locke, Leibniz, Spinoza) are not enough to satisfy the job specifications. *Their testimony does not suffice, for it is liable to treat a castle, a cloud, and the face of a loved one indifferently. It does not do justice to what I have already introduced as the question of "presence."*

Whitehead now turns to *the Romantic poets of the early British nineteenth century*. The phrase *those who reacted to scientific abstractions by celebrating a nature to which these abstractions fail to do justice* is Stengers's framing.

Wordsworth's accusation — *we murder to dissect* — is the entry point. Whitehead chooses to understand it *not as a testimony to the violence of the assassin, or to the pain of the victim, but to the inadequacy of the "materialist" scientific approach, as well as of the empiricist approach that was contemporary with it*. The poet's protest is testimony, not partisan argument.

Then Stengers's most important methodological line in this section: *The philosophy of the organism is first and foremost an epistemology.* This is worth pausing over. Most readers come to Whitehead expecting metaphysics. Stengers is telling you that *the philosophy of the organism, as articulated in SMW, is first an epistemology* — a critique of what science's abstractions can and cannot do, with the metaphysical apparatus emerging from the epistemological work. The *order of nature* is not a metaphysical thesis; it is the requirement that any epistemology adequate to nature must satisfy. The poets are witnesses to what current epistemology fails at.

The SMW 83 quote: *It is important to ask, what Wordsworth found in nature that failed to receive expression in science. I ask this question in the interest of science itself; for one main position in these lectures is a protest against the idea that the abstractions of science are irreformable and unalterable.*

The point is not anti-scientific; it is *for* science. The poets are witnesses to what science's current abstractions miss, and the philosophy of the organism is the discipline of letting the witnesses' testimony force science to *reform its abstractions* rather than pretending the witnesses are sentimentalists.

What did Wordsworth find? Not a faith that some life-element escapes science. Rather, *the brooding presence of the hills which haunts him. His theme is nature in solido, that is to say, he dwells on that mysterious presence of surrounding things, which imposes itself on any separate element that we set up as an individual for its own sake.*

#### Presence — and the pivot from Cleopatra's needle

Stengers's gloss is precise, and connects back to the earlier material: *When I go for a walk, it is no longer a matter of saying of a hill, "Say, there it is again," as was the case with Cleopatra's Needle. The hill, brooding and ancient, has a weight; it is a "presence" and not simply "there" for me, who perceives it "here." The experience of its presence imposes itself in such a way that I belong to it much more than it belongs to me: this is what a science focused on separability cannot express.*

This is the move from chapter 5 of *Concept of Nature* finally extended. The bird's-world / my-world overlap from chapter 5 is now amplified. The hill's *presence* is not a property of the hill that I observe; it is a *belonging-to-the-hill* that I undergo. The asymmetry of perceiver-and-perceived is being undone in the direction the previous apparatus could only gesture at. The hill *has me* in its world more than I *have it* in mine.

This is the seed of what PR will articulate as *the percipient event's prehension of its world*, where the percipient is *constituted by* what it prehends rather than the prehender of pre-existing objects. SMW does not yet have the technical vocabulary to articulate this. But the testimony is registered: the hill's *brooding presence* is a job specification that any adequate apparatus must host.

#### Shelley, change, and eternity

Then Shelley enters as the contrasting witness. Shelley *loved science*; his poetry celebrates *nature in flux, perpetual change*, an *incessant shimmering, an elusive rustling*. The SMW 86 quote: *Shelley thinks of nature as changing, dissolving, transforming as it were at a fairy's touch.*

The contrast between Wordsworth's brooding presence and Shelley's shimmering flux is *not* a difference in the temperaments of two poets facing the same landscape. *This would silence experience in favor of bargain-basement psychology.* The two testify to *two facts*, both of which must appear among the job specifications.

Stengers names them: *one of them testifies to what Whitehead will call "endurance," which includes change, the other to what he will call "eternity."*

The SMW 86-87 quote is the chapter's most important formulation of the contrast. *The mountain endures. But when after ages it has been worn away, it has gone. If a replica arises, it is yet a new mountain. A colour is eternal. It haunts time like a spirit. It comes and it goes. But when it comes, it is the same colour. It neither survives nor does it live. It appears when it is wanted. The mountain has to time and space a different relation from that which colour has.*

This is the apparatus's first articulation of what will become *eternal objects*. The mountain *endures* — it persists as the same mountain through time, but its persistence is finite; when worn away, it is gone, and any successor is a *new* mountain. The colour *does not endure but recurs* — when it appears, it is *the same* colour, and its sameness is independent of any particular instance. Color is eternal in the precise sense that it has *a different relation to time and space* than the mountain has.

This is a major technical move. SMW is now committing to *two distinct kinds of being-in-time*. Mountains, organisms, particles, persons — these are *enduring* entities, with finite continuity and with replicas counting as new instances. Colors, mathematical structures, qualities — these are *eternal*, recurring, with each instance being *the same as* every other instance, with eternity being a real feature rather than a metaphor.

This is exactly the earlier discussion we had about *what Plato was reaching for* and *the unreasonable effectiveness of mathematics*. The eternal objects are real, but their eternity is not Platonic separation; it is a different *mode of being-in-time* than the enduring mode. The mountain's mode of being is enduring; the color's mode of being is eternal. Both are real; both are necessary; the apparatus needs both because experience tests both.

That's a natural break point. The chapter has more material — the introduction of *eternal objects* as the technical term, the relation between events and eternal objects, the move into the actual structure of *the order of nature*, and the closing material on what SMW has and has not accomplished. Want me to continue with the next chunk?

### Chapter 9 — Part Three

#### Eternal in the precise sense

The chapter now articulates what *eternal* means as a technical term in Whitehead's apparatus. Stengers is careful to forestall misreading from the start.

*Whitehead is not mad enough to calmly announce that "red" as we perceive it existed before the biological invention of the visual organs. This is why he speaks of "eternality," a neologism that enables him to avoid "eternity." "Eternality" designates a dimension of the concrete, complete fact that must belong to the job specifications.*

The distinction matters. *Eternity* in the religious and Platonic traditions is a *separate domain* — eternity stands over against time, with eternal beings inhabiting their own ontological tier. *Eternality* in Whitehead's neologism is a *dimension of the concrete fact* — it is *one feature* that the complete fact has, alongside endurance and change. Red does not exist *outside* time as a separate Platonic form; red is what *the experience of red testifies to* as a dimension that has to be honored in any adequate account of what is the case.

This is the same move we worked through in the earlier discussion of *what Plato was reaching for*. The forms are real, but they are not in another world. They are *features of the world* that have *a different mode of being-in-time* than the enduring entities have. The colour is not in heaven; it is *here*, when called, with each instance of it being *the same* in a way that the mountain's instances cannot be.

#### Color as the test case

The contrast between colors and mountains *first calls into question the construction proposed by The Concept of Nature*. CN's apparatus had color as a *sense-object* and mountain as a *perceptual object*, in a hierarchy that made the mountain always *have* a color while we could experience a color independently of what it colors. The poets contributed *a testimony that raises a very different question*, because the poet's experience is not focused on the urgency of having to decide whether that yellowish shadow is or is not a tiger.

*And, in so doing, they also forced the experience Whitehead assimilated under the word "recognition" to diverge: "it's the same color" and "that's my coat" testify to experiences whose difference can no longer be referred to a hierarchy.*

This is a precise technical observation. CN's *recognition* covered both *re-encountering an enduring particular* (that's my coat, the same coat as before) and *experiencing the same color* (that's the same blue). The new apparatus has to register that these two are *different kinds of recognition* — one is *recognition of an enduring entity*, the other is *recognition of an eternal object's recurrence*. The hierarchy CN proposed cannot capture this difference. The job specifications now require the apparatus to honor *two distinct relations to time and space*.

The SMW 86-87 passage Stengers quoted in the previous chunk gets its full work here. *There it is again* was appropriate for Cleopatra's Needle and a birdsong because the contrast was between events that pass and objects liable to be recognized, both required by what we are aware of. The new contrast is different. *What is happening, however, is no longer the event, but what is unification, gathering, in a twofold sense, passive and active. The event not only discloses itself as related to other events: it realizes this relation in and for itself.*

This is the apparatus deepening. The event does not just *occur* and pass; the event *realizes itself* as the gathering of its relations. The event's duration is *that of an individualization, of the "holding together" of the gathering it constitutes*. This is structurally important because it sets up what *value* will mean as a technical term in the next move.

#### Once again, not again

Stengers's phrasing for the color side of the contrast: *As far as color is concerned, it is no longer there "again" but "once again," always the same but always new, for it is not worn out, does not live, does not endure.*

The distinction between *again* and *once again* is doing work. *Again* is the *repetition* of an enduring particular — the same coat seen another time. *Once again* is the *recurrence* of an eternal object — the same color appearing in a fresh ingression, with no diminishment, no wear, no aging. The eternal object's mode of being is *recurrence-without-diminishment*, and its sameness across instances is *not numerical sameness across time* (as the coat's sameness is) but *the eternal object's own identity that ingresses afresh each time*.

*Eternal not because it is always there — it would never be anything but an indefinite endurance — but because experience testifies to color in the sense that it is what it is, without reference to a process within time. Color is eternal in the precise sense that it requires that endurance and change do not define in an exhaustive way what is required in the order of nature.*

This is the precise formulation of what *eternal* means in this apparatus. The eternal is not what is *always there* (that would be just very long endurance). The eternal is what *is what it is independently of any process within time*. The color is fully itself in any single instance; it does not need duration to be what it is; it does not accumulate or diminish; it ingresses fully each time. This is a different mode of being than enduring, and the apparatus needs both modes because experience testifies to both.

#### Ingression, reserved

The technical move that follows is one of the chapter's sharpest. *In order to express this non-emergence, this appearance that does not testify to a process but rather to a call, Whitehead will henceforth reserve the term "ingression."*

*Reserve the term* is the key. *Ingression* in CN was a more general term for how objects make their way into events. SMW *narrows* the term: ingression is now reserved for *the eternal object's mode of entering an event*. The mountain does not *ingress* into events; it *endures*. Only eternal objects ingress.

This is a vocabulary discipline that lets the apparatus do precise work. Two distinct modes of being-in-events: *endurance* for enduring entities, *ingression* for eternal objects. The two modes are different and irreducible. The mountain's relation to time and space is one mode; the colour's relation is another. The apparatus tracks the difference by reserving distinct vocabulary for it.

The methodological consequence Stengers names: *Red does not emerge from the order to which the eye and the brain testify, but rather the eye and the brain must be understood on the basis of the possibility of the ingression to which the sensation "red" testifies. The fact of their existence testifies "for" the eternal object felt as red.*

The order of explanation is inverted. The standard reductive move says: the eye and brain produce the sensation of red, so red is *explained by* the neural mechanisms. Whitehead's move says: the existence of the eye and brain *testifies for* the possibility of red's ingression. The eye and brain are the way nature has organized itself such that red can ingress into experience. *Red* is not what the eye-and-brain produces; the eye-and-brain is what nature produces in order to make red's ingression possible.

This is the chapter's clearest statement of what *self-explanatory nature* commits to. Nature explains itself by being the kind of thing that *has both* enduring entities and eternal objects, with the enduring entities being structured precisely so that eternal objects can ingress through them. Neither side is more fundamental. The eye exists because red can ingress through it; red can ingress through the eye because the eye is the kind of structure red can ingress through. The reciprocal-presupposition shape returns at this new level.

#### Sensible and intelligible together

Stengers makes a careful clarification. *Both sensible objects and intelligible objects are "called," or gathered together, by the notions of eternal object and ingression.*

This is important. *Eternal object* is not just *qualia* (red, blue, the felt quality of sound). It also covers *intelligible objects* — geometrical forms, mathematical structures, propositional forms. The same apparatus that handles the felt quality of red handles the geometric form of the circle. The circle clumsily drawn in the sand is wearing away in the wind, but *the "circle" that qualified my experience of the contour will not disappear with the wind*. The geometric form is eternal in the same precise sense that color is eternal.

This is doing what the earlier discussion in our conversation about *Plato* and *mathematical structures ingressing into development* needs. The same eternal objects vocabulary covers qualia and mathematical structures because both have the same mode of being-in-events: recurrence-without-diminishment, fully present in any single instance, ingressive through the events that occasion them. This is a unified treatment, not a separate theory of qualia plus a separate theory of mathematics.

#### A trap to avoid

Stengers then names a Deleuzian trap and shows how Whitehead avoids it. *Whoever considers that Whitehead is proposing eternal objects as a solution to the problem raised by geometrical forms and colors will rightly conclude that he has fallen into a trap identified by Gilles Deleuze: that of the "decalque" (tracing), or the concept "made to explain," in this case made to found the contrast, to which experience testifies, between color and mountain.*

*Decalque* — tracing — is the move where you take experience as a given and then construct a concept that *traces* the structure of experience and calls itself the explanation of that structure. The concept is post hoc; it does not do explanatory work; it just renames what experience already showed in different vocabulary.

If Whitehead were proposing eternal objects as the *explanation* of why colors are different from mountains, he would be tracing — using *eternal* as a label that explains nothing and merely renames the contrast.

Stengers's reading is that Whitehead is *not* doing this. *And it is criticism that Whitehead would be the first to accept, since it would mean confusing job specification and concept.* The eternal object is a *job specification* at this stage in SMW, not a concept. Experience exhibits the contrast between color and mountain. The job specification is: *any adequate apparatus must register this contrast as a real feature of nature*. The concept that satisfies this specification — what *eternal object* will eventually mean technically — has not yet been built. The chapter is gathering specifications; PR will do the conceptual work.

This distinction between *job specification* and *concept* is one of the most useful single distinctions in the chapter for reading discipline. When you encounter Whitehead using a term in SMW, ask: is this term operating as a *specification* (a constraint on what the apparatus must satisfy) or as a *concept* (a worked-out technical term)? Most of SMW's vocabulary is operating at the specification level. PR will be where the concepts get constructed. Reading SMW as if its vocabulary were already concept-grade produces the *delirium* of the first reading we discussed in chapter 7.

#### Value enters

The chapter then makes its most consequential constructive move. The SMW 93-94 passage Stengers quotes is the introduction of *value* as a technical term:

*The endurance of things has its significance in the self-retention of that which imposes itself as a definite attainment for its own sake. That which endures is limited, obstructive, intolerant, infecting its environment with its own aspects. But it is not self-sufficient. The aspects of all things enter into its very nature. It is only itself as drawing together into its own limitation the larger whole in which it finds itself.*

Stengers's gloss is a structural turning point in the chapter: *We are at a crucial point here, and the tone has changed. For value is what is required by the divergence between the experiences of Berkeley, Spinoza, Leibniz, Shelley, or Wordsworth. Value is required by an author capable of using the same term, whether in reference to an electron, a living organism, or an industrial firm. The organism has now come into contact with its requisites.*

*Value* in Whitehead's apparatus is the *technical term* that brings together what the witnesses have been testifying to. The value of an enduring entity is *its self-retention as a definite attainment for its own sake*. To endure is to *succeed at being-itself in a way that is intolerant of dissolution*. The endurance is *the value*; the value is the *success*; the success is what the apparatus needs to honor about every enduring entity from electron to industrial firm.

Stengers immediately blocks the misreading. *There is not the slightest allusion in Whitehead's text to subjects who would "have values" in terms of which they would evaluate, or to which they would conform.* Value here is *not* what subjects assign or affirm. It is *not* a normative property. *Value belongs to the order of nature: it is what is realized by all that exists, in the sense that what exists succeeds in enduring, succeeds in maintaining its individual way of gathering together, that is, of making things hold together in a determinate way. Value indicates a success in and for itself.*

This is one of the more consequential moves in Whitehead's apparatus and one of the moves most often misread. *Value* in the technical sense is *what every endurance accomplishes by enduring*. The electron's continued being-an-electron is the electron's *value*. The mountain's continued being-this-mountain is the mountain's *value*. The bird's continued being-this-bird is the bird's *value*. None of these requires a valuing subject; the value is *constituted by the success of the endurance*, and the success is *for itself* in the technical sense — the entity's own success at being itself.

#### The economy is inverted

Stengers names what this commits to. *The habitual economy of scientific explanations is turned upside down, as is that of subjective motivations. What is maintained without change is no longer the ensured starting point for understanding what changes.*

This is sharp. The standard scientific economy treats stable identity as the *given* (the electron is what it is, the law of gravity is what it is) and treats change as *what needs to be explained*. Whitehead inverts this. Stable identity is *the achievement* — it is what has been *successful* against the background of activity that could have gone otherwise. Change is the default; persistence is the *valuable success*.

Stengers's example: *Don't ask me why I am distracted: on the contrary, be amazed that I am able to complete my phrase safe and sound. Above all, do not consider that it is "I" who am responsible for this exploit. "My" success, like that of an atom, has no justification higher than this fact: the experience of this phrase to be completed has succeeded in holding out in an environment that might have had a very different role: "Wait, you've distracted me: what was I going to say?"*

The completed phrase is the *success* against an environment that *might have* prevented it. The success is not *mine* in the sense that there is a pre-existing *I* who completed the phrase; the success is *the phrase's completing of itself*, which is *my self-completing-this-phrase* in the technical Whiteheadian sense where the subject is constituted by the achievement. Distraction is not the failure of an executive function; the completed phrase is the achievement of a particular kind of holding-together-against-environment.

#### Infection

Then the apparatus's most surprising piece of vocabulary. *The explanation of endurance is not an attribute of the individual who endures, but depends first of all on a dynamics of infection. All that succeeds in enduring has succeeded in infecting its environment in a way that is compatible with this endurance.*

*Infection* is the term Whitehead chooses to designate, *in a generic way*, what the poets celebrated as *presence*. The hill's brooding presence in Wordsworth's experience — the way *I belong to the hill* more than the hill belongs to me — is, in technical terms, the hill's *infection* of its environment. The hill *infects* the surroundings with its aspects, including the perceiver who walks among them. The hill's endurance depends on this infection: the hill remains the hill because it has succeeded in shaping its environment such that the environment continues to support hill-ness.

*Infection must be understood, not without humor, in a neutral sense, designating the success constituted by all endurance in a changing world. The interest of this chosen term is that it designates the specific character of this success.*

The dryness of *infection* is methodologically important. The term is borrowed from biology and disease but is being used neutrally. Every enduring entity *infects* its environment with its aspects. The mountain infects the landscape. The atom infects the field. The factory infects the social and economic surroundings. The poet's experience is *infected* by the hill. The infection metaphor has the advantage of being *active* — endurance is something the entity *does* to its environment, not just a passive persistence.

*This infectious holding-together is not a fusion but a valorization, a determinate shaping, conferring a value — that is, a role — on what is prehended.* This is the technical key. To infect is to *valorize* — to confer a *role* on what is prehended. The hill confers a role on the perceiver (you who walks among me); the perceiver confers a role on the hill (you who haunts my walking). Each is valorized by the other; each takes on the value/role that the other's prehension of it confers.

This is also where the variable-and-its-value passage lands: *The fact that the variables of a function, in the same way as the poet's experience, require a "value" thus ceases to belong to linguistic contingency. Far from being a mere quantity, the value of a variable presupposes the stability of the role that one thing plays for something else and measures the importance of that role.*

The mathematical *value* of a variable and the experiential *value* of an entity are *the same* technical concept. In both cases, *value* names *the role one thing plays for something else, with the stability of that role being what makes value definable*. The mathematician's value-of-a-function and the metaphysician's value-of-an-entity are the same notion at different scales. This is a deep structural unification, and it is one of the apparatus's payoffs.

#### The dynamics of mutual infection

Stengers then articulates the structure of how infection operates as a *dynamics*. *Every entity that realizes itself in its mode of prehension of other beings also prehends the way in which it itself is prehended by those others, or the aspect of itself that is taken into account and valorized by these others.*

This is the apparatus's first articulation of *the reciprocity of prehension*. Every entity prehends others, *and* prehends *its own being-prehended* by those others. The mountain prehends the perceiver who is now walking among it; the mountain also prehends *the perceiver's prehending of the mountain* — the way the mountain figures in the perceiver's experience. This second-order prehension is part of what the mountain takes into account.

*How will it prehend the prehension of itself that it reflected back to it? Such are the stakes of the "dynamics of infection," upon which the success of endurance depends. Infection designates the way in which the modes of prehension are reflected for each other, and success implies a co-adaptation of values.*

This is what *dynamics of infection* means precisely. Two or more entities are prehending each other and prehending each other's prehensions of each other. Each is valorizing the others; each is being valorized by the others; each is taking-into-account how it is being valorized; each is adjusting (or not) its own mode of prehension in light of the valorization received. *Success* — endurance — requires a *co-adaptation* of values such that the mutual valorizations are compatible with each entity's continued being-itself.

This is a remarkable apparatus. It explains endurance not by appeal to substantial properties or laws of preservation but by the *dynamics of mutual valorization*. An entity endures because the values it confers and the values it receives are co-adapted; if the co-adaptation breaks, the endurance breaks; if the co-adaptation holds, the endurance holds. The environment is *the totality of beings taken into account and valorized*, and the entity is *the determinate way of taking-into-account and being-taken-into-account* that succeeds in its environment.

#### What the apparatus selects

Stengers names the consequence. *This time, the question of the organism is indeed raised, and it selects what will count as the concrete, complete facts.*

*Farewell to Cleopatra's Needle, and even to the gloomy hill: for neither the perceptual object, nor weighty presence, enable the complete unfolding of the dynamics of repercussion and infection.* The CN-era examples are not the favorable cases for this apparatus. Cleopatra's Needle is not a good case because it does not *interact* in the strong sense; it is recognized but does not adapt to its perceiver. The hill is closer but still not enough; the brooding presence is one-directional infection of the perceiver, but the perceiver does not change the hill.

*Henceforth, the favorable cases will be those in which the "interaction" must be called an interaction, that is, the shaping of an attainment that exhibits its dependency by the way in which each of the interacting terms valorizes the other.*

Stengers's example: *the complex sexual parade in which a male and a female, from posture to posture, from approach to approach, gradually co-produce one another, as each one confers upon the other the role and the value corresponding to mating.*

This is the new paradigm case. Co-production through mutual valorization. Each posture by one valorizes the other; each response by the other valorizes the first. The mating display is not the application of pre-existing roles; it is *the determination of the roles* through the dynamics of mutual prehension. Both partners *become* what they are *by being prehended* in particular ways by each other.

#### The infernal dynamics of dependency

Stengers extends the apparatus to a darker case. *Or again, the set of cases in which the role assigned to a being becomes an integral part of its individualization. For better or worse, as, for instance, in the case of the infernal dynamics of relations of dependency — when the gaze you cast upon me becomes an ingredient of my experience of myself, to the point of producing myself in the way you need, or at least in the way I sense you need, and so on.*

This is the apparatus illuminating something that was obvious phenomenologically but had no clear vocabulary in earlier philosophy. *Toxic mutual dependency* is, in this technical reading, an *enduring co-production through pathological mutual valorization*. Both partners are individualizing themselves through being prehended by the other; both are conferring roles on the other that the other internalizes as parts of self; both have become what they are *only in this specific co-prehensive dynamic*. The result is *a remarkably enduring production of two beings whose respective modes of valorization call obstinately upon one another*.

The diagnostic shape is precise. Toxic dependency is *not* the failure of two autonomous individuals to maintain their autonomy in the face of the other. It is *the co-production of two individuals who endure precisely through their mutual call upon each other*. The co-production is real; the endurance is real; the dependency is the *structure of how this particular kind of endurance succeeds*.

The methodological note Stengers adds: *The fact that the dynamics of infection can find privileged examples in relations of dependency obviously does not mean that such relations become the truth of what makes human beings hold together.* These are *part of the way things may happen, of the adventures of individualization*. The human child needs the human environment to become a child; the child *dares* to become because she feels trusted; her clumsy efforts are approved and encouraged. *Such facts do not require any particular psychological explanation, in the sense that its goal would be to justify a departure from a norm of autonomy; for the idea of autonomy, for Whitehead, is obsolete.*

This is one of the chapter's most consequential moves and worth lingering on. *The idea of autonomy, for Whitehead, is obsolete.* Not wrong. Not refuted. *Obsolete* — superseded by an apparatus in which what was protected by *autonomy* (the integrity of individual being) is better honored by *valorization-and-infection-dynamics* (the structure of how individuals constitute themselves through mutual prehension).

The human becomes a child in a human environment by being prehended-as-trustworthy and by prehending-the-trust. The autonomy framework treats this as a temporary deviation from a norm of self-standing-individuality that the adult will eventually reach. The Whiteheadian framework treats this as *what individualization always is*, with the adult as another instance of the same dynamic, no less dependent on environmental valorization, just operating in a different ecology of valorizations.

That's a natural break point. The chapter still has substantial material — the closing on the SMW apparatus's accomplishments and limits, the transition to chapter 10, the Romantic poets' contribution as *job specification* fully articulated. Want me to continue with the final chunk?

## User

So why is Whitehead able to coin terms that seem to be fairly precise but I'm not familiar with many other people doing things like this

## Claude

This is a real observation and worth taking seriously, because it identifies something distinctive about Whitehead and points at why his apparatus is unusually well-built for the kind of work you're doing.

A few things make Whitehead's coinages work where most thinkers' coinages either don't take or don't carry the weight he asks them to.

The first is that *most of his coinages aren't really coinages*. They're *torquings*. We talked earlier about Whitehead reusing existing English words — *object*, *event*, *feeling*, *society* — under technical load rather than inventing new vocabulary. The terms that look most like coinages are usually *narrowings* or *shifts* of existing words. *Prehension* drops the *ap-* from *apprehension*; *concrescence* revives a Latin term that was lying around in technical English; *ingression* generalizes a word that was already in scholastic vocabulary; *valorization* and *infection* are existing words pressed into precise technical service. *Eternal object* is the most striking case — both *eternal* and *object* are common words, and the work is being done by their unusual combination plus the explicit narrowing of *eternality* against *eternity* (the actual coinage, but a small one).

This is part of why the coinages stick. They have *some* of their ordinary meaning ringing in the background, providing intuitive entry, while the technical narrowing does the precise work. A reader can feel something of what *infection* means before having grasped what infection means *in this apparatus*, and the partial intuitive grasp is the foothold for the technical understanding. Pure neologisms — Heidegger's *Dasein*, Peirce's *interpretant*, Deleuze's *deterritorialization* — have to do all the work themselves with no intuitive entry. They are harder to learn and easier to misuse.

The second is that *the coinages are generated by the problem*, not chosen for stylistic distinctiveness. We talked about this in the Stengers vocabulary — *requisite*, *job specification*, *prohibits and incites* — but it applies equally to Whitehead. *Prehension* is the term he reaches for *because* the apparatus has determined that there has to be a generic term for taking-into-account that is broader than apprehension and narrower than relation. The term is built to satisfy a specific job specification within a specific apparatus. It is not a flourish; it is the answer to a precise question the apparatus has been forced to ask.

You can feel this when you read him. Each coinage *does work* that nothing else in his vocabulary could have done. *Concrescence* covers the becoming-determinate of an actual occasion; *prehension* covers the taking-into-account that constitutes the occasion's relations to its data; *eternal object* covers the forms-of-definiteness that ingress; *subjective form* covers the *how* of integration; *satisfaction* covers the achieved unity at the close of concrescence; *superject* covers the occasion as it functions as datum for subsequent occasions. Each term is positioned in a precise relation to all the others. Removing any one term would leave a hole the others cannot fill. This kind of architectural completeness is rare. Most coinages in philosophy are spot fixes — a term invented to handle one local problem — and the term floats unrelated to the rest of the vocabulary.

The third is that *Whitehead was working with the discipline of a mathematician*. We talked about this earlier when you asked why the *types of object / objects* shift was philosophically loaded. He is unusually willing to torque vocabulary because his training in algebra and logic taught him what it means to specify a term *operationally* — to give it not a definition but a *role in a system of operations*. *Prehension* is defined less by a description of what prehensions are than by the *role prehensions play in the apparatus*: they are what unifies an actual occasion's data, what constitutes its becoming, what carries subjective form. The term is operationally specified the way a mathematician specifies a function by what it does, not by giving it a name and a paraphrase.

Most philosophers who try to coin terms specify them by paraphrase — *let X mean Y*, where Y is some existing combination of ordinary words. The coinage then collapses back into the ordinary words; the technical term does no work the paraphrase did not already do, and the term feels redundant or pretentious. Whitehead's terms are operationally specified, which means they cannot be paraphrased without losing what they do. *Prehension* cannot be replaced by *taking-into-account* because the role *prehension* plays in the apparatus exceeds what *taking-into-account* picks up in ordinary use. The technical term and the apparatus are *mutually constitutive* — neither can be paraphrased away without losing the other.

The fourth is that *he was working in a tradition that had stopped doing this and had thereby gone barren*. We talked about the railway-engineer figure of philosophy — Whitehead's claim that the *failure* of modern philosophy was its loss of the practice of inventing new abstractions. By his own time, the dominant traditions had largely abandoned the discipline. The British analytic tradition was committed to *clarification* (taking existing concepts and making them precise) rather than *invention*. The continental tradition was committed to *recovery* (going back to phenomenological grounds or pre-Socratic origins) rather than construction. The pragmatists, especially James, were doing something close to what Whitehead was doing but at a less systematic level.

This left a gap in the philosophical labor market. The work of *building new abstractions for the locomotion of ideas* was not being done. Whitehead stepped into the gap and did it. He could be precise because nobody else was being precise about these problems; the conceptual ground he was crossing had not been claimed by anyone else's apparatus, so he had room to specify his terms operationally without their colliding with established usage.

The fifth — and this is more speculative — is that *he was working at scale most thinkers don't attempt*. PR is a six-hundred-page systematic metaphysics with dozens of interlocking technical terms. The size matters. A vocabulary of three or four coinages is hard to get right because each term has to do a lot of work and must remain partially anchored in ordinary use. A vocabulary of forty interlocking terms can be more precise per term because the *system* carries weight that no individual term has to carry alone. Each term is constrained by its position in the apparatus. The constraints from neighboring terms force precision. *Subjective form* could not mean any number of things because it has to coordinate with *prehension*, *datum*, *subject*, *concrescence*, *satisfaction* in specific ways. The neighborhood disciplines the term.

This is why isolated coinages by other thinkers often feel less precise. *Différance* in Derrida is an island; nothing else in the apparatus has been built to constrain what it means. *Dasein* in Heidegger is more constrained because the rest of *Being and Time* is built around it, but the constraints are more atmospheric than operational. Whitehead's *prehension* is operational because the apparatus around it specifies what it must do at every step.

A few comparisons might sharpen this. The thinkers who have actually managed Whitehead-grade systematic coinage are a small list:

*Aristotle* coined or repurposed dozens of terms across his corpus — *energeia*, *entelecheia*, *ousia*, *hypokeimenon*, *to ti en einai*, *eudaimonia*, *phronesis*, *theoria*, *praxis*, *poiesis*. Each is operationally specified by its role in his system. The terms have lasted two and a half thousand years because they are doing precise work that cannot be paraphrased.

*Kant* coined or torqued an enormous vocabulary — *transcendental*, *categorical imperative*, *thing-in-itself*, *synthetic a priori*, *schematism*, *apperception*, *noumenon* and *phenomenon* under his specifications. Like Whitehead, he was working at system-scale, and the terms constrain each other.

*Hegel* coined the *Aufhebung* family and a host of terms (*Begriff*, *Geist*, the dialectical operations) that work within his system. Less operationally specified than Whitehead's; more dependent on the dialectical method to give them content.

*Peirce* did this — we talked about him — *interpretant*, *abduction*, *firstness/secondness/thirdness*, *semiosis*, *phaneron*, *synechism*, *tychism*, *agapism*. Many of these are crisp; some are still being clarified by his readers. His scattered corpus made the operational specification harder than Whitehead's systematic one.

*Husserl* did this, with *epoché*, *noesis*, *noema*, *Lebenswelt*, *intentionality* in his technical sense, and so on. The vocabulary is precise but the apparatus is closer to method than to system.

*Heidegger*, we discussed.

*Deleuze* did this at scale, especially in *Difference and Repetition* and *A Thousand Plateaus* — *plane of immanence*, *deterritorialization*, *body without organs*, *rhizome*, *assemblage*, *line of flight*, the *virtual* and *actual*. The vocabulary is rich but more poetic than Whitehead's; the operational specification is looser, the atmospheric specification is heavier.

After this list, the field thins. Most twentieth-century philosophers either declined the coinage discipline or did it at much smaller scale. Wittgenstein famously said he was trying to *clarify* rather than *theorize*; his innovations are methodological rather than vocabulary-based. Quine introduced terms but mostly as adjustments to existing analytic vocabulary. Davidson, Searle, Dennett, Brandom — each has a few coinages, but none has built a vocabulary at Whitehead's scale.

So part of the answer to your question is that *what Whitehead did was not standard practice in modern philosophy*. He was reaching back to a much older mode of philosophical work — the speculative-systematic mode — that had been dormant for most of a century when he undertook PR. The mathematician's discipline of operational specification, applied to metaphysics, is genuinely uncommon. Most philosophers either do not have the mathematical training to pull it off or have the training but not the philosophical ambition.

There's a second part of the answer that's worth saying separately. The coinage discipline *requires a specific kind of relationship between the philosopher and the tradition*. Whitehead was unusually free to invent because he had absorbed the tradition without being captured by it. He had spent decades on *Principia Mathematica* with Russell, taught mathematics and physics for forty years, lived through the relativity revolution, read widely in Greek philosophy and the British empiricists. This breadth gave him *resources* — terms he could draw on, traditions he could honor, problems he could inherit. But it also gave him *distance* — none of the existing traditions had a complete claim on him. He could borrow from Locke without becoming a Lockean, draw on Plato without becoming a Platonist, take from Spinoza without becoming a Spinozist.

The coinage discipline requires this combination. You need to know enough of the tradition to know what each existing term carries, what it is doing, what it cannot do. And you need to be free enough of the tradition to *modify* the term when modification is what the apparatus demands. The thinker who is too captured by the tradition cannot torque its vocabulary; the thinker who is too distant from the tradition does not know what is at stake in the words she chooses. The narrow band of thinkers who have both the training and the freedom is small, and Whitehead was unusually well-positioned in it.

There is a contemporary point to make, which connects to your repo's project. The coinage discipline is *available* — it is not technically out of reach for current work. What makes it rare now is partly the disappearance of the speculative-systematic mode (which has been recovering somewhat in process philosophy, in some strands of speculative realism, in Stengers herself) and partly the institutional pressures of contemporary academic philosophy, which rewards local clarity over systematic invention. The current institutional structure does not produce many Whiteheads.

But it also doesn't *prevent* the work from being done outside institutional structure. Stengers herself is a working example of someone who has built a small but real vocabulary — *requisite*, *cosmopolitics*, *ecology of practices*, *worlds in unison*, *idiotes* in her technical use. Her vocabulary is operational in Whitehead's sense; her terms are constrained by their roles in her apparatus; the apparatus at scale provides the discipline that makes individual terms precise.

Your repo is doing some version of this work, at small scale, in your own project. *Status: open* is a coinage in the operational sense — the term means *what it is to leave a question productively unresolved within this particular apparatus*, and it cannot be paraphrased without losing what it does in your discipline. *Atelier work* names a stage in your apparatus that the standard vocabulary of *research* or *writing* does not pick out. *Bildung 2.0* is a torquing of *Bildung* into a contemporary technical sense. None of these is at Whitehead-scale, but they are the same kind of work. The reason they feel right to you in his vicinity is that the *practice* is the same practice, scaled down to a single working repository.

So the short answer to your question is: Whitehead is rare not because what he did was technically extraordinary but because the conditions for doing it — mathematical training, breadth of tradition, freedom from any single tradition's capture, willingness to work at speculative-systematic scale, institutional position permitting decade-long projects — happen to converge in him as they did in Aristotle, Kant, Peirce, and very few others. The work is available; the conditions for doing it well are rare. Reading him gives you the model for what the work looks like when conditions are right, which is part of what makes him useful even if your own work is operating at much smaller scale.

## User

So it's not clear to me why having the discipline of a mathematician would not be a prerequisite for doing any sort of this work like table stakes

## Claude

You're putting your finger on something genuinely puzzling, and I want to take the puzzlement seriously rather than reaching for a soft answer. The puzzle, restated: if operational specification is what makes systematic philosophical work possible, and operational specification is what mathematical training cultivates, then mathematical training should be table stakes for doing this kind of philosophy. The fact that it isn't — that most philosophy is done without it, often badly — looks less like a contingent feature of the discipline and more like a structural failure.

I think you're largely right, with two refinements that matter.

The first refinement is that what you're calling *the discipline of a mathematician* is not actually unique to mathematicians. It is the discipline of *specifying terms by their role in a system of operations rather than by paraphrase or pointing*. Mathematicians cultivate this discipline because mathematics is the domain where the discipline is most exposed — if you don't specify operationally, the mathematics doesn't work, and the failure is immediately visible. But the discipline itself is portable, and there are cognate domains where it can be cultivated equally well.

Theoretical physicists have it. Engineers of complex systems have it, in their domain. Composers working in serialism or other rigorously formal idioms have it. Lawyers working at the highest level of constitutional or contract drafting have it — the discipline of specifying terms such that their meaning is fixed by their position in a structured set of obligations and operations is exactly what good legal drafting does. Programmers working on type systems, compilers, or formal verification have it. The discipline is *operational specification*, and mathematical training is one of several routes into it.
